# Index for Xianhua Wang – The Metamorphosis of Enlil in Early Mesopotamia.pdf

To my families






Acknowledgements
The monograph is a dissertation submitted to the Faculty of Oriental Studies of the University of Cambridge for the degree of Doctor of Philosophy in Assyriology in the summer of 2007, defended in the summer of 2008, and degree conferred in the spring of 2009. I am glad that, working for this dissertation, I somehow found a way out of all the mists. But I have also to be painfully aware that problems remaining with the early history of Enlil still disproportionally outnumber questions for which I could have ventured any answers. It is for this reason I decided to present it to the academic world as it was finalized some two years ago, though technical revisions are made for publication. I will benefit more if it is seen as it was by more scholars experienced in the field. The dissertation could not have reached its present form without Professor John Nicholas Postgate, my supervisor’s keen insights, warm encouragements, and meticulous corrections of my often messy draft. I would like to say first that I am indeed grateful to have studied ancient Mesopotamia with Professor Postgate who guided me into a different but wonderful world. Without the numerous forms and letters he signed in my support, it would be also unimaginable to come through my years in Cambridge. Dr Augusta McMahon and Dr Eleanor Robson had offered me much needed helps in Cambridge both for academic and other matters. Professor Dr Manfred Krebernik of Friedrich-Schiller-Universität Jena was the great host and academic advisor I had the privilege to have as a visiting researcher in Jena with a DAAD Stipendium, in 2005. The hospitality of Professor Dr Markus Hilgert in Jena, now in Heidelberg, is heartedly appreciated. Professor Dr Walter Sommerfeld, Professor Dr Walther Sallaberger, and Dr Joachim Marzahn were all most helpful when



ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS
viii
I visited their institutions in Germany in the same year. The friendship of Emre and Christina meant to me much more than just studying together in Cambridge. The friendship of Mr Mah Seakhit, Mr Man Chongyu, Dr Signe Gunderson was also a significant part of my time there. The Rentons, Dr James Renton, Monica, and Clementito, are my family in London. James my friend also proof read my semi-final draft, helping much with the English. The Schmidts, Dr Alexander Schmidt, Dr Beate Schmidt, Karl, and Johannes were my family in Jena. I came to Cambridge for Mesopotamian studies after having studied History and theological Philosophy in Beijing, China, and Hebrew Bible in Jerusalem, Israel. In the past more-than-a-decade of student life, there are many people who have offered me helps and it would be a long list to name them all. Here is a list of those I especially want to mention at this occasion: Prof Yang Shi, Dr Wayne Horowitz, Mr Charles Lo, Mr Zhang Chuntian, Mr Yen Houchien, Dr Joan Goodnick-Westenholz, Dr Francoise Barbira-Freedman, Dr Simeran Gell, Mrs Miriam Sheffer, and my friend Mr Doron Grossmann. I have been financially supported by the Cambridge Overseas Trust Scholarship for China, the Overseas Research Students Award, the C H W Johns Fund Award, and the Hardship Fund and Travel Grant of Sidney Sussex College in Cambridge, by the DAAD Stipendium for research in Germany, and by the Rothberg International School Merit Scholarship in Jerusalem. I thank all of the bodies that have offered me fundings, without which I could not have gone through the times. Sidney Sussex College, my college, did most understand when I could not pay the fees in time during my first two years in Cambridge. Last but not least, I would like to thank the editors of Alter Orient und Altes Testament for accepting this volume to be published in the series. Dr Kai A. Metzler has been most helpful along the preparation of this volume for publication.
Xianhua Wang August 2010

Introduction to the Enlil Project
The beginning lines of the cursing of Agade do not easily make sense for the modern reader (OB version; ETCSL No. 2.1.5):
1sag-ki gíd-da dEn-líl-lá-ke4 2Kiški gud an-na-gin7 im-ug5-ga-ta 3é ki Unugki-ga gud maḫ-gin7 saḫar-ra mi-ni-ib-gaz-a-ta 4ki ud-ba Šarru-gen6 lugal-A-ga-dèki-ra 5sig-ta igi-nim-šè dEn-líl-le 6nam-en nam-lugal-la mu-un-na-an-šúm-ma-ta
“After Enlil’s frown had slain Kiš as if it were the Bull of Heaven, had slaughtered the house of the land of Unug in the dust as if it were a mighty bull, and then Enlil had given the rulership and kingship from the south as far as the highlands to Sargon, king of Agade...”1
The Ur III “author” of “the cursing of Agade” went further to explain the fall of Akkade as by the will of Enlil and to the blame of Narām-Sîn. What the Ur III “author” could have meant by relating historical changes in the name of Enlil the particular deity? Can we translate this ancient wisdom,
1 The transliteration and translation are adapted from the webpages at ETCSL, hereafter referred to as Black, Cunningham, Zólyomi and Robson online, at: http://etcsl.orinst.ox.ac.uk/cgi-bin/etcsl.cgi?text=c.2.1.5&display=Crit&charenc =gcirc#. The ETCSL project edited segments of the composition dated to the Ur III Period. For the dating of the text, see discussions in Cooper 1983a, pp. 5–14. The use of the terms Akkade, Agade, or Akkad can be confusing. In this thesis, except in direct quotations such as here, where the spelling Agade is retained, I use Akkade to refer to the capital city of the Sargonic dynasty and Akkad the nothern part of Babylonia.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
2
in any sense of the word, into our modern language? I decided to write a history of the deity Enlil as it was before and during the time of Narām-Sîn, expecting that such a history would help expose the Ur III author’s hidden agenda.2 Anthony Giddens thinks that in our time of “high modernity” the knowledge of “the mechanisms of system reproduction” is increasingly important in the making of our common future.3 If so, our history of Enlil that might explain some mechanisms in early Mesopotamian history may as well contribute to the current world some critical knowledge. In our daily language the word “history” means simply a true story of a certain subject matter in a given duration of time or at least I could assume so. But writing a history of a deity seems problematic if not absurd.4 In this introduction primarily intended to be a critical review of previous studies on Enlil, I will begin from previous researches to justify taking the deity as a “subject matter” of a “history” and will tentatively give a definition of “The History of Deities” at the end, so to make clear my methodological assumptions.5 The definition, tentative as it is, will
2 The chronological scope is not the third millennium which consists of the Ur III Period as well. The choice of ending with the Sargonic Period has two reasons. The first reason is that the question initially asked has little to do with the Ur III Period. The second reason is due to the sheer amount of texts from the Ur III Period, which cannot be incorporated within the time allowed for this work, on a sufficiently detailed level required by the methodology designed for this work. For the amount of the Ur III sources, see the introduction in Sallaberger 1999. 3 Giddens 1984, p. 376. In Giddens’ famous “structuration theory”, “reflexive self- regulation” highlights the “feedback effect in system reproduction, where that feedback is substantially influenced by knowledge which agents have of the mechanisms of system reproduction and employ to control it”. 4 Cf. Marwick 2001, p. 290: “History: the most concise definition is: The bodies of knowledge about the past produced by historians, together with everything that is involved in the production, communication of, and teaching about that knowledge.” The subject of “the past” here would have to the human. 5 The review part of this chapter is solely concerned with what has been asked



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 3
no doubt need further theoretical explications and cannot be taken as the best possible. It is however provided because I believe “The History of Deities” as an academic approach to individual deities, if fully developed, has important consequences for the study of ancient Mesopotamia. Hruška categorized the primary sources available for the “history of Sumerian religion” into three major types, “die archäologischen Quellen”, “die direkten schriftlichen Quellen” and “die indirekten schriftlichen Quellen”.6 In his opinion, archaeological artefacts including iconographic materials cannot be used for the “history of religion” except when they were inscribed with information spelling out their functions. In “die direkten schriftlichen Quellen” including hymns, myths, ritual texts, magical texts, and letters to deities, Hruška considered only hymns and prayers to bear on his “history of religion” but not myths and epics.7 The third category of “indirect written materials” including date formulae, epistolary letters, royal inscriptions, offering lists, economic texts, and personal names, according to Hruška, “haben für den Begriff ‘Religion’ sehr große Aussagekraft.”8 Hruška’s scheme was aimed at the “history of religion”, an approach different from here and his evaluation is questionable if seen from a different persepctive, but his typology of source materials applies to the “history” of the deity Enlil as well. In handling primary sources such as personal names and geogra
about Enlil. I do not provide documentation for all questions discussed by previous studies, since first, some of them are beyond my concern and second, others will be readdressed in the following main text. I will retain in direct quotes the original forms of transliteration and transcription used by different authors who have followed different conventions. In my own words I will use conventional normalizations for Sumerian and Akkadian as in Edzard 2003b, von Soden and Mayer 1995 and von Soden and Röllig 1991. For convenience, however, archaic texts are quoted according to Englund, Nissen and Damerow 1993, and Old Akkadian royal inscriptions according to the practice of Frayne 1993. 6 Hruška 1971, p. 196. 7 Hruška 1971, pp. 196–197. 8 Hruška 1971, p. 197.

phical names, offering lists, royal inscriptions, year formulae, and cultic or mythic texts, the current status of our knowledge concerning early Mesopotamia, especially for the time before the Sargonic Period, and the limited time and resource available for this work have made it necessary for me to follow a policy of “eclectic pragmatism”.9 The result of the work as presented here is thus an organized study on the attestations of the deity with discussions on selected questions related to the central question how Enlil the deity was relevant to the early Mesopotamian practical world.10 In so doing, the attestations of the deity in different types of materials are seen as related to the attestations of the deity in other categories of sources as in “discursive contexts”.11 The main text of the dissertation as following this introduction chapter will be organized into four parts in a chronological order. Enlil from the accession of Narām-Sîn to the end of Šarkališarri’s reign.16 In the conclusion chapter at last, an outline of the development of Enlil, the result of “The History of Deities” approach as applied to the historical study of the deity will be provided as the end product of the project. 0.1 Philological Studies of Enlil and its Name 0.1.1 “Ellil in Sumer und Akkad”
The first monograph on Enlil by Nötscher was published in 1927 under the title “Ellil in Sumer und Akkad”, neatly divided into eleven chapters.17 The author began with the etymology of the name, despite the fact that in the millennia of cuneiform traditions no Akkadian translation was known for its presumed Sumerian name.18 Jastrow understood the líl(É) in the name of the deity as “Dämon”, so interpreting the deity as the “Haupt-Dämon” while Pinches on the other hand interpreted the deity as “the lord of the plain”. Nötscher read dEn-líl in a way similar to that of Pinches, as the combination of en and líl in a grammatical relation of genitive construct. Instead of Jastrow’s “Dämon” or Pinches’ “plain”,
chronological division of the transition from the Early Dynastic to the Sargonic Period, see especially McMahon 1993, p. 16, Fig. 1. 16 Di Vito 1993 and Visicato 2000 both use the chronological term “The Sargonic Period” as purely a technical term referring to the time of Narām-Sîn and Šarkališarri because earlier Sargonic materials cannot be precisely dated as distinguishable from those of late Early Dynastic. The correctness of this practice is confirmed by the archaeological insights of McMahon 1993. 17 It might be helpful to list the titles of his chapters here: “Der Name”, “Die Familie Ellils”, “Das Gefolge” to “Stellung im Pantheon”, “Wesen und Wirken”, “Ellil in den Mythen”, “Wesensverwandte Gottheiten”, as well as “Kultstätten und Verehrung”, “Elliltage und -Feste”, “Astrale Bedeutung” and “Symbole und Darstellung”. See the “Vorwort” and “Inhaltsübersicht” in Nötscher 1927. 18 Nötscher 1927, p. 3.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 7
he understood líl as basically meaning “wind” or “storm”. “Da en in der Regel=‘Herr’ und líl(-lá)=šâru, zaqîqu, so pflegt man En-líl gewöhnlich als ‘Herr des Windes’ zu erklären.”19 The author noted that the name may even be interpreted as “Herr des Menschen”, according to first millennium lexical sources, but the most plausible explanation remained to him as that, “En-líl ursprünglich nichts anderes als Herr des Windes oder Sturmes bedeutet.”20 Nötscher categorized the many titles of Enlil in the god-list series An=dAnum into five groups. The first group shared the basic meaning “Der Große Machthaber”, the second “Der ‘große Berg’”, the third “Der Schicksalsbestimmer”, and the fourth with the basic meaning “Sturm”. The other titles of the deity in the same series, including dDam-gàr and dAb, were grouped as “Verschiedene Namen”.21 In terms of genealogy, the section in An=dAnum that is known as “Ancestors of Enlil” gave Enlil as the descendant of dEnki and Ninki, “die beide zusammen als Einheit aufgefaßt sind.”22 Ninlil was “the” wife of Enlil for Nötscher, although Ninlil had been identified with goddesses including Nin-maḫ, Nintu, and Ninḫursag, each of whom in my opinion had better be considered as a separate entity.23 “Die Kinder Anus wie Bau, Ištar, die weibliche Gottheit Nab, Nudimmud oder gar die Söhne des Enmešarra und der Ninmešarra” were not considered as Enlil’s sisters.24 Nötscher’s careful gleaning of genealogical information following his particular principles would be related to his stress on the so called Mesopotamian “genealogical thinking” in which

The epithets of Enlil had been collected and studied by Tallqvist 1938. For dDam-gàr, “divine merchant”, among them, see especially Civil 1976. 22 Nötscher 1927, p. 18, noted that in the Vase Inscription of Lugalzagesi, to be discussed later, An was the beloved father of Enlil (a ki-ág-ni) (Luzag 1). 23 Nötscher 1927, p. 22. 24 Nötscher 1927, p. 23.

8
genealogical connection between deities presumably had real political implications. It is therefore interesting to find that he acknowledged the relevance of “genealogical thinking” only when the “sons” of Enlil were discussed, as like wives, sisters, and daughters were not genealogical categories at all.25 Nergal, Sin, Šamaš, and Iškur-Adad were known to be Enlil’s sons but no evidence is readily available to delineate the political backgrounds behind these genealogical links, so that Nötscher rightly left them unexplored. In the case of Ningirsu in Old Sumerian Lagaš, what historical relationship between Lagaš and Nippur could be deduced from the epithet of Ningirsu, ur-sag-dEn-líl-lá, was also difficult for Nötscher to specify.26 The etymology of the name, “Herr des Windes oder Sturmes”, in fact served as the master concept of Nötscher’s work, most clearly so when coming to the explanation of the position of Enlil in the early Mesopotamian pantheon.27 Enlil held the highest position probably from the earliest historical time and the roles of the deity “als Herr der Länder, als Vater der Götter und Menschen” were all explained in the context of its “Wirkungskreis” from the etymology of the name which identifies the deity as the personification of the storm.28 The entire fifth chapter of his work, entitled “Wesen und Wirken”, was devoted to an integration of the characteristics of Enlil evolving around the etymology of the name in the
25 Nötscher 1927, p. 24: “Die Babylonier pflegen ihrem Wesen nach verwandte Gottheiten in ein genealogisches Verhältnis zueinander zu setzen. Zuweilen sind dabei auch politische Gründe von Bedeutung: Der Gott des unterjochenden Volkes wird der Sohn des unterjochten oder umgekehrt, damit die mit der politischen Veränderung eingetretenen neuen religiösen Verhältnisse nicht als drückend oder verletzend empfunden werden.” 26 Cf. Nötscher 1927, p. 30. The possible political implication of Ningirsu being ur-sag-dEn-líl-lá in Lagaš inscriptions was first considered in Paffrath 1913, p. 79. 27 Nötscher 1927, p. 38: “später hat Ellil zwar noch eine Art Ehrenvorrang, in Wirklichkeit ist aber für Babylon Marduk, für Assyrien Assur der dEn-líl-lá ilâni.” 28 Cf. Nötscher 1927, p. 42: “daß Ellil in jener alten Zeit der bedeutendste Gott gewesen sein muß.”



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 9
sense that Nötscher interprets it.29 The philological thoroughness of Nötscher’s work is remarkable even to this day but methodologically his approach had serious problems, the most significant being the lack of attention to the contexts of his source materials. The attestation of the deity in any given context is ultimately the result of the deity’s relevance in social life. The study of the deity must be fully informed by this societal dimension, especially when there was clearly a diversity of traditions. The author laboured to provide an integrated theological image of “Ellil in Sumer und Akkad” beginning from his interpretation for the etymology of the name without explaining away the existence of diverse traditions. It is impossible that over a long period of time and a large geographical area such as ancient Mesopotamia the many peoples had the same theology of the deity that can be derived from the etymology of the name alone. Nötscher did not claim to have followed a historical approach, and it was clear that his work was primarily philological so that the problems resulting from the lack of attention to the contexts of his sources are perhaps tolerable, especially if considering the chronological scope covered therein, the entire cuneiform tradition from the third to the first millennium. The author’s documentation would have provided the basis for later enquiry and the problems observable from his work should have invoked re-examinations of the methodological questions in approaching the subject matter. The paradox is that, although the basic source materials were collected and discussed as early as in the 1920s, the many related questions and problems have been touched upon, the question that continued to receive scholarly attention, oddly enough, continues to be that of etymology, and more oddly also in combination with theology, without ever asking whether and how the ancients were concerned with abstract theology at all.30

0.1.2 “Religionswissenschaft”
If Nötscher’s work could be characterized as a combination of philology and theology, the second major work on Enlil by Piesl may be considered a combination of complex “Religionswissenschaften” with philology.31 “Religionswissenschaft” is the German equivalent of what is commonly known as “The History of Religion” or “The Comparative Study of Religion” in English that aims at universal theories of human religiosity. In the field of “religious studies”, however as we know today, the basic assumption in this approach, characteristically a universal definition of “religion”, has long been criticized to have suffered from problematic theoretical/theological prejudices.32 She divided her work into two major parts, entitled “Phänomene im religiösen und Sozialen Bereich” for part I and “Präanthropomorphem (Totem) und Anthropomorphem in der sumerischen Religion: kur und den.lil2” for part II. She was concerned with the “Religionswissenschaftliche” process of “Anthropomorphisierung” in Sumerian “religious history”.33 Piesl interpreted the Sumerian word kur especially known in the
with theology. Abstract theology is most characteristic in the Christian tradition to which Nötscher the author of Ellil in Sumer and Akkad certainly belonged. Nötscher was a well known theologian and Biblical scholar. 31 Piesl 1969, p. 8, quoting G. van der Leeuw: “Was der Religionswissenschaft Objekt der Religion heißt, ist der Religion selbst Subjekt.” 32 Cf. Smith 1963, p. 43: “Religion as a systematic entity, as it emerged in the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries, is a concept of polemics and apologetics”. Sharpe 1975 is a very useful introduction to “The History of Religion” though somewhat out of date. It is necessary here to clarify that the word “religion” in this work is used only for convenience or in quotes from secondary literature. Otherwise, I avoid using the word or put it in quotation marks when necessary. Veldhuis 2004, pp. 11–13 and p. 16, gave a brief introduction to the problems in the application of the term to ancient Mesopotamia. 33 Piesl 1969, pp. 57–62, began her practical part with another introduction to earlier views of Sumerian “religion”.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 11
Fāra texts as the “Numinose Macht” of the Sumerian “Kollektiv.”34 “Die Anthropomorphisierung” happened when a Semitic Enlil took over the é-kur in Nippur, later came to be known as Enlil’s abode but before then was the “Machtzentrum der jeweiligen menschlichen Gemeinschaft” of the Sumerians in Piesl’s perception.35 The introduction of Enlil together with his Semitic “*’ll-Theologie” reorganized the previous belief system though “eine Angleichung und zum Teil Identifizierung mit dem alten Numen kur(.gal)” could still be recognized in Sumerian literary texts and in personal names from Fāra where Enlil was attested as an element in onomastic contexts compatible with those of the Sumerian noun kur.36 The assumption that Enlil was not Sumerian but Semitic in origin was critical for Piesl’s theory for the development of the “mesopotamischen Hochkultur”, as in her opinion “Anthropomorphisierung” was achieved only when the Sumerians had “aus dem semitischen Bereich stammende Numen ’ll einbezogen und zur Gottheit den.lil2 umgebildet.”37 It may be pointed out that, while Nötscher constructed his theology of Enlil beginning from a Sumerian etymology of the name, Piesl brought in with *’ll a Semitic origin, also on etymological grounds and with the same assumption that the origin of the name explains the deity. It must be said that, the assumptions that Enlil was actually the Semitic *’ll with its theological import and that kur signified specifically the “Numinose Macht” of the Sumerian “Kollektiv” were both hypothetical in Piesl’s work. The linkage Piesl proposed between them is at best theoretical, while historically the assumptions are not well grounded. It is impossible here to delineate the complex chain of concepts Piesl labored under but as noted by Hruška soon after the publication of her work, she seemed to be unaware of the contradictory philosophical assumptions behind these concepts, some of which must be considered incompatible with one ano

ther and unable to be incorporated within any single framework.38
0.1.3 “The Overlord of Nippur”
Steinkeller reopened discussions of Enlil and Ninlil.39 In the shorter version of his conference communication published in a footnote ten years later, Steinkeller also started interpreting Enlil with the etymology of the name. “The traditional etymology of Enlil’s name, ‘Lord Wind’ or ‘Air-God,’ which assumes that this deity represented a life-giving breath animating the universe, cannot be maintained on either epigraphic or etymological grounds.”40 The evidence Steinkeller summoned to dismantle the traditional view comes first from epigraphy, as he pointed out that throughout the third millennium Enlil’s name was written with the sign combination dEN.É but not dEN.LÍL(KID). This somehow incorrect observation suggested to him that “Enlil’s name is a logogram, whose literal sense is ‘master of the household, paterfamilias’.”41 Steinkeller’s second category of evidence against the traditional view came from semantics, since the meaning of líl in Sumerian is “ghost, haunting spirit” (zaqīqu, lilû in Akkadian) such as in the phrase èš líl-lá,
38 Cf. Hruška 1971, p. 191. Piesl 1969, pp. 13–56, introduced a list of concepts from theories with philosophical backgrounds ranging from Marxist materialism, positivist structuralism and Phenomenology. The theoretical concepts Piesl took use of, including “Numinose Macht” and “Das Kollektiv”, may represent how the “religions” of remote historical peoples were perceived at the author’s time of writing. Piesl’s view of Sumerian “religion” was in line with Oberhuber 1963. Note Hruška 1971, p. 194, had proposed instead that anthropomorphism and totemism in early Mesopotamia are prehistoric phenomena. 39 Steinkeller 1988 was not published. 40 Steinkeller 1999, p. 114, n. 36. 41 Steinkeller’s observation is not precise at least, as will be shown in Part II of the main text. Note Steinkeller refers here to Enlil’s Early Dynastic title ab-ba dingir-dingir-re-ne so that Enlil the paterfamilias here meant more specifically “father of the divine family.” The same idea was expressed in Steinkeller 1993a.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 13
“haunted house” and eden líl-lá, “haunted plain”, but never “wind”. The meaning of the combination En-líl-ø would be therefore “Lord-Ghost”, “making the deity a harbinger of death, rather than a bestower of life.”42 His conclusion at this point was that, if the name of Enlil was Sumerian, the meaning of the name would be either paterfamilias or “Lord-Ghost”, the latter of which was unlikely since it does not match the known characteristics of the deity. The alternative interpretation Steinkeller offered is, Enlil was not a Sumerian name at all, since there are various indications that Enlil may have been a Semitic one. The evidence Steinkeller summoned in support of a possible Semitic origin of Enlil comes at first from Ebla, where the earliest attested spelling of the name is I-li-lu. The Ebla spelling suggested to Steinkeller a possible etymology *il-ilī, “god of (all) the gods”. Steinkeller’s second point is theological, since “Enlil was a typical universal god, lacking any clear individual traits and without any specific domain,” therefore very much like El, Aššur, and Dagan.43 The third point is that, dNIN.LÍL(KID) was nothing but the female reflection of Enlil, as Steinkeller pointed out that the only certain pronunciation of Ninlil’s name is Mulliltu which in the third millennium had to be read *Illiltu, simply the feminine form of the Akkadian *Illilu.44 That Enlil’s name is understandable in Semitic etymology, the characteristics of the deity, and the possible reading of his wife’s name in the third millennium, *Illiltu, as a Semitic feminine form, combined to support Steinkeller’s suggestion that Enlil was Semitic by origin, il-ilī, “God of Gods.” Steinkeller ended his communication promising a systematic treatment of Enlil and outlined the arguments he planned to make. The first argument he planned to present is that the original divine ruler of Nippur was Ninurta. The second is that the cult of Enlil was brought to Nippur from Northern Babylonia, though in great antiquity, and as a consequence of which Enlil became the overlord of Nippur and Ninurta’s
42 Steinkeller 1999, p. 114, n. 36. 43 Steinkeller 1999, p. 114, n. 36. 44 Steinkeller 1999, p. 114, n. 36.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
14
father. The third argument he was to make is that Ninlil was an artificial creation, which was superimposed on the cult of Ninḫursag, the goddess of Tummal and the original mother of Ninurta.45 Steinkeller’s promised study of Enlil in early Mesopotamia has unfortunately not appeared but it was clear that the author’s views were still being developed when the communication was published.
0.1.4 “The Lil2 of dEn-lil2”
Jacobsen made his response to Steinkeller’s idea concerning the etymology of the name of Enlil even before the latter publicized his views quoted above, in an article entitled specifically “The Lil2 of dEn-lil2”.46 Jacobsen pointed out that the question of the meaning of the divine name Enlil was to be resolved through the development of the orthography of the sign líl in the early periods, practically the third millennium, since in texts written after the Ur III Period the second signs in the names of the couple Enlil and Ninlil were consistently written with the sign LÍL = KID = SUḪ. In texts before that, only the name of Ninlil was so written while the second sign in the name of Enlil was always written É.47 In order to illustrate the development of the orthography of the sign líl, Jacobsen provided a palaeographical table in which it was considered that by the time of Ur-Nammu, the sign with the phonetic value lil/ kidx/suḫ converged with that of é/líl in the form RÉC 423 while before that time the value kid/lilx/suḫ was written with the sign RÉC 425.48 The
45 Steinkeller 1999, p. 114, n. 36. 46 Jacobsen 1989, p. 267, n. 1. 47 Jacobsen 1989, p. 267. 48 Cf. Jacobsen 1989, p. 267, with documentations in pp. 268–269. For the value of lilx for RÉC 425 in the name of Ninlil in earlier periods, see Biggs 1966, p. 84, n. 85, Biggs 1974, p. 111 with n. 3, Westenholz 1975b, p. 4, n. 14, and the personal name dNin-KID-lá in Westenholz 1975b, OSP 1 023. The sigla RÉC 423 and 425 refer to signs numbered in Thureau-Dangin 1898. KIDa from the archaic texts was added into Jacobsen’s table by Englund 1998, p. 75, n. 151.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 15
question arises why the names of Enlil and Ninlil were written with different signs which however had the same phonetic value /lil/. In Jacobsen’s view, “[t]he simplest explanation for this is clearly that two different words lil are involved in the two names.”49 RÉC 425 is known from Early Dynastic sources to have the value /lil/, which as written in the name of Nin-/lil/ (RÉC 425) resulted from the fact that the goddess was a personification of the full grown young barley plant.50 The writing RÉC 423 for the value /lil/ in the name of Enlil is more complex. In Jacobsen’s view the form Il-lil in An=dAnum in Akkadian context must be the Akkadianized form of the name Enlil in Sumerian, rendering the Sumerian /e/ as /i/ and assimilating /n/ with /l/.51 This is to say, phonetically the Akkadian spelling of *Illilu resulted from the Akkadianization of the Sumerian original and not the other way around. The phonetic value /lil/ could be written with RÉC 423 as well, though the meaning of RÉC 423 was different from that of RÉC 425. Jacobsen then deduced the meaning of /lil/ (RÉC 423) from the lexical series Ea in which LÍL has the meaning šâru, the general term for wind, and zaqīqu, the more gentle breeze. Therefore, in Jacobsen’s view,
“The name Enlil can thus be rendered ‘Lord Wind.’ It indicates that the god was seen as a personification of the wind and the numinous power in it much as the name of the god An presents him as the deified sky and the old name of Nin-ḫursaga, Ḫursaĝ presents her as the deified foothill.”52
Jacobsen concluded after examining the attestations of líl in Sumerian literary as well as lexical texts that, for líl, the impression one gets is that of a semi-archaic term surviving only in set literary tropes characteristic of literary genres. Even if it is possible to distinguish the use of the word
49 Jacobsen 1989, p. 269. 50 Jacobsen 1989, p. 269 and ns. 7–11. For a careful introduction to the goddess Ninlil see now Krebernik 2001b. 51 Jacobsen 1989, p. 270. 52 Jacobsen 1989, p. 270.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
16
in incantations from that in laments, and from that in myths and hymns, all of them derive from “wind”, “slightly differently seen and stressed.”53 Jacobsen reaffirmed in his article that there was no phonetic problem in writing the Sumerian name Enlil in Akkadian as *Illilu and that the etymology of the name of Enlil was safely dEn-líl-ø, “Lord Wind”, in Sumerian. It may be noted that Jacobsen’s understanding of the meaning of “líl” was by and large in agreement with that of Nötscher, though with more thorough documentation from the Early Dynastic to the Ur III Period including many source materials unavailable to the Nötscher.54 It needs to be stressed, however, that Nötscher’s etymology of the name of Enlil, “Herr des Windes”, would have assumed the name to be a genitive construct meaning literally “the en of líl” whose normalization in Sumerian is dEn.líl.á(k), while the relation between en and líl for Jacobsen was apposition rather than genitive, the normalization of which would be dEn.líl.ø and only in genitive compound (é) dEn.líl.á(k).55
0.1.5 “The Usurpation of Enki”
Michalowski was not slow to join in the debate on the origin of Enlil, though his method was more mythological analysis than epigraphy or palaeography. In his article with a very literary title, Michalowski started from the general cultural development of early Mesopotamia, which has to be interpreted anew to accommodate a different interpretation of Enlil.56 The author began his paper with a brief introduction to the con
53 Jacobsen 1989, p. 271 and pp. 271–275, explained that in incantations líl means the “wind demon”, in laments “wind”, in myths and hymns “spirit”. 54 Nötscher 1927, pp. 1–8. 55 Jacobsen 1989, p. 267, n. 1: “the earliest writing of Enlil with following la2 known to us is the genitive šuš-gal dEn-lil2-la2 ‘the throw-net of Enlil’ in Stele of the Vultures xvi 13-18”. 56 The title of Michalowski 1998, “The Unbearable Lightness of Enlil”, must be an imitation of The Unbearable Lightness of Being, translation of Nesnesitelná lehkost bytí written by the Czech novelist Milan Kundara first published in 1984.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 17
cept of language union, or Sprachbund in the German language, and went on to criticize Assyriology which as a whole suffers too much from “reverse evolutionism” and “naive realism”.57 Michalowski finds a kind of backward evolutionary trajectory in the methods of Assyriological studies. This methodological problem is particularly harmful for the study of “religion”. For example, the epic of Enūma Eliš is often taken to shed light on the “religion” of early Mesopotamia when the composition was in fact “cultic and religious polemics” that represented a radical break with past beliefs.58 Michalowski was also able to tell that studies of early Mesopotamian “religion” were too often “involved in collecting materials and performing limited concrete tasks that they had no inclination to revise their philosophical assumptions.”59 In relation to the early history of Enlil, Michalowski first of all points out that the etymological interpretation of the deity as “lord of the air” or the like is highly problematic, since the word líl which usually means “haunted” or “abandoned” in lamentation texts was never used with precisely the meaning of “wind” in Sumerian literature.60 The traditional identification of Enlil with an area between heaven and earth based on the same etymological interpretation, too often uncritically taken to explain the origin of the deity as the personification of air, wind, or as an astral manifestation, also cannot be true at least in the earlier times. The earliest mythic texts known from Mesopotamia, such as the UD.GAL.NUN texts, the literary fragment AO 4153 from Early Dynastic Girsu, and the Barton Cylinder from Nippur, do not actually have any normative views in their descriptions of primordial events that can identify Enlil as occupying an area between heaven and earth. Michalowski considers that the earliest myths witnessed only a
57 Michalowski 1998, p. 238. Edzard 2003b paid due attention to this approach. 58 Michalowski 1998, p. 239. 59 Michalowski 1998, p. 237 quoted Roman Jacobson and Peter Bogatyrëv of the Prague Linguistic Circle. The implication that Mesopotamian “religion” needs to be seen from a similarly multicultural perspective is undoubtedly right. 60 Michalowski 1998, p. 240, referring to Michalowski 1989, p. 98.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
18
“kaleidoscopic meditation on representation of a mythic space” in which Enlil may have acted to bring about the separation of heaven and earth but never flew in to occupy the void.61 In mythological texts that are only known through later manuscripts but probably with early backgrounds such as the composition Enki and the World Order, on the other hand, the concept of a sexually infused divine couple seemed essential. Michalowski drew on these texts to suggest that the head of the pantheon in the earliest tradition of southern Mesopotamia was perhaps the divine couple Enki and Ninḫursag associated primarily with fertility instead of any single deity cosmologically high in space.62 The order in which the earliest Mesopotamian pantheon was organized was perhaps very much different than the vertical order construed by the etymologists, who believe that An “the heavens”, or “on high”, was separated from the domain of Enki, “lord of the earth”, by Enlil “lord of the air”, but are unaware that all this is an anachronism.63 The collapse of the Sumerian etymology of the name and of the anachronic order in which the etymologists believe the earliest Mesopotamian pantheon was organized problematized the early history of Enlil so that a different interpretation is needed. Michalowki turned also to the Eblaite I-li-lu which in original contexts had the epithet “father of the gods”. Michalowski was certain that “there can be no doubt that we are in the presence of the Mesopotamian Enlil.”64 The attestation of Enlil in Ebla in the spelling I-li-lu suggests to Michalowski that the deity was Semitic by origin, and was only integrated into the Mesopotamian pantheon originally headed by the couple Enki and Ninḫursag in a later time. Michalowski interpreted the process of the integration as a usurpation of the old Enki by the new comer Enlil in mythological terms. The usurpation of Enki by Enlil, furthermore, was a part of a “constant flow of structural shifts in which systems and subsystems shifted and were
61 Michalowski 1998, pp. 240–241, with ns. 3 and 5. 62 Michalowski 1998, p. 244. 63 Michalowski 1998, p. 240. 64 Michalowski 1998, p. 241, with n. 8.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 19
redesigned”, along with which the principle of ordering the pantheon switched from that of the divine couple to that of a vertical hierarchy. But Michalowski warns that “the ebb and flow of systems and subsystems cannot be accounted for by recourse to a form of determinism derived from social evolutionary ideas.”65 The usurpation of Enki by Enlil does not find immediate evidence in later myths although Michalowski believes that there are echoes recognizable in later traditions. The roots of all later echoes that may be interpreted with a usurpation of Enki by Enlil, according to Michalowski, went “far beyond the time of the first written documents” and “much earlier than the end of the fourth millennium”, which is to say, deep in prehistoric time.66 The time period in which Michalowski locates the “usurpation of Enki” will betray the imaginative nature of his proposal but the essential part of his argument for the Semitic origin of Enlil, as the author acknowledges, does by and large agree with those proposed by Steinkeller, although the latter was more “involved in collecting materials and performing limited concrete tasks”.67
0.1.6 “Enlil, Vater der Götter”
In a footnote to his published paper, Michalowski reported that “in private discussions at the Rencontre several scholars noted the problems of the particular Ebla spelling with initial i, which would suggest /yi/ rather than /’i/.”68 He explained that “the Semitic origins of the name are much older than this, and that the Ebla syllabic spelling represents a secondary stage, in which the Semitic phonological structure of the name had long been adapted to the Sumerian language.”69 Edzard has recently taken up the challenge, focusing on the phonetics of the Ebla writing
65 Michalowski 1998, p. 243. 66 Michalowski 1998, pp. 243–244. 67 Michalowski 1998, p. 244. 68 Michalowski 1998, p. 242, n. 2. 69 Michalowski 1998, p. 242, n. 2.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
20
I-li-lu and Enlil.70 First of all, Edzard identifies the key question posited by the Ebla writing of the name:
“Falls É für [lil] steht, wäre li-l(u) ganz und gar unproblematisch. Auch die Assimilation des auslautenden –n von [en] an ein folgendes [l] lässt sich unschwer erklären. So reduziert sich die Aufgabe, eine Formannäherung vorzunehmen, auf den Anlaut von EN. Wir mussten bei einer interpretatio sumerica folgerichtig ein mit [y] anlautendes EN ansetzen, also *[yen] oder – mit neutralem V(okal) - *[yVn].”71
Edzard then presented materials from lexical lists, especially texts from Ebla, in order to show that “EN wäre wohl nicht so häufig als akk. Syllabogramm für [yi(n)] verwendet worden, wenn es nicht ein sumerisches Vorbild mit y-Anlaut gegeben hätte, also *[yVn].”72 The other way around, however, an original reading of *Yēn/Yīn-lil for the Sumerian EN is not impossible if we think of (y)ēn:umun in Sumerian in parallel to nin:gašan. But if approached this way, unwillingly,
“Wir kommen zurück auf das Dilemma, das sich uns darbietet in der Schreibung dEn-É mit seit Eanatum von Lagaš nachweisbarem Auslaut auf -l. Wir können auf eine – sehr knappe – Diskussion von líl nicht verzichten; denn ausser líl steht uns kein anderes sumerisches Lemma zur Verfügung, das einem É-l entsprechen würde.”73
The original reading *Yēn/Yīn-lil, nevertheless, is difficult to make mean “Herr Wind”, because the connotation of líl:šāru is negative and “líl kann daher auf keinen Fall eine gewöhnliche Entsprechung von šāru und

ein Synonym von im, tum9 ‘Wind’ sein.”74 The negative “Wind” cannot fit the image of the deity so that the candidate for the meaning of líl will have to be sought among “‘Schemen’, ‘Phantom’, ‘Leere’, ‘Nichts’”, the other meanings of the word /lil/ known in the Sumerian language.75 The uneasy question remains that whether any of these candidates could be thinkable for the Mesopotamians from the time as remote as the 3rd Millennium BCE:
“Hat es im sumerischen Pantheon einen ‘Herrn Schemen’ gegeben? Hat ein Gott, und zwar ein in der Götterhierarchie sehr hoch angesiedelter Gott, einen Bereich verkörpert, der un-fassbar, schwer zu begreifen war? Wäre es denkbar, dass man den Namen eines so gestalteten Gottes ausweichend, ‘tabuisierend’ schrieb: dEn-É?”76
Edzard’s study somehow resolves the problem in Michalowki’s argument except, most importantly, the Sumerian but not the Akkadian seems to be the original. Put again in Edzard’s words, the problems concerning the possible “Semitic” origin of Enlil remains:

untermauert werden.”77
0.2 Diachronic Presentations of Enlil
The philological tradition as applied to the study of Enlil which systematically began with Nötscher is certainly fundamental but it must be pointed out that philological enquiry, mostly on the etymology of the name as presented above, served too often theological or not dissimilarly, “religionswissenschaftlische” purposes. The concrete results of philological studies like Edzard’s phonological argument that the Eblaite spelling (d)I-li-l(u) did seem to refer to the same deity as the Sumerian Enlil, must still be the basis of further research, but I believe the combination of philology with theological or “religionswissenschaftlische” theorybuilding is unfortunate. It has to be admitted that theology and theories of human religiosity are somehow both beyond the field philologists can do best, which is history that needs theoretical and even theological thinking but only in a particular way and which is primarily concerned with the development of things in the real world. It is clear that in the case of Piesl theories of “religion” did not help much but deverted her to unknown territories.
0.2.1 “Enlil und Nippur nach Präsargonischen Quellen”
In his presentation of Enlil, Selz set his context exclusively in historical terms without engaging with theology.78 In my view Selz was in principle right to adopt the policy of excluding literary texts of later dates. This is a more self-conscious choice when theoretical thinking is
77 Edzard 2003a, p. 184. 78 Selz 1992 was read at the 35th Rencontre in 1988 and was one of the earlier attempts he made to sketch an overall picture of early Mesopotamian “religion”, beginning from his studies of Pre-Sargonic Lagaš. The theme of the Rencontre was “Nippur, the Holy City of the Sumerians” with particularly relevant contributions on Enlil and Nippur. See especially Gibson 1992 and Lieberman 1992.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 23
applied in historical studies in a more appropriate way.79 Selz suggested that Nippur was not purely a religious centre without political or military role, as earlier views held, since he believes that at least during the Fāra Period, “Nippur greift aktiv [ein], auch militärisch”, in a “mesopotamischen Koine” associated with Kiš.80 Nippur had been the centre of “einer Amphiktyonie” for which Enlil was the head of the divine assembly, a situation that resulted from historical developments going back into the “frühgeschichtlicher Zeit”. The relationship between Enlil and the city of Nippur, according to Selz, is to be understood in terms of “Zuständigkeiten der Stadtgottheiten”. 81 Enlil was known earlier in Lagaš, such as in the inscriptions of Eannatum, but the establishment of its cult there was a part of the “Grossmachtpolitik” of Entemena.82
0.2.2 “Nippur als religiöses Zentrum Mesopotamiens”
Sallaberger gave a diachronic presentation on Nippur as known from sources from the later half of the third millennium to the first half of the second millennium.83 The author focused on the peculiarity of Nippur as a religious centre, the ways in which the rulers of the central government acted towards the city in comparison to that towards other cities in the course of time. The second and related question was the
79 Selz 1992, p. 193, explained that there would be too many complicated methodological problems if such materials are taken into consideration. 80 Selz 1992, pp. 192–193, against Westenholz 1979. 81 Selz 1992, p. 193. Ibid., pp. 199–200 posits a “kulttopographisch” theory for the principle of composition of the “Götterlisten” from Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ. 82 Selz 1992, p. 200, with documentation in pp. 200-203. 83 Sallaberger 1997, pp. 147–148. Ibid., p. 147: “Vom Beginn relevanter Quellen in der Mitte des dritten Jahrtausends bis zum Ende zweiten Jahrtausends gilt in Babylonien Enlil als höchster Gott; sein Hauptheiligtum Ekur liegt in Nippur. Enlils Macht äußert sich vor allem in seinen Entscheidungen, seinem ‘Wort’, das unabänderbar gilt, und das nicht nur den Lauf der Geschichte, sondern auch das Handeln der Götter bestimmt.”



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
24
implication of the control or the loss of control of the city of Nippur to royal ideology in relation to the role the city played in the changes of its political status.84 Enlil was the supreme deity in the Mesopotamian pantheon, Nippur was the cultic centre of Enlil, and there was a special connection between Enlil and kingship. But in the Early Dynastic and Sargonic Periods how exactly kingship was related to Enlil and whether coronations took place in the temple of Enlil in Nippur were unclear to the author.85 Sallaberger disagreed with Jacobsen in relating the locality of “Kiengi” within the city of Nippur, since more recent research based on early sources from the site Fāra and other places shows that the place “Kiengi” probably lies in the region of Uruk.86 Sallaberger pointed out that until the Sargonic Period, it was still Enlil and not Ninurta that fulfilled the role of the city god of Nippur, since the énsi of Nippur seemed to be still taking care of the cult of Enlil while the énsi of Nippur was not an interregional ruler.87 Ninurta was missing in the Temple Hymns and the godlists from Abū Ṣalābīḫ, while in the godlists from Fāra he was only attested in theologically insignificant parts. Ninurta is also unknown in Old Sumerian building and votive inscriptions, a fact that could be due to the chance of discovery but has also to be taken into consideration. The temple of Ninurta is not securely located and the wife of Ninurta, Nin-Nibru is unknown in sources from the time periods earlier than Ur III.88 Sallaberger thus believes that the differentiation between the national patron deity Enlil and the city patron of Nippur Ninurta most probably happened in the Sargonic Period. In Sallaberger’s perception,
“[i]m Status des Götterherrschers Enlil als königlichem Reichsgott
84 Sallaberger 1997, p. 148. 85 Sallaberger 1997, pp. 150–151, observed that Enlil at this time was invoked especially when Babylonian kings interacted with foreign lands. 86 Sallaberger 1997, p. 151 with n. 17. 87 Sallaberger 1997, p. 152. 88 Sallaberger 1997, pp. 152–153.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 25
mag sich durchaus die neue Situation der Großreichsbildung widerspiegeln. Die Suprematie Enlils führte also ab sargonischer Zeit zu einer einzigartigen Differenzierung von zwei Ebenen in Nippur: da Enlil nun vornehmlich als Reichsgott betrachtet wird, übernimmt Ninurta die Funktion des Stadtgottes”.89
Sallaberger was concerned with a much longer time than from the Early Dynastic to the Sargonic Period which is the time concerned in this work. In order to focus on current views on Enlil in the periods I am concerned, I introduced his major points only for the first phase in his periodization. Sallaberger’s study on Nippur is instructive as the author made explicit the questions to be answered, and the author also showed a much clearer methodological awareness though this is not always made explicit. It also needs to be pointed out that the subject matter of Sallaberger’s study is the city of Nippur but not the deity Enlil, of which methodological questions are even more complex, though the close link between the city and the deity make his work particularly valuable. In fact, Enlil is so central for the writing of early Mesopotamian history that diachronic presentations of the subject matter have to begin with re-examining the entire history of the early periods for which unfortunately too many questions remain.
0.2.3 “Beiträge zum Pantheon von Nippur”
The most recent diachronic presentation on Enlil is found in Such-Gutiérrez’ dissertation entitled “Beiträge zum Pantheon von Nippur im 3. Jahrtausend”. The purpose of the dissertation was to provide a diachronic presentation of the Nippurian deities in the third millennium
89 Sallaberger 1997, p. 153, with references to OSP 2 074 and MC 4 011, both of which are from classical and early Sargonic times. Note especially Ibid., p. 153, n. 29: “Daß es sich um eine Entwicklung der Stellung Enlils und nicht etwas schon vorher Feststehende handelt, zeigt m.E. die Gegenreaktion gegen diese Entwicklung durch Lugalnigzu in sargonischer Zeit.”



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
26
primarily using “Verwaltungsurkunden und der Weihinschriften” while “literarische Quellen” and onomastic evidence from personal names were not “systematisch behandelt.”90 Such-Gutiérrez paid substantial attention in his dissertation to the cultic aspects of Enlil, for instance that of the temple complex and the related cultic personnel and so forth. This has much to recommend it. But it may be pointed out that larger scale historical questions are not adequately addressed and the chronological order in which his source materials were organized was not always precisely defined. These problems may be understandable if considering the design of his work and the fact that the early history of Mesopotamia is by no means clear. Such-Gutiérrez presents Enlil as the first of the “Hauptgottheiten” of Nippur, Enlil, Ninlil, Ninurta, Nusku, and Inanna which were discussed one by one. The author organized his presentation of each deity in a chronological order from the Late Uruk Period to the Ur III Period.91 In his very short introduction on the writing of the name of Enlil and of the deity in the archaic periods, the author considers that “die erstern Zeugnisse für Enlil aus der Schicht Uruk III lassen kaum etwas über die Rolle Enlils aussagen.”92 The presentation of Enlil therefore effectively began with the “frühdynastische Zeit IIIa” Period for which materials from Fāra, Abū Ṣalābīḫ but also those from Early Dynastic Ebla were considered. The author considers that in this period of time, when Enlil seemed to legitimize nam-lugal, “kingship”, in northern Sumer, Inanna of Uruk may have held a similar role in the south where the matter of concern was instead nam-en, “rulership”. The two traditions in which Enlil legitimizes nam-lugal and Inanna legitimizes nam-en respectively were only integrated in the subsequent ED IIIb Period. It seems Such-Gutiérrez assumed that Enlil had become the national
90 Cf. Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 23: “Als grundlegendes Arbeitsmaterial für die vorliegende Untersuchung wurden hauptsächlich die Verwaltungstexte, die Weihinschriften und die Hymnen bei den ‘Hauptgottheiten’ herangezogen.” 91 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 28. 92 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 31, n. 28.

He somehow also presumed the existence of the temple of Enlil in the city of Nippur during the ED IIIa Period.93 This temple, however, is strictly speaking unknown in ED IIIa primary sources, as will be shown in Part II of this work. In the ED IIIb Period when the earlier regional traditions fused together, Such-Gutiérrez pointed out that Enlil was associated with the royal epithet lugal-kalam-ma.94 He believed that at this time é-kur was already known as the name of the “Tempelkomplex” of Enlil, and that èš-Nibruki as known from the Barton Cylinder meant the “‘Sanktuarium’ von Nippur” related to the priestly title nu-èš. He thus depicted a scene in Nippur in which:
“Im Tempel Enlils, dem Ekur, befindet sich èš, ein ‘Sanktuarium’, das den Vorgänger der Ziqqurrat von UrN [Ur-Nammu] darstellte. In diesem ‘Sanktuarium’ müssen Enlil, sein Zepter und Ninlil verehrt worden sein. ”95
The attestation of sanga-dEn-líl in ED IIIb votive inscriptions from Nippur that are dedicated to Inanna and Ninlil suggested to Such-Gutiérrez “eine Beziehung zwischen dem Tempel des Enlil und jenem der Inanna hin, die auch in der Ur-III Zeit bezeugt ist”.96 The cursing of Agade and the literary works traditionally attributed to Enheduanna were both discussed in the section for Such-Gutiérrez’ “altakkadische Zeit” though he was aware that the dating of the contents of the latter texts is not certain and that of the former clearly later than
93 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 32, n. 36. 94 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 33. 95 The title nu-èš is interpreted here as “etwa der ‘Mann: “Sanktuarium”’, eine Kategorie von Priester, die bis jetzt nur bei Enlil bezeugt ist.” Such-Gutiérrez 2003, pp. 34-35, discussed also his identification of é-kur with é-dEn-líl-lá regardless of the fact that the latter phrase is known only from the time of Šarkališarri at the earliest. 96 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 36, n. 63.
