the Sargonids. Enheduanna’s works appeared to the author to testify “der Aufstieg Inannas” at the time of Narām-Sîn at the expense of Enlil, the more traditional god of kingship.97 The royal inscriptions of Narām-Sîn were also summoned to support “der Aufstieg Inannas”, though the author acknowledged that they could be interpreted otherwise.98
0.3 Enlil in Historical Perception
Steinkeller noted that among Thorkild Jacobsen’s many pioneering works, based on “a combination of exemplary deduction, great intuition, and free imagination”, his studies in the historical and cultural development of early Mesopotamia occupy an especially prominent and important place.99 Jacobsen actually provided the only hitherto most complete outline of ancient Mesopotamian “history of religion” that does in my view represent how the “history of religion” has been done for the Sumerians, at least insofar as Enlil is concerned. As introduced already, Jacobsen is particularly interested in this deity. First of all for this reason it becomes legitimate to begin with Jacobsen’s works to explore the methodological questions diachronic presentations of Enlil may encounter. The great influence Jacobsen has imposed on the current general understanding of early Mesopotamian “religion” will also call for a methodological reflection beginning with his works.100
0.3.1 “Ruler Metaphor” and the “Lord Wind”
Jacobsen in his outline of the ancient Mesopotamian “history of
97 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, pp. 39-40. 98 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 38: “die Frage muß aufgrund der wegigen verfügbaren Daten über ‘Krönungsriten’ in der altakkadischen Periode offenbleiben.” 99 Steinkeller 2002a, p. 249. 100 The articles of Mesopotamian religion in Encyclopedia Britannica and The Encyclopedia of Religion, here referred to as Eliade and Adams 1987, were all Jacobsen’s contributions.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 29
religion” published in the seventies of the last century divided the entire history into three phases, each characterized by a type of “metaphor” as he understood the word. The earliest phase dates to the fourth millennium when worship was centred on powers in natural and other phenomena essential for economic survival, and the dying god, the power of fertility and plenty, was the typical figure. The second phase dates approximately to the third millennium when the concept of the ruler and the hope of security against enemies featured most pre-eminently. “The great ruler gods” of the Nippur assembly were typical figures in the second phase. The third phase dates to the second millennium when the fortunes of the individual increased in importance until they rivalled those of communal economy and security. The personal god was the typical figure in the third phase.101 In the second phase the highest authority in the universe as known from political mythology was the assembly of the gods in which Enlil or Nunamnir, next to An in rank, embodied energy and force.102 Jacobsen acknowledged in his “history of Mesopotamian religion” that it would be easy for his readers, “to think the religious metaphor far more representative than it in fact is and can be, however widespread and dominant it may seem.”103 The readers may also “overweight the human nature of the religious metaphor and forget that its purpose is to point beyond itself and the world from which it was taken”.104 The problem Jacobsen did not explain in his reflection on his methods is that, his “history of Mesopotamian religion” does not have a definition for the Mesopotamian “deity” at all when deities are spoken of everywhere in his history. Jacobsen used the term “ruler metaphor” for the characterization of the second phase in which Enlil was the most characteristic divine figure but apparently neither Enlil nor any other deities are
101 Jacobsen 1976, p. 21. 102 Cf. Jacobsen 1976, p. 87, and Jacobsen 1970, pp. 140–141, the latter is a reproduction of “Early Political Development in Mesopotamia” published in 1957. 103 Jacobsen 1976, p. 4. 104 Jacobsen 1976, p. 5.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
30
individual “metaphors” in his terminology. The “nature” of Enlil, one of the primary “ruler gods” in the second phase, however remained in an unexplained way that of “Lord Wind”, which is mystically “primarily the power in the moist winds of spring – growing weather”.105
0.3.2 The Historian and the Sumerian Gods
Jacobsen’s last speech from the perspective of the practicing historian, not “the historian of religion”, is more illustrative of the author’s more theoretical reflections on his approach to “Mesopotamian religion”. 106 In his presidential address delivered to the American Oriental Society, which was posthumously published, Jacobsen started his reflections with a quote from Collingwood, who had discussed “The Cone of Entemena” in The Idea of History.107 For Collingwood, the cone of Entemena,
“expresses a form of thought which no modern historian would call history, because, in the first place, it lacks the character of science: it is not an attempt to answer a question of whose answer the writer begins by being ignorant; it is merely a record of something the writer knows for a fact; and in the second place the fact recorded is not certain actions on the part of the human beings, it is certain actions on the part of gods . . .. The knowledge furthered by such a record is not, or at any rate is not primarily, man’s knowledge of man, but man’s knowledge of the gods.”108
105 Jacobsen 1976, pp. 98–99. 106 Jacobsen gave his presidential address on the 20th of April, 1993, at the 203rd annual meeting of the American Oriental Society, and passed away on the 2nd of May, 1993. The manuscript was prepared by Sasson for publication as Jacobsen 1994 together with Abusch’s introduction of Jacobsen at the meeting before the speech was given. 107 Collingwood 1967, pp. 10–13. 108 Collingwood 1967, pp. 11–12.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 31
For Collingwood, the only way in which “a modern historian with his eye fixed on human res gestae” can use the inscription for historical purposes would be that, to interpret the text “as evidence concerning actions done by Mesilim and Ush and their subjects.”109 Jacobsen noticed that Collingwood carefully avoids any suggestion that the historian should simply ignore all references to gods and their doings in Entemena’s account. He went further to point out that the more important problem is, even if only considering Me-salim checking a measurement and erecting the stele, where and why he did that would be simply lost. The action will be isolated and unmotivated while no history can be based on such disjointed fragments. And indeed the references to gods and their actions in texts as such cannot be simply ignored since “the gods as supernatural forces have no place in history, the ancients’ beliefs about them is a purely human matter and so a historical datum to be dealt with as such.”110 The commitment of the ancients to their gods was so deep that the historian can be fairly confident in accepting that the ancients believed certain things about their gods and acted on these beliefs in a “theocratic mode of experiencing”, in which the sky above was the god An, the wind Enlil and so on.111 In the cone of Entemena the original decision to fix the border between Lagaš and Umma under Me-salim was recorded as having been made by Enlil, while Me-salim was credited only with the subsidiary task of checking the measurement. The inscription would be a testimony to the legitimacy of the Me-salim border and to the divine sanctions protecting it, which would discourage enemies and enhance the morale of defenders of the border as fighting a “just war” with the gods on their side, for whom the enemy’s actions would be not merely aggression but also sacrilege.112
109 Collingwood 1967, p. 12. 110 Jacobsen 1994, p. 147. 111 Jacobsen 1994, p. 147. 112 Jacobsen 1994, pp. 149–150.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
32
“The historian should not ignore all references to gods and their activities in his sources. Rather, he should accept the historical fact that the Sumerians had such and such beliefs and should note how their beliefs may have shaped their actions in the events with which he deals. He should not try to become an ancient Sumerian in the mind or rethink the Sumerians’ thoughts, since he could not do so without sharing their beliefs that events were indeed caused by gods.”113
In the “theocratic mode of experiencing” actions would be never undertaken without prior consultation with the gods by dreams or omens and were not carried out unless the omina were propitious. This means, there will be a kind of arbitrariness and irrationality in the decision-making process of the ancients. But Jacobsen thinks that there is not unlikely some chance that the ancients were not arbitrary or incompatible with the rationality of the historian, or at least the historian may have a chance to suppose a certain degree of appreciation instead of being completely paralysed. The historian, for instance, may suppose the ancient divination to be in a yes or no frame. Therefore, the degree of arbitrariness can be limited to some extent or be retraceable, and there is the possibility that the ancients repeated their inquiry in divination until a favourable answer was obtained. They could thus have been unconsciously following a practical logic that overran their arbitrary mode of action. The ancients could also have manipulated the “margin for interpretation” which may be potentially suitable to the historian’s rational chains of causality. But even if all is said and done, “the fact of a deep-seated, uneasy, wondering remains.”114 Although the historian may use studies of ancient omens or dreams to overcome the arbitrariness inherent in the Sumerian process of decision-making, facing the “deep-seated, uneasy, wondering” that he finally has to admit, Jacobsen leaned back again on poetic imagination,
113 Jacobsen 1994, p. 150. 114 Jacobsen 1994, p. 150.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 33
completing his speech with a recitation of the Lament of Ur:115
“Enlil’s storm tells what happened in theocratic language, and for the ancient that meant more profound truth than the language of immediate experience would be capable of, much as for us scientific language states a deeper truth, even though that truth is not immediately experienced. That a pencil, for instance, is a collection of atoms and molecules, or that, when the sun rises, it doesn’t: it is rather the earth turning on it axis. On the level of immediate experience, Enlil’s storm amounted to a devastating attack by barbarians from the mountains.”116
I quote Jacobsen here verbatim to show how Enlil was appreciated in Jacobsen’s historical imagination expressed in an idiosyncratic style. But if taking Jacobsen’s particular interest in Enlil into consideration, it is surprising that, in his reflections on the historian and the Sumerian gods, Jacobsen does not mention the fact that the name of Enlil and those of Šara and Ningirsu were not interchangeable in the text of the cone of Entemena. The individuality of the deities cannot be ignored since apparently the position where the name of Enlil appears in the passage quoted above cannot be filled in by the name of Šara or Ningirsu. The “theocratic mode of experiencing” has yet to explain the distinctions among the numerous deities within it only if the historian wishes to go further than a general statement of the “mode” itself and to take more seriously the appearance of deities in texts like the cone of Entemena.
0.3.3 The Indices to the Prototype Agent
Jacobsen’s charaterization of the Sumerian “theocratic mode of
115 Note, Jacobsen actually began his speech from the absurdity that students of Sumerian history encounter when having to deal with the Sumerian gods because, simply, history “by definition, excludes any supernatural agencies.” See Jacobsen 1994, p. 146. 116 Jacobsen 1994, p. 152.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
34
experiencing” in itself does not necessarily preclude the existence of a highly differentiated pantheon. The fact that the individuality of deities was left unaddressed in Jacobsen’s speech was at least partially because, a certain kind of realist doctrine was imposed onto history. The exclusion of the supernatural from history is on the philosophical ground that supernatural beings are of another “nature” which prescribes that the vocabulary for their description is theological. This assumption works well when there is only “one” supernatural being which does not appear in materials treated in modern history. And when it does appear, the text can be left for the theologian or decoded in terms of a singular “mode of experiencing”. But if there are many such beings which then frequently appear in the sources the modern historian has to deal with, the distinction made of “nature” does not work because there would be too many different subsets of that “mode”. In this sense, Jacobsen’s failure to notice in his speech that “the” Sumerian mode of experiencing has also to explain the apparent different roles of Enlil, Šara, and Ningirsu as against one another, thus losing the opprotunity of having more historical information, is actually concomitant with his failure in defining “deities” in his “history of Mesopotamian religion”. Although Jacobsen’s “history of Mesopotamian religion” or his general characterization of the Sumerian “theocratic mode of experiencing” are still valuable without going beyond the realist doctrine of “nature”, the history of the individual deity Enlil that stands in its own right will have to redefine the different “nature” of the deity at first. Jacobsen has rightly stressed: “Irrespective of what the Sumerians believed the fact that they so believed makes it proper historical material which can command the historian’s full attention.”117 The problem lies in the fact that the ancients believed was that dEn-líl was a particular agent and not “the” all-in-one single agent. I believe this observation is the starting point for redefining Enlil in order to write a history of it. The fact that the deity was however regarded as an agent, in my opinion, will justify us to relate it in social-relational contexts taking use of the
117 Jacobsen 1994, p. 149.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 35
conceptual framework Alfred Gell developed as his “art-nexus”.118 In Fig. 0.1 below drawn after Alfred Gell’s conceptual explication, “A” stands for Artist, “P” for Prototype, “R” for Recipient and “X” for Index. Index mediates agency but is at the same time capable of motivating inferences, responses or interpretations in a variety of relations to Prototypes, Artists, and Recipients. The Prototypes are things that the indices may represent or stand for. The Recipients are those that the Indices captivate or are those that can only be effective via the Index. The Artists are immediately and causally responsible for the existence and the characteristics of the Index. The Agent-Patient relationships are expressed by arrows in the Figure. The self-referential Agent-Patient relationship each participant has is expressed by the two arrows pointing in opposite directions on the circle enclosing it.
Fig. 0.1: The Prototype-Agent and the Material Index
In Gell’s terminology, the Index as material entity is “secondary agent”, the Artists and Recipients as human beings are “primary agents”, and the Prototypes can be either “secondary agents” or “primary agents” depending on contexts.119 The fact that the deity is not a material entity
118 Gell 1998, p. 28. The concept of agency has attracted considerable attention in recent archaeological theorization, for instance, contributions in Dobres and Robb 2000 and Osborne and Tanner 2007. 119 Gell 1998, pp. 28–29, and Ibid., Table I. Note, Ibid., p. 5: “a purely cultural, aesthetic, ‘appreciative’ approach to art objects is an anthropological dead end.”



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
36
will exclude it from being the Index while the fact that it is not human will exclude it from being the Artist or the Recipient, leaving Prototype as the best choice.120 In our context, the available primary sources need to be understood as the material Indices that represent or stand for the Prototype Enlil. The Recipients are the people who were passively related to these material Indices like the audience of a particular group of royal inscriptions. The Artists are the ancients immediately and causally responsible for the existence and the characteristics of the primary sources which represent or stand for the Prototype. It must be stressed that in this paradigm, the relationships between the Artist and the Recipient, between the Artist and the Prototype, as well as between the Prototype and the Recipient are impossible to know unless material Indices are available.121 The historical study of deities, based on the above definition of the “deity” as the Prototype in the above paradigm, would therefore be explained as the diachronic study of the social relations between the Prototype and the Artists and between the Prototype and the Recipients, effectively also those between the Artitsts and the Recipients, provided that they are knowable from the available material Indices. It is then clear that, “The History of Deities” is not primarily concerned with the theological views the ancient Artists and Recipients could have of the Prototype, which are about faith and need to be studied otherwise, but with the practical relations between the humans and the Prototype, simultaneously also those between the Artists and Recipients. In order that the definition of “The History of Deities” can be applied in research practice, nevertheless, it is necessary to clarify some consequences of it with the help of Latour’s recent theorization. Latour recently formulated an Actor-Network Theory (ANT), in which the
120 It may be relevant to note that, Attinger 1993, p. 150, divided Sumerian substantives to be of personal and non-personal only, as did also Edzard 2003b, p. 29. The perspective elaborated here, from which we see them, is thus openly at odds with that of the Sumerians, for whom the deities were however “personal”. 121 Gell 1998, pp. 13–14, and p. 36.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 37
“sociology of associations” is set against the “sociology of the social” characteristic of most of the schools of sociological thinking in the last two centuries. Latour held that social networks cannot be defined in any prescriptive way since that will inevitably characterize the “social” as “matters of facts” which is utterly wrong.122 “Reassembling the Social”, meaning “reassembling social relations”, is the only task the student has to be concerned with in all forms of social research for which “frameworks” can only do harm. Latour warned that accounts following the ANT approach will be risky. But he refused to spell out how to use ANT in practice, to the student’s despair.123 In “The History of Deities”, there are also no “frameworks” of any kind, and such a history, as the end product based on the “reassembling social relations” approach will also be inevitably risky. The lack of analytical concepts in Latour’s theorization, not those prescriptive labels he rightly discarded right away but that which can provide a different way of “understanding” primary sources, however, makes Latour’s ANT more a theoretical statement than a methodological innovation. In my opinion, Gell’s “art-nexus” provides the lacking tools of ANT, while the general theorization by Latour makes clear the consequences of seeing primary sources as Gell’s Indices.124 The “reassembling” of the ancient “social relations” has to rely on the interpretation of the Indices so that “The History of Deities” will still have much to be concerned with “collecting materials and performing limited concrete tasks.”125
122 Latour 2005, pp. 129–131. Latour’s reflections may echo those of Mann 1986 where Mann was ready to abandon the word “society” altogether. Schroeder 2006 stressed that Mann does not presume “society” as any concrete substance. 123 Latour 2005, pp. 141–158, presents a drama between the professor and a student of social sciences who is only disappointed by the impracticality of ANT. 124 I will try to explicate elsewhere that Latour’s Actor-Network Theory is philosophically compatible with the “Anthropological Theory of Art” presented in Gell 1998 and the lacking analytical concepts in Latour’s ANT may actually be the latter’s “art-nexus”. 125 Cf. Michalowski 1998, p. 237.



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT
38
At the end of this introduction that first of all aims at providing a research history of Enlil, based on which I attempted to delineate a methodology particularly suitable for writing a history of Enlil, some further clarifications are needed. It is clear from the history of research that the focus has always been on the particular deity Enlil in the early periods of Mesopotamia but not on early Mesopotamian “religion” in general. There would be many more important works that need to be taken into consideration if a critical research history of all the studies of early Mesopotamian “religion” is anticipated. It would perhaps need more than a monograph in its own right if given the involvedness of the term “religion”. All parts of the present work, therefore, including the research history and the methodology, are meant to serve only the study of Enlil the subject matter. The methodology I attempted to delineate, although it is daringly termed “The History of Deities” approach instead of an awkward “The History of Enlil” one, has also to be seen in the same light. The fact that the methodological criticisms that have led to the “construction” of the methodology are only on the previous studies of Enlil is sufficient to explain that the methodology is only meant to serve the present study of the particular deity. Although I believe it may be applicable to the historical study of other Mesopotamian deities as well, there is no claim that no similar efforts in terms of methodology have already been made by scholars who did not choose Enlil as their subject matter. The methodological representativeness of Jacobsen’s presidential address and of his overview of the “history of Mesopotamian religion” needs to be seen in the same context. This is to say, although Jacobsen’s reflections are taken to serve an in-depth review of the current status of methodological explication for the historical study of Enlil, there is no presumption that scholars of Mesopotamian “religion”, especially of Mesopotamian deities, have all been following Jacobsen’s line of thinking. I noted above in the research history that even for the study of Enlil other scholars have indeed made their efforts to develop their own methodologies. The attempt made here is but one step further, I hope so,



INTRODUCTION TO THE ENLIL PROJECT 39
to make methodological assumptions clearer when embarking on a more systematic study of the same subject matter following a historical approach. In this sense, it is more than a happy coincidence that recent studies of other Mesopotamian deities have often been in the same spirit as that of “The History of Deities”.126
126 Schwemer 2001 and Feliu 2003 are examples of recent monographic size diachronic works on invididual Mesopotamian deities. Both works have sophisticated methodological awareness when reaching historical conclusions based on careful analysis of prmiary sources. Schwemer 2001 is much more comprehendsive than Feliu 2003 or the attempt here for Enlil.






Part I: EN.KID in the Archaic Texts
The time from the Late Uruk Period to the Early Dynastic I Period in Southern Babylonia is considered “archaic” in the development of cuneiform writing, which is perhaps the earliest writing in the world. Warka (Uruk) is hitherto known as the site that produced the earliest corpus of cuneiform writing datable to the Uruk IV Period. It can be assumed that writing was widely used for administrative recording across southern Mesopotamia in the Uruk III Period, to which date written tablets were found from as far as Tell Asmar in the Diyala.127 In northern Mesopotamia numerical tablets are known. For example, from Tell Brak numerical labels were excavated in secure Late Uruk contexts, but the practice of writing seems to have been not as well developed in the north as in the south, nor was it deeply rooted.128 The archaic cuneiform script is not yet fully deciphered but it is difficult to imagine that the language in which the writing system was first applied could be much different from Sumerian.129 In “classical” Sumerian AN.EN.LÍL was the writing for the name dEn-líl “the god Enlil” and EN.LÍL.KI was Nibruki “the city Nippur”. In the Late Uruk Period, it can be certain that the use of determinatives, if there was such practice already, was at least not consist and predictable.130
127 Englund 1998, p. 31. 128 Quenet 2005, p. 31. 129 Van Soldt, Kalvelagen and Katz 2005 included several recent contributions on the problem, among which see especially Wilcke 2005. 130 Edzard 2003b, pp. 9–10. Note, however, Westenholz 2002, p. 37: “Nippur was written EN.LÍL.KI at least from 3100 B.C. onwards, literally ‘Enlil place’.” In the archaic corpus from the Late Uruk Period, I did not see anywhere NIBRU, if we

I will draw on the later tradition to refer to the subject matter of this study as ENLIL/NIBRU but importantly I have no assumption particularly in this part that the later tradition went unchanged back to the Late Uruk Period. In fact, nothing is presumed before the following re-examination of the sources.
1.1 EN.KID in Uruk III Sources
ENLIL/NIBRU has been related to the Late Uruk corpus in three contexts. The first is the Archaic City List known from Uruk (W21126). ZATU gave also W20921 but the line in question is most likely to be analysed as two separate logograms rather than a single word.131 The second is EN:É:TI from Jemdet Nasr, Uruk, Umma, and probably Tell Uqair (IM 45975, MSVO 1 196, MSVO 1 212, MSVO 1 213, MSVO 3 030, MSVO 4 013, MSVO 4 036, MS 2500).132 EN:É:TI known from Jemdet Nasr texts had been analysed in the twenties of the last century as a personal name in which EN:É stands for En-líl and TI for -til, so to mean “may Enlil give life”.133 The third context is the Archaic City Seal Impression reconstructed from the seal impressions made on 12 tablets from Jemdet Nasr and probably one from Tell Uqair, all Uruk III in dating (MSVO 1 161, MSVO 1 163, MSVO 1 166, MSVO 1 167, MSVO 1 168, MSVO 1 169, MSVO 1 170, MSVO 1 172, MSVO 1 173, MSVO 1 174, MSVO 1 176, MSVO 1 178, MSVO 1 180, ATU 1 656). Also in the category of seal impressions, NIBRU has been considered recognizable from a sealing excavated from Uruk (W11456).134

1.1.1 ENx+KIDx?!.NUNx? on the Archaic City Seal
The Archaic City Seal refers specifically to the undiscovered seal that produced the impression reconstructed from the twelve tablets from Jemdet Nasr and one from Tell Uqair. The unknown original Seal was likely to have a convex girth with a diameter of 4.93cm, therefore of considerable size. The original seal cutter must have cut the signs to have the correct orientation and sequence on the Seal face so that when it was rolled across the tablet surfaces a mirror image of the scene would be produced (Fig. 1.1.1, Fig. 1.1.2). This practice of sealing is in clear contrast to that of later times when seal inscriptions were generally written in reverse thus producing a true image when rolled.136 The contents of the texts inscribed on the tablets sealed with the Archaic City Seal all fall into a very distinctive category of fruit and textile distribution although tablets of similar contents are not always
Tell Uqair. The Archaic City Seal Impression was presented as Matthews 1993, Fig. 10a, and the Uruk City Sealing W11456 as Fig. 10b. 135 Vajman 1990, pp. 114–115, argued that the sign combination EN E2a TI should be read either as E2a EN.TI, “house of the god Enti,” or as E2a EBIḪ, “house of Ebiḫ”, the hypothetical divine figure Enti being perhaps “lord bow or/and arrow” or “lord life”. Glassner 2003 argued from the polyvalence of É, “house”=KID “reed mat”=LÍL “breath”, to speculate on the role of Enlil (Lord LÍL) in cosmogony, but see George 2005, p. 108, especially concerning the perceived “personal name” EN:É:TI. 136 The definition and description of the Archaic City Seal here follow those in Matthews 1993, p. 34, where the author pointed out that the true images on the original seal will confirm that the original orientation of cuneiform writing have been turned 90 degrees in a later time. The format of the inscriptions on the City Sealed tablets is distinctive in that they always commence with a short list of sexagesimally counted objects, including probably dried fruits and grape products. The inscriptions commonly concluded with a series of sign combinations not connected with numbers, most typically in the form of NUNa TAK4a KIa NIa+RU 3N57 MUŠ3a UNUGa.137 Matthews commenced reading the Archaic City Seal according to the sequence of the beginning of the City List as we know from later sources (W21126, SF 023).138 The sign combination URI3a.ABa on the Archaic City Seal would stand for the city name of Ur, following which the second entry UDa.ABa for Larsa, the fourth entry UNUGx for Uruk, the sixth entry MUŠ3a.ABa for Zabala, and the fifth entry pictographically resembles the name of Keš in cuneiform writing.139 The third entry that comes after Larsa in Matthews’ description consists of “two difficult signs, the first of which appears to be an archaic form of ENa (very close to ENc) followed by a part of a NUNa sign.”140 Based “primarily on the sign sequences attested in the lexical list of city names”, Matthews interpreted the sign combination as ENa/ENc.NUNa and indentified it as the precursor of EN.LÍL or EN.KID which was the name of NIBRU later, mentioning that “in the Fāra Period UD.GAL.NUN

In Steinkeller’s view, even if the sign combination was EN.NUN, there is no reason to suppose that NIBRU was meant since there is no evidence that the UD.GAL.NUN orthography, exemplars of which are only known from the ED IIIa Period, was already known in Uruk III times. The sign combination EN.NUN in the archaic corpus was common but nowhere can it be interpreted as a geographical name.142 According to Steinkeller, then, the sign combination cannot be analyzed as NIBRU at all. In order to re-examine the interpretations by Matthews and by Steinkeller of the sign combination on the Archaic City Seal, I present below Matthews’ drawing of the seal impression reconstructed from the tablets along with that by Amiet, both to be set in contrast with the proto-cuneiform signs, ENa, ENc and NUNa. As can be seen in Fig.s 1.1.1-3 below, the sign Matthews read as NUNa does not seem to me a big problem or at least may plausibly be considered as NUNx?. The first part of the sign combination, however, that was read by both Matthews and Steinkeller as EN, seems difficult to match any of the archaic ENa or ENc signs since apparently the lower part of it is too thick to be compressed into the correct ratio, a fact cannot be ignored (Fig. 1.1.3).143 I prefer to divide the first ideograph in the combination into ENx+KIDx?!, which is then in combination with NUNx?. Although no clear traces of the limitations of each sign are discernible, the first ideograph can be divided with no problem, at least in terms of proportion. The upper part is close to the achaic sign ENc and the lower part a square in which a KID will take form. The sign combination on the Arhcaic City Seal, therefore, can be analysed as ENx+KIDx?!.NUNx?, which I believe was the earliest known

1.1.2 ENa.KID in the Uruk City Sealing
Fig. 1.2: ENa.KID in the Uruk City Sealing (W11456)
In addition to the Archaic City Seal, a sealing from Uruk, perhaps slightly later than Uruk III in dating, bears the sign ENa over a certain KID in line with U4.ABa standing probably for Larsa, UNUG probably for Uruk, and DÙG probably for Keš (W11456). W11456 was excavated from rubbish layers dated to Uruk III, and was therefore later than the original layer into which the rubbish was dumped.146 The style of the sealing resembles some of the ED I Ur city sealings to be discussed later, but clearly the division of boxes containing individual names was not yet practiced consistently in the sealing of W11456, and the forms of signs were thicker and stouter in proportion if compared with the ED I Ur sealings, suggesting a somewhat earlier dating than the latter (Fig. 1.2). I agree with Matthews to read ENa.KID in W11456 for NIBRU.147

In the 1970s, the broken second sign of the sign combination was restored as E2a ( ) based on an excavation photograph but collation of the fragment W21126 in the Iraq Museum later led Englund to restore the sign as KIDa ( ), therefore resulting in the reading ENa.KIDa ( ) for NIBRU.148 It is impossible to check the original fragment at the moment but from the photograph it is relatively certain that the ancient scribe had made four horizontal and longer lines crossing three vertical shorter ones to make a quadrangle. The lines are evenly distributed so as to divide the quadrangle into six smaller parts with the same geometrical features of the sign as a whole.

1.1.4 ENa.KIDa in Uruk III Administrative Texts
If the second entry on the Uruk III fragment W21126 can be restored as ENa.KIDa as a geographical designation, a renewed examination of the attestation of NIBRU in the archaic corpus becomes possible. In earlier studies the search for the attestation of ENLIL/NIBRU in the archaic administrative corpus had been undertaken upon the assumption that the name was written as ENa.E2a in W21126, and that it was identical with the writing of the city name in the ED IIIa version of the city list known from Fāra where it is more securely readable as ENa.E2a(SF 023).151 The semiological problems are clear here for restoring earlier sign forms if only later exemplars are known, since both the semantics of the signs and the visual features were liable to have been transformed in the course of time. I believe this was the case with ENa.KIDa. The following is a list of the few texts in which ENa.KIDa is attested, as can be gleaned from the archaic corpus:

The sign combination ENa.KIDa was almost exclusively attested in the distinctive group of Jemdet Nasr texts dated to the Uruk III Period, and all of which were records of the highest level of accounting involving several years of time and large amounts of goods (MSVO 1 094, MSVO 1 095, MSVO 1 107, MSVO 1 115, MSVO 1 185).152 W15772,r from Uruk III Warka is too broken to provide further information except for the fact that the fragment was from the southern site. It may be observed, however, that the sign combination was mostly combined with signs presumably designating officials, such as GALa (W15772,r), ENGIZ (MSVO 1 095), ENa (MSVO 1 107), and SANGAa (MSVO 1 185), suggesting that ENa.KIDa might have been a designation of some institution especially related to long period accounting and was associated with a variety of
152 The tablets are all in the form of Englund 1998, p. 59, Fig. 17.6–7. Monaco 2004 concluded that MSVO 4 094 accounted for a period as long as eight years.



PART I: EN.KID IN THE ARCHAIC TEXTS
52
officials during the Uruk III Period.153 This observation of the above texts can accommodate both the idea that EN.KID denoted a geographical location, which is possibly the city of NIBRU, and that of a divine name that is ENLIL as we would like to have. But no less significantly, there is no sign such as the appearance of divine determinative to confirm the “divinity” of the referent.
1.2 EN.KID in Ur SIS IV-VIII Sealings
The Ur Seal Impression Strata levels IV-VIII, stratigraphically earlier than the well known Royal Cemetery, which may be dated to the Early Dynastic III Period, produced the only major group of texts available from the time after the Jemdet Nasr/Uruk III Period and before the Fāra Period.154 There is no attestation of either ENLIL or NIBRU in the textual corpus from Early Dynastic I Ur in so far as the archaic writing EN.KID is concerned. The clay sealings that gave the archaeological levels the term Seal Impression Strata, nevertheless, bear seal impressions considered to contain the name of NIBRU, thus played a significant role in the study of early Mesopotamian political development.155 In my perception, the city name of NIBRU is securely attested on the sealing MSVO 2 U001 (Fig. 1.4), and there are other attestations of sign combinations that may plausibly be read as also the name of the same
153 The officials can be recognized in archaic Lú A. Cf. Englund 1998, p. 104. 154 UET 2 015–273 were from SIS level IV. UET 2 001, UET 2 002, UET 2 308 and UET 2 310 were from trial trenches dug before the SIS system was determined. UET 2 305 and UET 2 306 were from SIS level VII, UET 2 307 level VIII, UET 2 232 and UET 2 233 pit G dug at the northern corner of the site cutting across SIS level VI to SIS level VIII. Burrows 1935, p. 1, dated UET 2 001, UET 2 063, UET 2 064, UET 2 232, UET 2 233 to the Jemdet Nasr Period, UET 2 002, UET 2 308, UET 2 310 the Fāra Period, and the rest in between. 155 These Ur city seal impressions, especially MSVO 2 U001, were the oldest evidence based on which Jacobsen hypothesized his influential “Kiengir League”. See now Jacobsen 1970, pp. 132–156.

city, though in somewhat varied forms.156
1.2.1 UD.EN.KID in SIS Ur City Sealing
Fig. 1.4: UD.EN.KID in the Ur SIS City Sealing (MSVO 2 U001)
The seal impression MSVO 2 U001, 6.8×5.2×4.9 cm2 in size, is known from two rollings from SIS level IV. The reverse faces of the sealings were both broken away but traces of striation can be recognized on one of the bases as perhaps being from wood. The seal impression recognized from the two rollings is fortunately preserved in its entirety. The symbols on U001 are arranged in two registers with horizontal borders dividing them. The upper register of the seal impression contains in the middle three signs that seem to form an entity and are readable as UD.EN.KID, on both sides of which there are signs supposedly belonging to an unidentifiable compound. 157 The lower register contains sign combinations readable as city names, including in sequence AN.DÙG+DÙ probably for Keš, ÚR+AN for an unrecognized locality, ÙRI+AB probably for Ur, UD+NUN probably for Adab, and UD.AB probably for Larsa.158
156 The city sealing impressions were redrawn as Matthews 1993, Fig. 12–25. MSVO 2 U001 refers to that of Matthews 1993, Fig. 12–25 for Impression 1. For identifications of city names on U001-U023 see Matthews 1993, p. 41, Table 5. 157 Matthews 1993, p. 61, read the unidentified sign compound as edinnu?. Legrain 1939, p. 39, interpreted the edinnu motif as to represent the fertile plain. 158 In addition, MSVO 2 U114 perhaps contains a reference to ENx.KIDx.NUNx, which was the first entry of the lower register in Matthews’ illustration. The



PART I: EN.KID IN THE ARCHAIC TEXTS
54
In total, 124 Ur SIS clay sealings, when sufficient information is preserved, have been subjected to functional analysis by Matthews. 85 such sealings were recognized as door sealings, and 39 container sealings. The most common of the latter category is that of pot sealing, the second reed matting package sealing, the rarest basket sealing attested only in a single case.159 The sealing MSVO 2 U001, based on the striation traces recognized on its base, was considered by Matthews to be probably used for a package or box whose opening was perhaps made of wooden or other perishable materials.160 If this was the case, the seal U001 would have been fairly unique in its use. If it was used for door sealing, traces of pegs would be preserved on the reverse of the sealing but U001 does not seem to belong to this category. Similarly on the grounds of the traces on the reverse, the sealing does not fit into either the category of pot sealing, reed matting package sealing or basket sealing.
1.2.2 ANx?.KID in SIS Ur City Sealing
The seal impression MSVO 2 U016, 7.1×4.3×3.9 cm2 in size, is known from two rollings from SIS level IV but could also be from level VIII, which would date the impression later than U001. The function of the sealing can be securely recognized as reed matting packaging since on the reverse faces of the rollings, traces of either fibre string or traces of split-reed matting are recognizable. The seal impression cannot be fully restored but the original seal is likely to have two registers with sign compounds enclosed in boxes at least for the upper register. If read from the upper left, the first entry contains an image like a two-legged stout cartoon figure with three equal-sized up-facing limbs representing hands and head, upon a quadrangular object perhaps to be read as KID. The

Matthews read both the miniature form of U016 and the star-like form of U002 above KID as ANx? and interpreted both of the combinations as NIBRU, presumably on the ground that the other signs on the sealings, although not readily recognizable, do resemble city names. I am not convinced by Matthews’ reading the signs as ANx?, unless this reading is only meant to express the fact that the form of the sign somehow resembles that of the DINGIR sign as we know. But Matthews is perhaps right that both combinations referred here to NIBRU the city. The miniature form of U016 and the star-like form of U002 above KID may have unknown origins other than AN written with the DINGIR sign, in my perception, though they may have merged into DINGIR in the development of cuneiform writing.164 If the ANx?.KIDs in U016 and U002

1.3 The Pronunciation of ENa+KIDa in the Archaic Texts
The Archaic City List, the Archaic City Seal with its new reading, the Uruk city sealing W11456, and the Ur city seal impression MSVO 2 U001 provide the basis for making several observations, first of all the archaic writing of the name NIBRU. In the Uruk III Period and even earlier, we may risk saying, NIBRU was represented by ENx+KIDx.NUNx? as engraved on the Archaic City Seal.166 The core of this early representation, EN.KID, was transformed into cuneiform writing for the name of the city in the Archaic City List and in the slightly later city sealing W11456. EN.KID in Uruk III administrative texts perhaps also referred to NIBRU the city but was difficult to be interpreted as the name of the deity ENLIL since no evidence supports the “divinity” of it. In the Early Dynastic I Period, the name of NIBRU would be UD.EN.KID but if taking the less certain MSVO 2 U002 and MSVO 2 016 as both containing NIBRU, the graphic form UD.EN.KID would be exchangeable by that time with ANx?.KID in which for some reason ANx? was replaceable with UD. The sign understood as KID in the Uruk city sealing W11456 represented a form similar to KIDb ( ) though not precisely identical. In the
IV. U003 is known from eight rollings, where identifiable, of door pegs. 165 EN.KID in the Late Uruk Period was indivisible. If only considering the Ur SIS exemplars, it is equally possible to regard ANx? as alternating with UD.EN. 166 In addition, the scene on Porada 1993, p. 566, Fig. 8, on which a king-like figure on a boat in between a reed-box like object and an EN object on the back of a bull, graphically resembles the representation of NIBRU on the Archaic City Seal, ENx+KIDx.NUNx?. Porada 1993, p. 564, considered the scene certainly a narrative ritual scene but called the ruler-figure instead of the object on the back of the animal en, which would be misplaced perhaps due to a kind of anachronism. The better term to name the ruler figure must be nám-ešda or numušda, the first entry of the archaic Lú A discussed in Wilcke 2003, p. 19.



PART I: EN.KID IN THE ARCHAIC TEXTS 57
city seal impressions from Ur SIS levels, the forms in question were in all cases more or less RÉC 425 = KID, which resembles more KIDb rather than KIDa. KIDa may now be introduced into Jacobsen’s orthographic table to stand as the precursor of RÉC 423 = É in the name of Enlil.167 Englund more recently suggested that KID alone perhaps did not have the value /lil/ until the Ur III Period since he found no direct evidence for that value. At the same time, he understood the combination ENa.KIDa as a rendering of a substrate Ellil/Illil from which both the Sumerian ENLIL and the Akkadian ellilu or ellilūtu were derived.168 In this line of thinking, the conclusion would be that, ENa+KIDa or ENx+KIDx was originally an indivisible compound that reads /ellil/ or /illil/. In later traditions NIBRU the city name and ENLIL the divine name took the same form except for the determinative DINGIR or the postdeterminative KI, but as the transcriptions already show, the reading of the toponym is different from that of the divine name. The consequence of Englund’s phonetics of ENa+KIDa or ENx+KIDx would be that, it is only possible to think that for some reason when the toponym of Niffar was rendered in writing, it took the writing of the divine name which was read /ellil/ or /illil/ from time immemorial, so that EN.KID could obtain the value /nibru/, by which the place was known in the vernacular. The question is related to the prehistory of the site of Niffar which was occupied as early as the Hajji Muhammad Period but unfortunately we do not know the reading of the name of the site before writing can give any hint, since simply the evidence is lost.169 The attestations of EN.KID in the sources re-examined above, however, are on all occasions interpretable as a toponym while hardly anywhere could it be taken as a
167 Jacobsen 1989, p. 267. Cf. Englund 1998, pp. 75–76, n. 151. 168 Englund 1998, pp. 75–76, n. 151. 169 Gibson 1976 considered that the foundation of Nippur as a city was however probably around 3300 B.C., due to the drying up of the eastern branch of the Euphrates. In the Late Uruk Period the northern part of the Eastern Mound of Niffar and the eastern part of the Western Mound were both occupied, according to Gibson 1992, pp. 36-38.



PART I: EN.KID IN THE ARCHAIC TEXTS
58
divine name. I would disagree with Englund since EN.KID as the representtation of the city NIBRU was more likely originally read in a way close to /nibru/, the reading of the city name, rather than to /ellil/ or /illil/, the reading of the divine name. The writing of the city name obtained the reading /ellil/ or /illil/ sometime later when the “divinity” known as ENLIL was associated with the city as the result of the “deification” of NIBRU, as like “labelled”, by the divinity. The difficult orthographical variations of the writing of the name NIBRU as seen from the archaic Ur sources suggest that the evolvement of the tradition concerning ENLIL and NIBRU was a part of a much more complex historical process. The fact that both the format and the content of the Ur SIS sealings were full of discrepancies, on the other hand, betrays that they were remnants of a time of rapid change not long after the beginning of the process. I am inclined to put the time in which EN.KID obtained the reading similar to /ellil/ or /illil/ in consequence to the “deification” of NIBRU in the transitional period from the Late Uruk to the Early Dynastic Period. From the viewpoint of this interpretation, Englund’s reading /ellil/ or /illil/ for the original pronunciation of ENLIL, from which both the Sumerian and the Akkadian spellings might have been derived later, is the equivalent of Edzard’s Sumerian *Yēn/Yīn-lil put in a multi-cultural context.170 But there are questions against this re-interpretation of the original pronunciation of ENa+KIDa. As understood by Edzard, /en/ in the writing of the name ENLIL is often taken as a phonetic indicator. The above re-interpretation of the pronounciation of ENa+KIDa will also have to explain how an originally different phonetic realization /nibru/ happened to have such an indicator that agrees well with this part of the writing of the name of ENLIL in later times. For this question, it needs to be pointed out that, Englund’s reading /ellil/ or /illil/ for the original pronounciation of EN.KID was proposed without support from any reconstruction of the phonetics of the supposed substrate language and Edzard’s reconstruction of the pronunciation of *Yēn/Yīn-lil was not
170 Edzard 2003a, pp. 177–178.



PART I: EN.KID IN THE ARCHAIC TEXTS 59
meant to be valid before the time of his earliest sources. The pronunciation of the sign combination EN.KID at the time of the Jemdet Nasr sources is effectively an unanswered question and the only legitimate method of resolving the problem is to base on contemporary sources from the earliest time. The fact that EN in the divine name might be a phonetic indicator can be explained through a historical retrospect into the course of time. In this scenario, when /nibru/ was associated with the divinity that we know as ENLIL, the writing of the city name EN.KID and the original pronounciation of the name of the divinity that we do not know exactly, both conbributed to the reading *Yēn/Yīn-lil through learned interpretation of sign forms. The orginal divine name contributed the /lil/ part while the *Yēn/Yīn part was at least partially a learned interpretation of the EN sign.171
171 I put above the time when EN.KID obtained the reading similar to /ellil/ or /illil/ in consequence to the “deification” of NIBRU by ENLIL in the transitional period from the Late Uruk to the Early Dynastic Period and considered Englund’s reading /ellil/ or /illil/ the equivalent of Edzard’s Sumerian *Yēn/Yīn-lil in a multi-cultural context. The origin of the divinity that was “imposed” onto NIBRU cannot be determined unless the pronounciation of the divine name prior to the event can be established. It might be Semitic if combining the arguments of Michalowski 1998, p. 242, n. 2, and of Edzard 2003a, pp. 176–177, both quoted earlier in the Introduction. I will return to the question later but will not provide final solution to it, since Enlil has anyway to be seen in a multi-ethnic context.






Part II: Enlil in the Fāra Period
The temporal period included in this chapter could be unduly long since it will have to encompass perhaps more than three centuries, ca. 2800 to ca. 2450, while the first substantial groups of cuneiform documents came from Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ and were dated to ca. 2500. The chronological gap between the archaic texts from the Ur SIS levels and the texts from Fāra, ancient Šuruppak, is currently not well defined, estimates ranging from three centuries to not much more than a hundred years.172 The situation is unfortunate since in the Early Dynastic I Period of southern Babylonia, or Early Dynastic I-II in the Diyala system before the date of the corpus from Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ Mesopotamia must have been in a time of continued change resulting in a political and probably also cultural reorganizing of the entire region into a landscape different, though to an unknown degree, from that of earlier times.173
172 See Krebernik 1998, p. 259, quoting Karg 1984, for a short time gap between archaic Ur and Fāra. Visicato 2000 observed that the archaic Ur script seems to be in a time of rapid change, to be dated probably not much more than a hundred years before the Fāra corpus. Pomponio 1984 related the list of proper names from Fāra to archaic Ur sources. Krebernik 2002 however followed conventional dating for the archaic Ur corpus as ca. 2800 and the Fāra corpus ca. 2500, as did van de Mieroop 2004, p. 40. 173 Wilcke 2003, p. 10, observed that in the law of sale, the purchases of movables were distinguished from the purchases of immovables almost from the first example of written sales contract while only early in the 24th century BCE the distinction between ownership and possession came to be documented. The relationship between the change of legal concepts and the change of politics is too

62
It would be a part of the systematic shift that writing fell into disuse in northern Mesopotamia after the Late Uruk Period. It was not until the beginning of the Early Dynastic IIIa Period that seal inscriptions, statue labels and numerical tablets reappeared in the north.174 Indeed, there are altogether very few sources with references to Enlil available from sites other than Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ and dated within the time span discussed in this chapter. I will try to put equal weight on the attestations of Enlil in these sources but some of the suggestions to be made may be still biased towards the sources from Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ.175
2.1 Enlil in Fāra Period Administrative Texts
The site of Fāra, ancient Šuruppak as we know it, lies some 45 km southeast of Nippur and 50 km northwest of Uruk while Abū Ṣalābīḫ lies 20 km northwest of Nippur and 50 km southeast of Kiš. In the third millennium, from the northwest down the Euphrates to the southeast, Kiš, Abū Ṣalābīḫ, Nippur, Šuruppak, and Uruk were probably the major centres downstream of Sippar, the city written synonymous with the river Euphrates.176 Šuruppak must have been prosperous during the
big a question to ask but it may be assumed that substantial political changes are pretexts to the changes in the concept of ownership. 174 Besides those to be discussed below, the following objects, listed in a plausible chronological order, might also be of interest: Amiet 1980a, GMA0960, inscribed šita-dumu-nun; Quenet 2005, pl. 72bis-A and B, inscribed utu-ur-sag and sar-il respectively; Amiet 1980a, GMA 0952 inscribed ma-zu-é; Moortgat and MoortgatCorrens 1978, Abb. 12-13, inscriptions not readable; Quenet 2005, Fig. 3. 175 Barth 1977 documented that in a fairly closed region the mythologies in different localities can vary to a considerable degree even if the basic pattern in different localities remains compatible within the same cultural tradition. PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD 63
Uruk III Period though the earliest known documentation of the site came from by and large the Early Dynastic IIIa Period when the site was “rich in tablets”, as so remembered by the ancients later.177 The site of Fāra was excavated first by the Deutsche Orient-Gesellschaft (D.O.G.) and then by the University Museum, and certainly also by clandestine digs whose findings were sold on the antiquity market.178 The entire textual corpus from the site of Fāra has been tentatively linked together through prosopography in combination with palaeography. The majority of the D.O.G. tablets, also tablets available from surface collecting and clandestine digs were, according to current studies, probably written within one single year, most likely the year preceding the destruction of the city Šuruppak by fire. The fewer texts excavated by the University Museum were probably written within a generation’s time from that of the D.O.G. corpus, according to recent research.179 It is fairly
reconstruction of the course of the Euphrates in the third millennium, whose changing courses must have affected the Mesopotamian cities considerably. certain that literacy was widespread in ancient Šuruppak since a high proportion of the excavated buildings contained written records.180 The same situation of literacy probably applies to the site of Abū Ṣalābīḫ. The ancient name of Abū Ṣalābīḫ is yet not securely identified but the attestation of lugal-Ereš in one of the few administrative texts from the site may suggest Ereš to be the most plausible candidate (IAS 505).181 The finding of the Keš Temple Hymn from the site has been taken to support Keš as an alternative choice but this is unlikely. It is well known that most exemplars of the Keš Temple Hymn actually came from the city of Nippur, while apparently there is no reason to regard Nippur as Keš.182 Abū Ṣalābīḫ was occupied and prosperous in the Late Uruk Period, though archaic texts are not known there. The IAS texts mostly came from Area E on the east mound of the site, probably a temple area. If compared to the large number of tablets and fragments of literary contents, the amount of administrative texts from Abū Ṣalābīḫ is very small but this may be explained by the chance of discovery.183 The Abū Ṣalābīḫ
have been written within one generation’s time also through an identification of a personal name Ur-ra-ni as one and the same person. These two names, though known from different sites and different time periods, seem too much alike each other, suggesting a possibility that they were both inherited title/name rather than names of individual persons used only in their lifetime. Note also, Šuruppak was not entirely deserted after the destruction since occasional attestations of the city name are available from Pre-Sargonic and Sargonic sources, and it had an Ur III ensi. corpus is dated to be roughly contemporary with, but probably later than, the Fāra texts.184 In addition to the major discoveries from the sites of Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ, roughly contemporary texts are reported from Babylonian sites including Kiš, Nippur, Isin, Adab, Girsu, and Uruk. The dating of the texts from these places is often solely on palaeographical grounds and the total number of such texts is small.185
2.1.1 Fāra Personal Names composed with Enlil
The following is a collection of identified personal names composed with Enlil from the textual corpus from the Fāra Period, building upon previous prosopographical studies. I try to provide prosopographical information for each name in three subcategories, a. b. and c. If there is no clear prosopographical information for the attestation of the name in question, the siglum of the text in which the attestation is found will be put in subcategory a. If the attestation is followed or preceded by a title or by other kind of prosopographical information, the title or the other information will be quoted together with the siglum of the text in subcategory b. Subcategory c. is designed for parallel names, namely names not composed with Enlil but except the Enlil element they consist of the same elements in the same grammatical structure. The collection of the parallel names does not aim to be complete and clearly not every name has all the three subcategories of attestation. In addition, texts from Fāra are quoted without repeating their provenance but the provenances of texts from other sites including those from Abū Ṣalābīḫ will be provided in brackets.

of the names seem to be fairly obvious. The chance of discovery and the imbalance of geographical coverage of the database do not allow solid conclusions to be drawn from the regional distribution of the Enlil names listed above. But it is important to note that Enlil was used as a theophoric element among the names known in the few texts from Nippur and Adab, suggesting that Enlil was widely known and perhaps deeply rooted in the name giving practices of these cities already at the beginning of the ED IIIa Period.
2.1.1.1 dEn-líl-“UNKEN”-(a)
The fact that the theophoric element Dumuzi was not found attested together with “UNKEN.A” may remotely hint that the element “UNKEN” was somehow differentiated from “UNKEN.A”. I have therefore listed the two apparently similar names as separate entried above. But this is not the problem here. The question is concerned with the interpretation of these names. “UNKEN” in the “UNKEN” and “UNKEN”-a names is usually transliterated as unken in Sumerian. But the sign used here is actually different from that of LAK649 = UNKEN, puḫrum in Akkadian, as the former obviously has an extra wedge.197 If it were identical to LAK649, the name dEn-líl-“UNKEN”-a would be readily interpreted as “Enlil (is) in (the) assembly”, in which the –a at the end is taken as the locative ending. It is tempting indeed to assume that the two signs were the same or
197 UNKINa is known in the archaic corpus denoting a vessel, shown in Englund 1998, p. 159. Deimel 1922 did not include the sign “UNKEN” with one more wedge than LAK 649 in his list of cuneiform signs known from the Fāra corpus. Pomponio 1987, pp. 100-101, read the sign in question as unken without comment. The sign LAK649 = UNKEN was frequent in archaic Lú A as a part of official titles such as GAL.UNKEN = kingal, “assembly leader” according to Wilcke 2005, p. 441. Krebernik 2002, p. 21, n. 87, noted the connection between dNin-PA and GAL.UNKEN (LAK649) = kingal. The first entry of ED Officials I is UNKEN, which may or may not be an “official” title, despite the modern designation of the list.



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD 71
at least they had the same meaning, so to follow Selz to see all of the theophoric elements of the parallel names in the “divine assembly”.198 The theophoric elements of the parallel names including Inanna, Ištaran, Sud, and Utu, in this scenario, would be the other deities taking part in the mythic “divine assembly” besides Enlil. The attestations of É-kur and É-kur-ra in the same onomastic contexts, however unfortunately, are not easily explained in this scenario. If they were to present in the supposed “divine assembly”, there would be too much speculation.199 The parallel names of names 8 and 9, nevertheless, show to us the basic fact that Dumuzi, dInanna, dIštaran, dSùd, Utu, É-kur, É-kur-ra and also Nin were associated with “UNKEN” in ways parallel to that of Enlil, regardless whether “UNKEN” referred to the “divine assembly”. In the -á-maḫ names also, the parallel elements to Enlil include dSùd, Utu, Lugal, and Nin. It is therefore possible to summarize our finding here as, much less exciting than a reconstruction of the mythic “divine assembly” based upon an uncertain reading of “UNKEN”, that in the Fāra Period dSùd and Utu were the theophoric elements most compatible with Enlil in their onomastic use. In other words, dSùd and Utu were probably perceived in a way closest to that of Enlil in the namegiving practice of that time.

These Šuruppak lists are nowhere dated with the bala notation, unlike that practiced in the Ur III Period, although the notation system was already known there.201 The delivery of offerings in most of the cases was made through Urni, who was probably responsible for the expenditures of an “administrative centre”.202 Urni is also known to be associated with the household of dKin-nir, which was probably the institution responsible for the expenditure of kids delivered to the recipients as listed here.203 The offerings recorded in the first entries of the lists most frequently targeted the èš whose affiliation was not specified. The èš in these lists has been interpreted as synonymous with é-dSùd which may be the most important centre of husbandry in the city. It is possible that èš was used in the context of offering and é-dSùd in the context of economic activities when both terms actually denoted the same institution which was the

major temple oikos, that of dSùd in the city Šuruppak. The attestation of Enlil immediately following the èš of dSùd in these lists conforms to the close relationship between dSùd of Šuruppak and Enlil known from literary texts such as “The Marriage of Sud”.204 In SF 036 written in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography, we do find é-Enlil in close connection with the èš of dSùd.205 It is however to be noticed that, although “The Marriage of Sud” specifies that the relation of Enlil and Sud was that of a divine couple, the above sources from ED IIIa Šuruppak do not make this as clear as we would like to have. The close relationship of Enlil with Sud, that of a couple or not, would have political connotations. It has been shown that Sud was the most compatible figure with Enlil in the onomastic contexts which do not discriminate according to sexuality. The fact that Enlil receives offering close in rank with Sud in Šuruppak may suggest Nippur was one of the most important participants in the political circle centred on Šuruppak. In CT 50 023, a text of cattle delivery, ki dEn-líl was attested in parallel position to kin-nir and ki EN.ZU, in a sequence resembling the sequence of dEn-líl dEn-ki dGibil6 dNin-kin-nir dEN.ZU dAma-Ušumgal dNísaba in the SF lists known vaguely as the “Opferlisten” (SF 005-006). It is not entirely certain whether ki dEn-líl is to be interpreted as the region of Nippur, but the text definitely shows that the deity Enlil was deeply involved in the Šuruppak administration.206

PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD 75
2.1.3 The Office sanga-GAR dEn-líl
The beginning of TSS 627 recording si-NU×U/NU×ŠUŠ, “fishing net”, contained a difficult phrase, maškim Da-da sanga-GAR dEn-líl šeš-ama-na dub-sar.207 In all likelihood sanga-GAR dEn-líl was an official title of Da-da in particular connection with Enlil.208 The title sanga-GAR is attested in the Fāra corpus with more than a dozen personal names while only here in the case of Da-da was it combined with dEn-líl, probably showing that sanga-GAR was a profession but not a particular position so that various offices could be named by attaching different elements to it. The function of sanga-GAR dEn-líl can only be inferred from the function of sanga-GAR in general. In doing this, we are assuming that sanga-GAR dEn-líl was a special instance in the category of sanga-GAR. The function of sanga in the Uruk III Period was probably similar to what was later known as dub-sar, “scribe”.209 SANGA was common in the colophons of lexical and literary texts from Fāra, where it is likely to be read as umbisag for “scribe” instead of sanga for “the sanga priest”.210 Both of sanga and sanga-GAR are known in the lexical list ED Lú E but importantly they are attested in widely separated positions. The list ED Lú E is known from ED III Abū Ṣalābīḫ (IAS 055-060) and Ebla (MEE 3 037-046), as well as from Sargonic Nuzi (HSS 10 222), Kiš (MAD 5 035) and Urkeš.211 The great popularity of ED Lú E suggests that it may actually have
the phrase ki dEn-líl remains open, but note Krebernik 2001a on ki dUtu. For ki dEn-líl in the Fāra corpus, see Edzard, Farber and Sollberger 1977, p. 95. 207 Krebernik 1998, p. 307, n. 716. 208 Pomponio 1987, p. 100, understood dEn-líl as a personal name though the same author in Martin, Pomponio et al. 2001, p. 105, took sanga-GAR dEn-líl as an administrative title. Pomponio 1987, p. 226, read Šeš-ama-na as a personal name. 209 Visicato 2000, p. 4, especially ns. 16–17. 210 Visicato 2000, pp. 4–5. 211 Buccellati 2003, pp. 46–48, introduces the newly known Urkeš exemplar of ED Lú E. 76
rooted in the administrative practice at the time of its origin.212 The term sanga appears in line 2 of ED Lú E following dub-sar while sanga-GAR is attested in line 62 preceding gudu4 and nu-èš.213 Their different positions in ED Lú E suggest that the two titles denoted different things in practice, the former probably umbisag while the latter belonging to the same category as gudu4 and nu-èš, both “priestly” titles. Visicato recognized that sanga-GAR, as well as sa12-du5 and dub-sarmaḫ, were all high officials of the é-géme, “house of female workers”, the central administrative organ under the énsi-GAR(-gal) and supplementary to é-gal and é-uru, the highest authorities, in Šuruppak.214 The office of sanga-GAR dEn-líl in Šuruppak, accordingly, would have also been affiliated with the “priestly” é-géme through which it could be active in the administration of the city.215 It is important to note, however, that in the lexical and literary texts from Abū Ṣalābīḫ, unlike in the case of Fāra, only IAS 132 and IAS 255 had the sign for sanga in their colophons and the sign sanga in these cases may be read not as umbisag but indeed as sanga.216 This fact suggests that the administrative terminologies in Abū Ṣalābīḫ may be different from those of Šuruppak. But in any case, the particular office sanga-GAR dEn-líl is unevidenced in Abū Ṣalābīḫ. The above suggestion based on sources from Šuruppak, that the office sanga-GAR dEn-líl was involved in both the temple and the civil administration of the city may be untrue in the city of Abū Ṣalābīḫ.

The borders of the hypothetical Kiš kingdom in Steinkeller 1993a cannot be defined. 213 ED Lú E, 1–7: dub-sar, sanga, sagi, šabra, énsi, nu-bànda GÌR.NÍTA (šagina); 62–70: sanga-GAR, ú-a, gudu4, é-dù, SA6:DI, PA:GIDIM, PAP:GAR (var. 2.2 Enlil in the Earliest Royal Inscriptions 2.2.1 Pa-bil-ga-gi4 lugal-ŠÁR×DIŠ
The inscription LUGAL PA ŠÁR×DIŠ GIŠ BIL GA AN EN KID GI4 on a nude alabaster statuette found at Umma had been interpreted either as “For Enlil, Pabilgagi, king of Umma” or “Enlilpabilgagi, der König von Umma?”217 The writing of the assumed name Enlil was dEn-KID here and in fact the second sign of the sign combination EN.KID was very close to the KIDa in the Archaic City List presented in the last chapter (Figures 1.3 and 2.1).218
Fig. 2.1: AN.EN.KID in the Inscription of Pa-bil-ga-gi4 (Enp 1)
On art historical and palaeographical grounds the figurine Enp 1 was dated to Early Dynastic I Period or Early Dynastic II in the Diyala system, earlier than the texts from Fāra. The characteristics of the figurine, which was probably used as a support, are comparable to figurines from Early Dynastic II Diyala.219 The sign ŠÁR×DIŠ here was evidently used to
217 See Cooper 1986, p. 92, and Steible and Behrens 1982b, p. 265, respectively. The inclusion of GI4 into the personal name seems somehow difficult since apparently the sign was separated by the line AN EN KID. Cf. George 2003 on the name of Bilgames in Sumerian, and Marchesi 2004, pp. 196-197 on pa-bìl-ga = abu. 218 Fig. 2.1 is from Lloyd 1946, p. 3, where the autograph was up side down. 219 Spycket 1981, pp. 56–57, Fig. 20, dated the object to ED II, meaning 2750–2600 BCE. See also Cooper 1986, p. 92: “considerably earlier than Pabilgaltuk, the ruler of Umma conquered by Urnanshe.” Spycket 1981, p. 56, compared the figurine with that of Frankfort 1939b, pp. 26-27, suggesting that they came from the same workshop.

write the name Ašgi in Pre-Sargonic sources. It is possible also to read ŠÁR×DIŠ as the name of Ašgi and take it as the dedicatee.220 The inscription, therefore, is evidence that before the ED III Period Enlil was used as a theophoric element in the name of a ruler active in the Umma region where Ašgi, a deity originally at home in the region of Adab-Keš, received dedications.221
2.2.2 Me-salim(DI) lugal-Kiš
The “divine intervention” of Enlil in the demarcation of the border line between Lagaš and Umma in Pre-Sargonic Lagaš inscriptions that Collingwood commented upon cannot be set in history even if original inscriptions of Me-salim(DI) lugal-Kiš are known to testify to the king as a historical figure. Nevertheless, if seen together with other attestations of Me-salim in Early Dynastic royal inscriptions, a careful (re)-reading of the beginning of the cone of Entemena provides new insights (Ent 28-29):
Obv. 1,1dEn-líl 1,2lugal-kur-kur-ra 1,3ab-ba-dingir-dingir-ré-ne-ke4 1,4inim-gi-na-ni-ta 1,5dNin-gír-su 1,6dŠára-bi 1,7ki e-ne-sur 1,8Me-salim 1,9lugal-Kiški-ke4 1,10inim-dIštaran-na-ta 1,11éš GÁNA bi-ra 1,12ki-ba na bí-rú
“Enlil, king of all lands, father of all the gods, by his authoritative commands, demarcated the boundary between Ningirsu and Šara. Me-salim, king of Kiš, at the command of Ištaran, measured it off and erected a monument there.”222
220 See Biggs 1971b, p. 2. The interpretation of the inscription would be then “For LUGAL Ašgi, Enlilpabilgagi (dedicated)”, or “For Ašgi, lugal Enlilpabilgagi (dedicated)” or the like. For the writings of the city name of Umma see Lambert 1990. Note ŠÁR×DIŠ was a part of a goddess name attested as a spouse of Enlil in the fragment from Abū Ṣalābīḫ, IAS 345, which will be edited below. 221 For the connection of Ašgi with Keš see Biggs 1974, p. 23. 222 The translation is adapted from that of Cooper 1986, p. 54.



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD 79
In this later Lagašite interpretation of the past event, it may be noted that Enlil demarcated the boundary line between Ningirsu and Šara by its own command, while Me-salim acted on the command of dIštaran, inim-dIštaran-na-ta. The action taken by Enlil was not simply a parallel translation into theocratic terms of that taken by the lugal-Kiški, since Enlil acted by his own command while Me-salim had to act by the command of dIštaran. The relationship between Me-salim lugal-Kiš and dIštaran but not that between Me-salim lugal-Kiš and dEn-líl lugalkur-kur-ra ab-ba-dingir-dingir-ré-ne, was that which is in parallel to the relationship between Eannatum and Ningirsu.223 It has been suggested that Me-salim(DI) was actually from Dēr rather than Kiš since dIštaran was the patron deity of the city of Dēr.224 The possibility also exists that the attestation of dIštaran in the account was due to its functionality as a deity instead of its city of patronage if assuming that the writing of the name dIštaran with the signs KA.DI had to do with the “role” of the deity in legal affairs such as an event of border demarcation.225 In any case, the association of Me-salim with an dIštaran also from places other than Lagaš has to be taken into consideration. In my perception, it attests to Me-salim’s identity as a ruler from the outside in the border demarcation. Me-salim(DI) lugal-Kiš in an inscription from a stone vessel found at Adab was named as dumu-ki-ág NIN.SAG.ḪUR.AN, to be read dumu ki-ág dNin-ḫur-sag, “the beloved son of Ninḫursag”, who performed the bur-gi4
223 Cooper 1983b, p. 27, however, regarded the relationship between Me-salim and Enlil as in parallel to Eannatum and Ningirsu in the ruler’s dream in which Ningirsu came to tell the ruler “Kiš will itself abandon Umma”. 224 Cf. Postgate 1994a, p. 31. Franke 1995, pp. 95–96, took Kiš in the royal epithet as the name of the city but it is clear that people with this title did not all come from Kiš. Gelb and Kienast 1990, p. 64, interpreted the title as šar kiššati. 225 Cf. Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 333, n. 1445, on dIštaran in the “UNKEN.A” names from Fāra. dIštaran(KA.DI) was ku-li-ki-ág of Lugalzagesi the énsi-kal-ga-dEn-líl in another text also records events related to border, then the construction of the border channel (Luzag 2).



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD
80
rite presumably in the é-sar of Adab when Nin-kisal-si was the énsi-GAR of Adab, according to another inscription (Mes 2-3).226 It can be inferred from the title of Me-salim not only that dNin-ḫur-sag was revered in the city of Adab, not necessarily centred there, but also that Me-salim, who in his own tradition was associated with dIštaran, had bee once active in the local rites of Adab as an outsider at that time. In the inscription on a mace head found at Girsu, Me-salim was named as the builder of the temple of Ningirsu, é-dù-dNin-gír-su, while the name of the énsi of Lagaš was Lugal-šà-engur (Mes 1). Lugal-šà-engur as attested in this inscription is also unknown in other Lagašite royal inscriptions. The phraseology of the border demarcation and the situation in Adab may support the interpretation of Me-salim’s title in this inscription that Me-salim was granted the title on some occasion of his actual involvement in Lagašite affairs, more specifically the (re)building of the temple of Ningirsu. In terms of political history, the fact that Me-salim participated in the local affairs of Lagaš and Adab is important although precisely which relationship the two cities had to the lugal-Kiški cannot be immediately inferred from the sources. The suggestion can only be that, the actual involvements of Me-salim in Lagaš and Adab may have been justified by a rationale that he was the arbitrator for the cities not of his own but under his jurisdiction, as the beginning of the cone of Entemena explains.
2.2.3 Ur-dNanše lugal-Lagaš
Urn 26 is an inscription on a stone tablet that recorded the building activities of Ur-Nanše, the earliest ruler of Lagaš whose original inscriptions we know.227 The text of Urn 26 tells that, Ur-dNanše lugal-Lagaš

2.2.4 En-na-il lugal-[Kiš]
In ECTJ 219 En-na-il lugal was mentioned in the context of dEn-líl, dNin-líl, and Ur.234 Westenholz considered ECTJ 219 as an Ur III copy of a Pre-Sargonic text but he did not identify the En-na-il lugal here as the same as the En-na-il lugal-Kiš known from the Early Dynastic statue fragment from Nippur, 6N-T271. He believed that ECTJ 219 described the cultic function of the “kings of Kiš” in Pre-Sargonic times in a way “similar in scope to those of the Ur III kings”.235 If we suppose the
Níg-en-na-aša5-dEn-líl-ka (RTC 066, RTC 071), and Nag-TAR-dEn-líl-le-pà-da (VAT 4740). 233 Jacobsen 1994, pp. 149–150. 234 The text of ECTJ 219, according to the transliteration in Westenholz 1975a, p. 100, runs as: [...] x [...] gi AB, [...] é sikil, [...] gi maḫ, [...] maḫ, lines missing; gazi ki-UD è, banšur dEn-líl, ì-áb ga sikil, nisagx Urimki, banšur dEn-líl, [x] sipa?-gal, [...] x; [...], tin? authenticity of ECTJ 219 as a copy of an original inscription of Ennail and at the same time take him as the same ruler as the lugal-Kiš recorded in fragment 6N-T271, En-na-il lugal-Kiš would be the earliest historical ruler from early Mesopotamian that is known to have collected “first fruit offerings” for Enlil and Ninlil.236 The “table of Enlil”, banšur dEn-líl, which was mentioned twice in the text ECTJ 219, was apparently a significant part of the “first fruit offering” rite. It is also noteworthy that the lugal likely had a Semitic name though beyond this the text is largely unexplainable.237 In addition, other Ur III copies of earlier inscriptions mentioned that Ennail son of A-dIM.MImušen made dedication to Inanna in Nippur after having besieged Elam.238 In AnLag 1 from Lagaš, a certain A-dIM.MImušen is attested to have dedicated a statue to Ningirsu for the life of Du-du the dub-sar during the time of Ur-dNanše. If all of these sources, scanty as they are, are put together, it seems plausible that Ennail the lugal-Kiš was also in some way once associated with Lagaš.239
