In the so called “god lists”, when the name of dEn-líl was attested and preserved, the signs in which the name was written were always AN.EN.É, as was also in the administrative texts from the Early Dynastic IIIa Period. The city name of Nippur was unfortunately broken away from SF 023 which began with the ED City List, so that it is unknown how the city name was actually written, but based on sources from Abū Ṣalābīḫ the writing EN.É may be assumed (IAS 021, IAS 022).244 In IAS 082 Col. 9’ (Rv), lines 1–8, the so-called “Abū Ṣalābīḫ God-List”, dNin-KID was attested in one section along with dEn-É, the latter to be understood as dEn-líl, as also the case in “The Parallel List from Fāra” SF 023.245 In IAS 082 Obv.
242 The only possibility to make “sense” out of the beginning lines of the list is to read the lines in each case as divine names followed by toponyms, like Enlil, Nibru, Inanna, Zabala?, Enki, Eridu, Sud, Šuruppak but this is apparently not the kind of theology Wiggermann 1992 wanted. The choice to regard SF 007 as an exercise tablet therefore explaining away the difficulty in interpreting it for theological purposes has no bearing on the question since the other “god-lists” are equally open to doubts as of their theological significance. 243 The autographs are re-produced based on those available at CDLI, originally prepared by Biggs and Krebernik respectively. 86
Col. 1, line 3, dNin-KID alone was mentioned.246 SF 023 stands out as instrumental for the interpretation of the pair attested in IAS 082 since SF 023 Obv. Col. 5, lines 17–25 began a section listing deities after the entries of the City List.247 In my perception, the part listing deities in SF 023 was probably a later addition to the previous list and was a “divine” translation of the City List in a process of mythologizing. The Mesopotamian scribes seemed to mythologize real events and real people as soon as they passed away in their memories. The fact that the legendary albeit human figures, Gilgamesh and Lugalbanda, were not only listed in the “god lists” among “deities” but also were used in personal names as theophoric elements, would suggest that the transformation from the real to the supernatural may even be a self conscious process for the ancients.248
246 The only “theological” way to read dNin-KID in IAS 082 Obv. Col. 1, line 3 is to regard it as in pair with dEn-ki, between the pairs of dEnki-dNinki and dNannadInanna while dInanna and dNin-gír-su formed another pair. 247 Englund, Nissen et al. 1993 edited the known fragments of the Archaic City List. See also the reproduction in Englund 1998, p. 91, Fig. 26: Composite copy of the lexical list “cities”. The last restorable line of the Archaic City List is EZEN×AŠ, which corresponded to the line AŠ.EZEN×AŠ in SF 023 from ED IIIa Fāra. The line following AŠ.EZEN×AŠ in SF 023 read AN.EZEN×AŠ, after which each line begun with AN for the divine determinative. In the archaic lexical corpus the line presumably correspondent to AN.EZEN×AŠ was broken away and no more lines were identified for the part hereafter as those in SF 023. 248 Cf. Krebernik 1998, p. 310: “Obwohl Gilgameš ebenso wie Lugalbanda in die grosse Götterliste aus Fāra aufgenommen ist, erscheint er–im Gegensatz zu seinen Eltern Ninsun und Lugalbanda–bislang in keiner der Fāra-zeitlichen Dichtungen.” The so-called Archaic Tribute List is perhaps an example of another kind of mythologizing, that of earlier texts. Englund 1998, pp. 99–102, considered the List as “the earliest work of written literature on earth”, and Westenholz 1998b took it as a text of esoteric knowledge. Veldhuis 2006, pp. 196–197, pointed out that the Tribute List was probably an exercise list with utilitarian purposes by origin though it became venerated in the OB scribal circles. The problem is,



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD 87
The fact that the texts allowed the discrepancy in the writings of the names of dNin-KID and dEn-É could only come from the minds of the compilers. In the Uruk III Period, the name of Nippur was represented with the compound ENx+KIDa.NUNx on the Archaic City Seal while the same name was written as ENa.KIDa on the Archaic City List, in basic agreement with that on the Uruk City Sealing (W11456). In the Early Dynstic I Period, if accepting traditional wisdom in reading the Ur SIS sealings, Nippur was written sometimes as UD.EN.KID while in other cases ANx?.KID. The quoted inscriptions of Pabilgagi and Ur-dNanše in their original material forms both wrote Enlil as dEn-KID, in correspondence with the writing of the city name, while in the god-lists above, dNin-KID appears to be attested regularly in pair with AN.EN.É, which most likely is to be understood as dEn-líl. Steinkeller explained that Ninlil was a creation out of Enlil but he assumed that the name of Enlil was written EN.É from the earliest times.249 Steinkeller failed to acknowledge the fact that epigraphically Enlil was written AN.EN.KID rather than AN.EN.É at least in the inscription of Pabilgagi who was certainly earlier than the Early Dynastic III Period (Fig. 2.1). The sign was still unmistakenably KID before or around the beginning of the Early Dynastic IIIa Period, as in the inscription of Ur-Nanše (Urn 26). It is problematic to interpret Ninlil starting from a problematic assumption of the name of Enlil being EN.É. The sign É in the name of Enlil in the sources presented above, furthermore, can hardly be simply a variant of KID in the process of the merging of cuneiform signs, since apparently the sign KID continued to be in use. Entry 38 of the Archaic City List, É.NUN, has been interpreted as NINLIL (W20335,2+, W20218,18, W23998,1, W20218,17+).250 Insofar as the archaic corpus is concerned, serious doubts can be raised against this
the mechanisms of such mythologizing transformations, from reality to myth, from utilitarian texts to literature, are not known. I thank Dr Eleanor Robson for bringing to my attention Veldhuis 2006. 249 Steinkeller 1999, p. 114, n. 36. 250 This is how Green, Nissen et al. 1987 took it.



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD
88
identification. First of all, the only fragment on which entry 38 of the Archaic City List is clear enough has instead E2a.NUNa.E2b, not É.NUN or NUN.É (W23998,1)! It is hard to imagine that E2a.NUNa.E2b was the archaic name of Ninlil. Second, in later Sumerian literature, É.NUN quite securely stands for agrun but not the name of Ninlil.251 It is true that the fact that É.NUN stands for agrun in later Sumerian literature does not preclude the possibility that similar writings in the Archaic City List could stand for Ninlil, but without explaining away its later use, the identification of the name Ninlil in the archaic corpus has to remain speculative. The identification of the entry of the City List as Ninlil is presumably based on Obv. Col. 3, line 1 of SF 023, written NIN.KID. It is identical with the writing of Ninlil as found in other contemporary sources from the ED IIIa Period. But it must be pointed out that this is the single case in which the entry is written so. It is difficult to base on a single piece of evidence to interpret the entry of the List. I would prefer to consider the line as a re-interpretation of the archaic E2a.NUNa.E2b, whose original referent we do not know. In IAS 021, another ED IIIa examplar of the City List, Obv. Col. 3, line 4 is written KID.NUN.252 It might be possible to read NUN as NIN, as in the UD.GAL.NUN orthrography to be discussed below, so to make IAS 021 another case in which the entry reads Ninlil. But it needs still to be explained why this particular line writing the particular name has to prefer the UD.GAL.NUN orthography.253 In any case, the attestation of Ninlil in the Archaic City List is highly problematic even the ED IIIa lines here do name Ninlil. If combining with Steinkeller’s point that Ninlil simply was a reflection of Enlil, in consequence to the non-attestation of Ninlil as explained here, it may now be possible to hypothesize that, Ninlil was indeed a creation out of the
251 For É.NUN for agrun, see Caplice 1973. 252 The sign forms are excerpts from the line arts at the DCCLT website managed by the team led by Niek Veldhuis at http://cdl.museum.upenn.edu/dcclt/. 253 Krebernik 2001b quoted ED IIIa attestations that might be Ninlil but unfortunately did not give an interpretation of the the evolvement of the writing of the name in the early periods.



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD 89
original AN.EN.KID when an dEN.É took the position of AN.EN.KID, feminizing the latter to be dNin-KID.254 Ninlil was so literally “created” out of AN.EN.KID. This hypothesis explains at the same time the origin of Ninlil and the orthographic change of ENa.KIDa to EN.É for the name of Enlil, which is first seen in the ED IIIa Period.255 It must be admitted that, the ED re-interpretation of the entry 38 of the Archaic City List by Obv. Col. 3, line 1 of SF 023 as NIN.KID, however it may refer to Ninlil, does not necessarily lead to the conclusion that Ninlil was completely a new creation. The ED re-interpretation of the entry can have a different background in which Ninlil/NIN.KID was already there. This stands true no matter whether the writing E2a.NUNa.E2b was the archaic form of the name of the goddess. Therefore, a less fantastic scenario may be considered as well. This scenario is relatively simple: An unprovenanced dNin-KID became the wife of Enlil around the same time when dEN.É replaced AN.EN.KID as the writing of Enlil. Both of the above interpretations for the orthographic change of the name of Enlil and for the discrepancy in the writings of the names of the pair Enlil and Ninlil may appear to be unnecessary since the switch of scribal convention seems to be capable of explaining everything. In the case of the archaic attestation of Ninlil, the problem may also seem to be easily resolved only if a free alternation between É and KID is assumed for early cuneiform writing and one of the És in entry 38 of W23998,1 can be safely ignored. But explaining everything by assuming the free change of conventions or the free alternation/deletion of cuneiform signs seems too easy to be true for me. The scribes would be too freehanded to allow for such apparent discrepancy in the case of the names of Enlil and Ninlil if they had really nothing to mean. It is unimaginable also that such discrepancies could be as consistently kept as we know from the sources. The orthographic change of the name of Enlil and the discrepancy in writing the names of Enlil and Ninlil must have their historical context in
254 Cf. Steinkeller 1999, p. 114, n. 36. 255 It needs to be stressed, of course, that the phenomenon of the feminization of deities as considered here is somehow unparalleled in ancient Mesopotamia.

which either Ninlil was remembered in writing as the femenized form of AN.EN.KID or as a goddess who was originally unrelated to Enlil in any particular way but was married to AN.EN.KID at a time when the latter’s name was changed due to the switch of scribal covention.256 In any case, the orthographic change of the name of Enlil must have coincided with a major breakthrough in the development of his “personhood”. The promiscuous characteristics of AN.EN.É may or may not be inherited from the earlier dEn-KID but they must be somehow compatible since there needed connections to smoothen the change of the writing of the deity along with which perhaps also the change of “personhood”.258
2.3.2 UD.GAL.NUN and ENx+KIDx.NUN
In the Abū Ṣalābīḫ corpus Biggs recognized that a group of texts was written differently from the “standard” Sumerian orthography. Biggs termed the orthography UD.GAL.NUN after one of the most frequent sign combinations in the texts. In addition to the Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ corpus, texts written in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography are known also from Early Dynastic Nippur.259 The orthography was mainly used to write mythic texts but the contents of these texts do not seem to differ substantially from those written with the “standard” orthography, except that Enlil, whose name being written UD.GAL.NUN, was appearing more frequently than in the “standard” texts.260 The current academic interpretation of the UD.GAL.NUN orthography is that, it was “eine Kryptographie und repräsentiert keine dialektale Variante des Sumerischen”.261
dNin-KID as the dam of Enlil. 258 Selz 1995, p. 125, n. 491, took dEn-É as a “Urzeitgottheit” other than Enlil. 259 See Lambert 1976 for an overview of the discovery. Lambert 1981, pp. 81–82, reported the identification of ECTJ 173 as a biorthographical list of names in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography, made by Krecher 1978. Lambert 1981, p. 305, referred to unpublished Nippur UD.GAL.NUN duplicate of IAS 116. Cf. Biggs 1981, p. 126. 260 Lambert 1981, p. 93, observed that Enlil and Enki were the highest in the pantheon when the UD.GAL.NUN texts were written but noted that Zababa has occurred in IAS 129 and IAS 142. Lambert 1976, p. 431, noted that Zababa may be understood as the “seed of Enlil” in these texts. 261 Krebernik 1998, p. 309. 92
The use of multiple alternatives of signs for the same phonetic value, a common phenomenon in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography, as against the “standard” Sumerian orthography, however, makes it extremely difficult to “crack” the cryptographic system of writing, although a number of regular alternations between the signs used in the UD.GAL.NUN system as against their use in the “standard” orthography have been successfully recognized.262 The question of the historical circumstances from which the UD.GAL.NUN orthography came about was never addressed before, though for good reason if considering the status of our knowledge of the literary corpus from the period as a whole.263 The similarity of the writing ENx+KIDx.NUNx for the name Nippur, if correctly interpreted for the Archaic City Seal, with the name of Enlil in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography, precisely as UD.GAL.NUN, is striking. The current decipherment of the name UD.GAL.NUN is that, each of the signs in it corresponds in sequence to the signs DINGIR.EN.LÍL respectively. But this interpretation is based on sources much later than the Archaic City Seal. The centuries’ long history of development that finally led to the correspondence of GAL and EN in the normalized UD.GAL.NUN system of writing is still unknown. In this situation, especially considering the fact that in the Ur SIS sealing MSVO 2 U001 the name of Nippur was UD.EN.KID which in terms
were developed from the same cultural repertoire. Cf. Lambert 1981, p. 92: “As the modern decipherers are finding, only where similar material is known can understanding be gained. of chronology is dated in between the achaic corpus and the UD.GAL.NUN corpus, an alternative line of thinking that pays attention to the historical process that led to the correspondence of EN and GAL in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography may be possible. If taking EN.KID in the Ur SIS city sealing as however a descendent of the ENx+KIDx of ENx+KIDx.NUNx on the Archaic City Seal, for instance, the suggestion seems to be that, the GAL element took the place of ENx+KIDx in the name of Enlil so that the latter became UD.GAL.NUN. The new writing of the name is perhaps just a joining together of the Ur SIS tradition and the Archaic City Seal tradition, only if the UD.GAL.NUN orthrography actually came out of traditions back into the archaic periods. The new writing of the name of Enlil as UD.GAL.NUN was related to the archaic name of Nippur in a process of development different from that of the “standard” orthography and more complex.264 In the UD.GAL.NUN orthography as we now know of, therefore, the phonetic correspondence of GAL and EN was not original but was due to the normalization of the “new” orthography. In the same hypothetical mode and open to the challenge of historical chance, it is furthermore worth noting that this line of thinking may also provide a possible interpretation of the frequency of the name of Enlil in the form of
264 Steinkeller 2002a criticized Matthews 1993 for reading NIBRU on the Archaic City Seal for not considering the time gap between the UD.GAL.NUN and the Late Uruk Period. 2.3.3 Fāra Period Cultic Hymns 2.3.3.1 nam-lugal-la in the Keš Temple Hymn
The Keš Temple Hymn was known only from Old Babylonian copies before the discovery was made in Abū Ṣalābīḫ, which proves that the Old Babylonian version was not a creation of Old Babylonian scribes using older material, but was a faithful reflection, with only a few revisions of details, of a text that had already been fixed in the Sumerian literary tradition for centuries.266 The contents of the Keš Temple Hymn may reflect the mythological outlook of the universe in the early periods. Let us begin from the Prologue (OB version, ETCSL No. 4.80.2):267
265 The NUN of UD.GAL.NUN is unlikely to do with Enki as Michalowski 1998 suggests. Woods 2005 recognized that NUN was not an essential part of the name UD.KIB.NUN for Sippar. The case of UD.GAL.NUN is again more complex since we have apparently two writing traditions of the name. Lambert 1989 and Civil 1976 noted that commercial activities were related to both Šamaš of Sippar and Enlil of Nippur. The names of these two cities, if following the interpretation here, would be both written in an archaic tradition that was compatible with the UD.GAL.NUN orthography. The writings of the two city names and the comercial connections of the two cities’ patron deities may provide the background for the interpretation of the origin of the UD.GAL.NUN orthography which was practiced in northern and central Babylonia along the Euphrates. 266 Biggs 1971a, p. 196. In addition to what is reiterated below, the Abū Ṣalābīḫ version did not divide the text into sections marked with é 1-kam-ma, “the first temple,” é 2-kam-ma “the second temple,” and so forth. 267 The text is quoted form the OB version since many lines of the Abū Ṣalābīḫ

“The princely lord, the princely lord came forth from the house. Enlil, the princely lord, came forth from the house. The princely lord came forth royally from the house. Enlil lifted his glance over all the lands, and the lands raised themselves to Enlil. The four corners of heaven became green for Enlil like a garden. Keš was positioned there for him with head uplifted, and as Keš lifted its head among all the lands, Enlil spoke in praise of Keš.”268
The Sumerian word nám here in the Prologue undoubtedly can be understood an independent noun meaning “lord” or the like so that nám-nun for “princely lord”.269 It is extremely unfortunate that the line in the Abū Ṣalābīḫ version of the hymn that corresponded to the OB version line 3, nám-nun-e nam-lugal-la é-ta nam-ta-ab-è, is broken away. The sign NAM here, unlike nám which is used semantically, was used phonetically in the OB prologue, albeit with the single exception in the case of nam-lugal-la where it must be a part of a nominal compound that reminds us of “kingship”, nam-lugal in Sumerian. Here nam-lugal-la has the morpheme -la at the end. In Sumerian the /a/ ending would be either the genetive or the locative so that the phrase would be analyzed as either nam.lugal.a (k) or nam.lugal.a. 2.3.3.2 EN.LÍL(É)ki in the Zà-mì Hymns
The Zà-mì Hymns are known only from Abū Ṣalābīḫ and were so named after the frequent key term in the composition, zà-mì, meaning “praise” in Sumerian. The publisher of the composition noted that the hymns seem to be in the tradition of the Sumerian Temple Hymns and the Keš Temple Hymn, which all characteristically use the word zà-mì.275 But unlike the Collection of Sumerian Temple Hymns and the Keš Temple Hymn the Zà-mì Hymns were not intended for temples but were for deities with their corresponding cultic centres. The Zà-mì Hymns are similar to the Keš Temple Hymn in that it has a prologue which the Collection of Sumerian Temple Hymns lacked. In the text of the composition, normally a divine name precedes the phrase zà-mì though in some cases there is no divine determinative. The principle of organization of the hymns does not seem to be clearly in geographical order but very often deities whose cultic centres are close to each other do appear in proximity.276 The prologue of the Hymns describes Nippur and Enlil, the former is called dur-an-ki, “the bond of Heaven and Earth” while the latter is described in terms difficult to make sense of themselves:277
1uru an-da mú 2an-da gú-lá 3dingir EN.LÍL(É)ki 4dur-an-ki 5dEn-líl(É)
275 The word zà-mì is however not a designation of literary genre, for instance, Steible and Behrens 1982a, pp. 110–111, Urn 49 began with an incantation for the efficacy of the reeds of Enki to be used in the ceremony upon building the èš of Girsu with the ending dNin-gír-su zà-mì. Krebernik 1998, p. 323: “In vielen Texten begegnet die Doxologie GN za3-me ‘Preis sei GN’.” The zà-mì texts may be linked through the use of certain phraseology, for instance, Cooper 1986, p. 33, noted that the reed of Enki, GI EN.KI, was probably also referred to in the Zà-mì Hymns from Abū Ṣalābīḫ in Biggs 1974, pp. 46–53. Note also, however, that in the OB version o the term is usually zà-mí, with mí but not mì. 276 Biggs 1974, p. 45. 277 Biggs 1974, p. 46.



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD 99
kur-gal 6dEn-líl nu en 7nam-nir 8en du11-ga 9nu-gi4-gi4 10LAK809 nu LAK809 11dEn-líl a-nun 12ki mu-gar-gar 13dingir gal-gal 14zà-mì mu-du11
In his review of Biggs’ monumental publication of the IAS texts, Lambert ventured a translation of the prologue:
“In the city that grew with heaven, that embraces heaven, Nippur, the bond of heaven and underworld: Enlil, the great mountain, Enlil, the lord, the noble Namnir, the lord, whose command is not reversed, is not confounded, Enlil, seed which the noble established, spoke praise of/concerning the great gods (as follows).”278
I would disagree with Lambert here for taking Enlil as the subject of the Sumerian verb du11, “speak”, therefore the active of the praising of the great gods. For the interpretation of the relevant lines, most if not all of scholarly views now seem to prefer taking Enlil as the recipient of praising rather than the one who took the active.279 The passage of some extra interest is lines 09-10, nu-gi4-gi4 LAK809 nu LAK809. Lambert’s reading, nu-gi4-gi4 nu-ḫi-ḫi, as for “is not reversed, is not confounded”, has not found general agreement in scholarly understanding of the passage.280 It is important to note that within the IAS
278 Lambert 1976, p. 230. 279 For instance, Westenholz 2000, p. 49, has based on Krecher 1992, p. 293, to translate lines 11–14 as: “O Enlil, the Anunna gods have founded it (= the city), the great gods have said (to you? To it = to the city?) ‘hail’.” Ibid., p. 54, quotes from Krebernik 1998, p. 319, n. 785: “die Doxologie am Strophenende wird als elliptische Schreibweise für ‘in ON pries GN ihn (Enlil)’ interpretiert.” Cf. also d’Agostino 1988, p. 82: “Enlil, verstreute den prächtigen Samen auf der Erde: (dann) teilte er den grossen Göttern di (ihre) Kenzeichen zu.”
280 D’Agostino 1988, p. 82, n. 23, suggested a reading for LAK809 nu LAK809 as nu-ár-ár: “nicht verbessert, nicht hinzugefügt (werden kann).” Westenholz 2000, p. 54, n. 21, quoted the translation/interpretation offered by Krebernik 1994, p.



PART II: ENLIL IN THE FĀRA PERIOD
100
corpus, similar descriptions of Enlil are attested in texts written in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography as well. Krebernik has transcribed Obv. Col. 2 lines 5-9 of IAS 113, UDKIŠ.NUN GAL nu-nám-NAGAR GAL du11-TUKU DU6-GAG-GAG UD UNU-ta LAGAB ki UD-ta LAGAB, as dEn-líl en nu-nam-nir en du11-ga nu-gi4-gi4 an ki-ta bad ki an-ta bad, which may be understood as, “Enlil, lord Nunamnir, the En, his utterance cannot be countered, separated Heaven from Earth, separated Earth from Heaven.”281 The passage du11-ga nu-gi4-gi4 LAK809 nu LAK809 in the prologue of the Zà-mì Hymns needs to be understood in its context which is expressed in contemporary sources by the more specific du11-ga nu-gi4-gi4 an ki-ta bad ki an-ta bad. The prologue of the Zà-mì Hymns may thus join with the quoted UD.GAL.NUN text IAS 113 to attest to that, in addition to Enlil being the “princely lord” described in the Keš Temple Hymn, he was at about the same time cosmologically considered the deity who separated Heaven from Earth and Earth from Heaven, a most significant role in the creation of the world. It is not clear whether the eulogy for Nippur as dur-an-ki in the prologue of the Zà-mì Hymns was a consequence of Enlil its deity being the one who separated Heaven from Earth. But Nippur as dur-an-ki is certainly to be understood in the same context of IAS 113 as well as the rest of the Zà-mì hymns.282 For our purpose, the suggestion that may be drawn here is that, at the time when Enlil was regarded the separator of Heaven from Earth, and the “princely lord” who “came forth in royalty”, the city of Nippur was naturally the focus of the political ideology surrounding Enlil, the patron deity of the city by this time. The fact that the Zà-mì hymns are found from the site Abū Ṣalābīḫ, furthermore, suggests that the political relevance of Enlil and Nippur was interregional and was not confined to
155: “dessen Wort nicht eingeschränkt und nicht erweitert werden kan”. 281 Krebernik 1998, p. 321, n. 805. 282 It is here not legitimate to take Babylon and Marduk in the epic of Enūma Eliš as the model. The translation of lines 10-14 of the prologue by Westenholz 2000 may have to be subject to the criticism offered by Michalowski 1998, p. 239.

the city of Nippur.
2.3.4 Enlil in Ebla Literary Texts
The destruction of Tell Mardikh, ancient Ebla, has been previously attributed to the Sargonic king Narām-Sîn but more recently a dating of the destruction before Sargon and an attribution of it to ḪI-da’ar of Mari at the time of Lugalzagesi has been proposed.283 It seems to be more expected to discuss the Ebla corpus in the next chapter if considering the chronological order here followed. However, the connection between the Eblaite literary and lexical texts and those from especially Abū Ṣalābīḫ, which resulted from the close relationship of Ebla with central Babylonian scribal traditions, makes it better to introduce them here.284 The history of the transmission of the texts from Babylonia to Ebla is too much unclear so that I will confine myself to a few observations.285
283 Archi and Biga 2003, p. 35, attributed the destruction of Ebla to ḪI-da’ar king of Mari at the time of Lugalzagesi. The synchronisms between Ebla, Mari and other Babylonian cities may be found in Table I in Archi and Biga 2003, pp. 6-7. 284 Literacy was perhaps limited to the palace in Ebla, unlike the situation in Babylonia, as according to Postgate 1988, pp. 113–114. The scribal connection is most clearly expressed by the syllabic duplicates of lexical texts SF 081, SF 015 in the Eblaite MEE 3 062, MEE 3 063 respectively. The Ebla texts include also ARET 5 023. Note IAS 278 from Abū Ṣalābīḫ was indeed transcribed syllabically in ARET 5 020-021 from Ebla. Visicato 2000, p. 8, n. 25, noted that the recording techniques of the royal archives of Ebla resemble those of Babylonia, with connections especially with Kiš and Mari. Lambert 1989, p. 3, regards the Šamaš Hymn as “an Old Akkadian work of mythology, not a text reflecting the ideology of Ebla or Syria.” 285 Archi 1992 was not concerned with the social contexts of the lexical texts. In fact, the social context of the entire corpus of Early Dynastic Lexical and Literary sources remains largely unknown. Mander 1995 attempted to achieve a chronology of the ED IIIa literary corpus including Ebla texts, drawing on colophons and tablet designs. For the textual history of ED Lú E, the Names and Professions List, and of the Šamaš Hymn, see especially Krebernik 1992.

The name Enlil is known at Ebla in literary or lexical texts only (TM.75.G.2195, TM.75.G.1501, TM.75.G.1519, TM.75.G.1619, TM.75.G.2459, VE 802).286 This is a fact not to be ignored as sometimes been so. It may be noted that the title a.mu dingir.dingir.dingir in Ebla Semitic contexts, to be understood as “father of gods”, of di-li-lu is analogous to the Sumerian ab-ba-dingir-dingir-ré-ne from the South and may indeed be linked with the latter (ARET 5 001, ARET 5 003).287 Another point to note is that, É di-li-lu in Ebla was likely synonymous with the phrase èš UDGAL.NUN in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography and the Eblaitic É di-li-lu clearly had a twin 2 KÁ i-li-lu, “doors of Illilu”, built with bricks, as according to narrative texts in Akkadian found from the site.288
286 VE 802 is edited in Pettinato 1982, p. 290. For the attestations of i-li-lu see Pomponio and Xella 1997, pp. 170–171, with references to Pettinato 1979 for Eblaite Sumerian sources, to Krebernik 1992 for the earliest Semitic literature, the Šamaš Hymn and the Nisaba Hymn. Krebernik 1996, p. 13, recognized TM.75.G.1601 as an incantation. Note also that in the place of the personal name En-na-URU in the Šamaš Hymn from Ebla we have the name dEn-líl in the Abū Ṣalābīḫ source, as recognized in Lambert 1989, pp. 15-16, showing how little is known of the earliest “literature”. 287 Cf. Part III: Enlil in the Pre-Sargonic Period
The history of Mesopotamia from the later part of the Early Dynastic III Period to the time before Narām-Sîn the Sargonic king, fundamentally important as it must be, has not become a history of peoples, or even a history of kings, since no clear sequence of events can be established.289 In the absence of even an agreed political chronology in which events may be located, the history of Enlil will inevitably remain elusive. The documentation from this time, on the other side, is arguably easier to understand than that of earlier times but is by no means sufficient and balanced.290 I present in this chapter the attestations of Enlil as can be gleaned from scattered sources, but will pay particular attention to texts from Nippur, though they are mostly deprived of archaeological contexts
289 The Sumerian King List (SKL) is useless for the later part of the Early Dynastic III Period and the beginning of the Sargonic Period on the detailed level. Cf. Jacobsen 1939, Hallo 1963, Nissen 1965, Lucacs and Vegso 1975, Michalowski 1983, Geller 1987, Wilcke 1988a, Wilcke 1989, Vincente 1990, Wilcke 1990, Klein 1991, Young 1991, Steiner 1992, Vincente 1995, Steinkeller 2003b, major works on this “historical” text. I would like to express my sincerest thanks to Dr McMahon here for lending me her dissertation manuscript on the transition from the Early Dynastic to the Old Akkadian Period, McMahon 1993, and for her assistance over the years. Her most valuable work is now available as McMahon 2005. 290 Bauer 1998, pp. 432–433, after briefly introducing the textual sources from other sites, presents only the Girsu sources for “Der vorsargonische Abschnitt der mesopotamischen Geschichte.” Westenholz 2002, pp. 23–24, estimated that the rate of availability to us of cuneiform administrative texts as about one out of 20,000 which furthermore is unevenly distributed by random chance.

due to the sorry methods of record-keeping in excavations conducted in the early years of Assyriology.291 The development of Enlil from the Fāra Period to the Pre-Sargonic Period is impossible to elucidate in the absence of a reliable relative chronology so that only passing notes are provided for the diachronic development of Enlil on the more detailed level. Regarding the problems in relative chronology and the difficult situation of primary sources in this time, Gelb’s warning is especially relevant, the nectar of gods cannot be known without knowing the daily bread of the people.292

In addition, 104 administrative texts from Zabalam in the vicinity of Umma are known through clandestine excavations in the late twenties and early thirties of the last century. These texts were probably from the archive of the temple of Inanna, Ezikalama, but were only a small portion of the total archive. The texts were dated to years 28–30 while according to the Sumerian King List Lugalzagesi ruled 25 years only. It cannot be ruled out that the texts that are dated 28–30 years may be attributed to an unknown predecessor of Lugalzagesi at Umma.301 The Pre-Sargonic Girsu administrative corpus remains the largest available from southern Babylonia from the time, though probably the second largest if considering the alleged but unavailable Adab corpus. These Pre-Sargonic texts from Girsu had been the basis of the influential “Temple State” theory.302 The majority of them were compiled probably over a period of some 20 years spanning the reigns of three Lagašite rulers, Enentarzi, Lugalanda and Uruinimgina. The texts were republished and studied by Selz for the local pantheon of Lagaš, for which purpose it was reported that 1,752 administrative texts were utilized.303 I will not give full references from Girsu for the attestations of personal names and will use Selz’ collection if not analysis.304 The largest corpus of cuneiform administrative texts from the entire cuneiform world and dated to this time is however from Ebla, 15,000 in total if counting fragments, when Mari, Tell Beydar, Tell Brak and Tell Bi’a added significantly into the northern textual repertoire through more recent excavations.305 I am not going to discuss the administrative

practice in the last chapter but is reiterated here for convenience: If there is no other prosopographical information associated with the attestation, the siglum of the text in which the attestation is found will be put in subcategory a. If the attestation is followed or preceded by titles or other kind of prosopographical information, the titles or other information will be quoted together with the text under subcategory b. The subcategory c. is designated for those termed parallel names, namely those which were not composed with Enlil but except the Enlil element they had the same elements in the same grammatical structure. In the listing below and in order to save space, texts from Nippur, except for the few royal inscriptions included here, are quoted without repeating the provenance. The provenances of texts from the other sites will be provided in brackets following the siglum of the text in each case. If a name is relatively certain to be understood in Akkadian, it will be typed in italic, though this does not mean that the names typed otherwise are certainly to be read in Sumerian.

PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD 115
As seen above in the listing, though translations have been provided with the best effort for the names listed, they are again tentative ones. The reason is even more complicated in this case than in the last chapter. Enlil as a theophoric element was widely used in the onomasticons of different Babylonian cities during this time, but the names in different cities, even identical in written form, probably meant different things. It may be tempting, for instance, to regard the ending –le always as of the Sumerian ergative ending and -lá as the genitive in every case. But when these names are seen in the light of their geographical contexts, we at once come to realize that such interpretations do not always apply to the uses of these endings. Apparently, there existed regional variations or dialects of Sumerian behind these names. The name Lugal-dEn-líl-le may be reasonably analysed as Lugal dEn.líl.e as an abbreviated verbal sentence in which Enlil takes the ergative ending and the verb may even be ki-ág, as given in Lugal-AN-né-ki-ág, “the king whom AN loves”. I cannot claim with the same confidence, however, that the –le ending in Dumu-Ur-dEn-líl-le from Nippur must have resulted from the ergative, nor that of A-ba-dEn-líl-le known from Girsu.318 The variation in the writing of grammatical elements in the Enlil names collected above opens the question of whether the distribution of Enlil in personal names in the different cities was representative of the cultural diversity in southern Mesopotamia during the Pre-Sargonic era. The use of different dialects may be considered as a symptom of cultural diversity but the degree of diversity and the particular ways in which different traditions differed from one another are not definable. In comparative terms, nevertheless, it may be observed of the names listed above that Nippur and Adab shared much more in common concerning Enlil in terms of its onomatic contexts than that between those from Nippur and Lagaš. The tradition associated with Enlil in Lagaš must be quite different from that in Nippur and Adab, since in addition to
318 Selz 1995, p. 131, analyzed all –le endings, of course all from the Girsu corpus, as the ergative. In my perception, he was perhaps too optimistic in doing so. For changes of official language in Early Dynastic Lagaš, see recently Krispijn 2000.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
116
the grammatical difference in the Enlil names, the name elements that are combined with Enlil show also certain biases. The Šul- names seem only known from Girsu. If taken into consideration that the Girsu names were perhaps only representative of the last decades of Old Sumerian Lagaš and that Enlil was attested in the canal name dEn-líl-pà-da as early as the time of Ur-dNanše, the differences in the Lagašite names from those in Nippur may be interpreted as resulting from a regionalization of an early but common Enlil tradition shared by both Lagaš and Nippur.319
3.1.1.1 lugal in Pre-Sargonic Personal Names
Westenholz observed that lugal for “king”, written as GAL+LÚ but sometimes GAL.LU!, was frequently attested in the Pre-Sargonic Nippur onomasticon, while on the other hand, no known tradition attributed any king to Nippur. The lugal names were interpreted to have reflected the general Sumerian concept of kingship and it was considered possible that lugal in some of the names referred not to the earthly ruler but to the deity Enlil or An, the “divine” kings. Regarding lugal, Westenholz suggests that “[p]articularly interesting are the names linking the king with the cult, as it seems that the cultic functions performed by the king are largely the same as those known from Ur III sources.”320 In the 32 names attested in the “Sargon Texts” from Nippur there is only one name that contained Enlil, dEn-líl-alx-sa6, and with a lugal name in parallel from the same group of texts. The entire corpus of OSP 1 and ECTJ recorded some 20 names composed with Enlil, in some of which Enlil also shared identical contexts with lugal. The verbal meanings of these personal names containing lugal do not appear to tell whether the worldly ruler or the divine “king” or “kings” were meant. It is true that some of the personal names do speak of the cultic function of the earthly
319 Note that the Lagašite pantheon, according to Selz 1990, Selz 1995, may have undergone considerable reorganization in times from Ur-dNanše to Uruinimgina. 320 Westenholz 1975b, p. 8.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD 117
king, but whether these personal names can be so selectively used for the interpretation of “the” earthly king rather than a more conceptual “kingship” that could be associated with both the earthly ruler and with the divine ruler remains an open question. In my perception, therefore, except that in the few “cultic function” names Westenholz singled out, it is difficult to distinguish the word lugal in the other names containing this element as of more specifically the worldly or the divine.321 The interpretation of the lugal element in these personal names cannot be based on such a priori assumptions and has to consider the obvious fact that the ancients used the same word lugal for both the worldly and the divine ruler. The somehow unusual popularity of the lugal names found in PreSargonic Nippur might however shed light on the interpretation of the unusual popularity of lugal names in the Pre-Sargonic texts from Ur. In the 180 personal names recognized from the UET 2S tablets, 28 names contained the element lugal.322 It seems to me that the name giving practice of the population in both of the cities of Ur and Nippur was especially sensitive to the fact that their places were the homes of some lugals. If, based on the observation above, lugal could at least in some personal names refer to the “divine” king, which helps account for the high popularity of lugal names in Nippur, the particular “divine” king in Nippur and Ur cannot be assumed to be the same figure. In the personal names from Ur, most tellingly, there is no attestation of Enlil as the theophoric element. This fact at least would suggest that Enlil was not the divine king there in the city of Ur. I would assume Enlil was the “divine” king in Nippur in some of the Nippurian personal names, but the local divine king in Ur was most likely
321 Westenholz 1975b, pp. 7–8, observed that the names containing lugal spoke of qualities like “strength, dominion, lordship”, “wisdom”, “justice, protection”, “kindness and care”, also “cultic functions”. Ibid., p. 8: “A very similar picture is given of the queen, nin, perhaps with proportionally less emphasis on strength and more on kindness and care for the individuals.” 322 Alberti and Pomponio 1986, p. 18.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
118
An. The attestation of the names Lugal-AN, Lugal-dEn-líl, Lugal-AN-né, Lugal-dEn-líl-le from this time testify to the fact that lugal was by then associated with Enlil in a way somehow similar to its association with An. The easiest interpretation of this similarity is that, An was at this time another divine ruler recognized, together with Enlil, across southern Babylonia. It is worth noting here that the Semitic Aš-dar also shared identical contexts with Enlil in some personal names, though importantly the association of Aš-dar with lugal was unlike that with Enlil or An.
3.1.2 Nippur and Lagaš Offering Lists
ECTJ 033, ECTJ 082, ECTJ 084-086, ECTJ 154, ECTJ 160-161, OSP 1 016, OSP 1 105–106 had been with different degrees of certainty identified as offering lists recording offering deliveries to deities and temples.323 In order to elucidate the cultic environments of Enlil in the Pre-Sargonic Period, an examination of the offering lists from Nippur and elsewhere is inevitable. The following is a collection of sources from Nippur and Lagaš respectively, as they are most relevant to our questions. The Nippur texts are grouped, and numbered, under the royal figure who supervised the offerings recorded in each text, so to serve better our discussions. The collection does not exhaust the available offering lists from Nippur as we are concerned only with particular questions, for which purposes more texts however may be quoted. As it is not necessary to quote in full the often broken texts, only descrptions with the most relevant information are provided:
Lugal-zà-ge-si 1. ECTJ 082 recorded one black ox brought to the Ninurta temple as food-offering for the new moon festival, sag-ITI.SAR, in iti-zíz-a mu Lugal-zà-ge-si nam-lugal šu-ba-ti, “the year when Lugalzagesi assumed kingship.”

The ox, gu4, was ki-Ur-ra-ni-ta, “from the place of Ur-ra-ni”, but no identification of the recipient of the offering is given. 2. nidba Sar-um-GI-kam and was dated with Sargon’s year name recorded offerings from Adabki. This is reminiscent of OSP 1 016 where offerings were termed as nidba Adabki. Lugal-an-DU, known in OSP 1 117 as lúganun, “manager of the storehouse”, was the person passing offerings both in OSP 1 016 and ECTJ 154. The combination of the information would therefore date at least ECTJ 033, ECTJ 154, OSP 1 016, and ECTJ 084–086 to the reign of Sargon, though otherwise not every offering list can be dated with any certainty. We have also En-šà-kuš-an-na of Uruk and Lugal-kisal-si of Uruk in the Nippur corpus making offerings, too.325 I noticed above that Nippur and Adab seemed to have shared much more in common concerning Enlil at least than that between Nippur and Lagaš. In fact and also from the personal names listed in the last section, it seems the cities of Nippur and Adab may have had frequent exchanges of personnel during the Pre-Sargonic Period, since some of the personal names known from Adab most likely referred to the same people known in the Nippur sources. The offering lists from Nippur here, especially ECTJ 033, ECTJ 154, OSP 1 016, and ECTJ 084-086, those which may date to the reign of Sargon, would provide a part of the historical background of the exchange of personnel between Nippur and Adab and of the cultic links between the two cities, if this was truly the case. It is difficult, of course, from the above sources to conclude already that king Sargon actually initiated this close connection since the link between the two cities must be ancient. But by supervising offerings to Nippur from Adab, Sargon the Akkadian would have willingly enhanced

3.1.3 The Meaning of èš in Nippur
It was somehow surprising that Enlil was nowhere recorded directly as a recipient of offerings in the lists from Nippur.328 This surprising fact may be explained through clarifying the meaning of èš. The designation èš, written as AB, is frequently attested in the offering lists from Nippur, in which it is always followed by a grammatical ending -šè (ECTJ 033, written TÚG; ECTJ 085, written –šè; OSP 1 016, written –šè; and ECTJ 154, written -šè). OSP 1 085 records the offering of 2 aktumtúg ì-du8 èš-kam, “2 aktum clothes for the door keeper of èš.” OSP 1 137 menitons two titles in relation to èš, PA.ITI?.UD-èš and sukkal-èš.
326 Cf. DP 043 in Allotte de la Fuÿe 1912. 327 Cf. Selz 1995, p. 127. 328 The known recipients of offerings included: èš (ETCJ 033, ECTJ 085, OSP 1 016, ECTJ 154), dNin-urta (ETCJ 033, ECTJ 082 as é-dNin-urta, ECTJ 085, ECTJ 154), Dingir-maḫ (ETCJ 033, ECTJ 085, ECTJ 154), dEn-ki (ETCJ 033, ECTJ 154), dNin-tinugx-ga (ETCJ 033, ECTJ 154), AN? (ETCJ 033), dEn-líl-zi (ETCJ 033, OSP 1 016), dEn-ur (ETCJ 033), d?Utu (ETCJ 033), dIr-da (ETCJ 033, ECTJ 085?, ECTJ 154), x-[x]-bàndada (ECTJ 085), dInanna (ECTJ 154), dNusku (ECTJ 154). 122
From among the Pre-Sargonic personal names, Ur-èš is attested in OSP 1 063, ECTJ 019 and ECTJ 159 from Nippur, and in TBIA 013, TBIA 015, TBIA 146 from Adab. In addition, the name Ur-èš-KID-lá known in Nippur can only be read as Ur-èš-/lil/-lá as otherwise the final –lá of the name would be unexplained (OSP 1 056, OSP 1 153). It is very tempting here to identify this person with the Ur-èš-KID-lá ab-ba-uru in the inscription Bar 1 and the Ur-èš-KID-lá šu-i known from Adab (TBIA 043, TBIA 064).329 The attestation of the title ì-du8 with the term èš known from OSP 1 085 suggests that in Nippur there was only one èš, which was in a form of spatial enclosure so to require a “door-keeper”. This èš as an institution in Nippur was specific enough to the locals that the ì-du8 of it did not have to be further explained when used to designate an individual or an occupation at least in the text concerned.330 The existence of the official titles sukkal-èš and PA.ITI?.UD-èš would suggest that the èš must be an administrative body.331 The affiliation of the èš, to which authority the institution was ascribed, is not readily deduced but it seems that the èš could best be understood as belonging not to any of the individual deities hosted in the city but to the city which was by then probably already centred upon the cult of Enlil. Westenholz considered that the èš recorded in the texts quoted above may be understood as a “sanctuary” and was architecturally a part of a temple complex called Ekur in the time of Sargon.332 The first part of his view is likely but the second part concerning the architecture of èš

Westenholz 1975b, p. 15, on OSP 1 016, and Westenholz 1975a, p. 27, on ECTJ 033. Architecturally or inscriptionally there is however no evidence of the architecture of Ekur is found in situ for times before Narām-Sîn. Cf. Gibson, Hansen and Zettler 2001, and McCown and Haines 1967, pp. 1-2.

and of Ekur is problematic in a nuanced sense. It can be inferred from the opaque administrative sources that the structure of the èš was guarded in a way not unlike that of é but the architectural features of the specific “building” called èš cannot be so easily determined. Such-Gutiérrez drew on the form of the sign èš, AB, to relate èš to a kind of Ziqqurrat and effectively also associated èš with é-kur, “der Name des Tempelcomplexes von Enlil (é-dEn-líl)”, so that the structure of èš in his words, “gemeint ist der Vorläufer der Ziqqurrat des Ekur”.333 The use of the sign AB in the city names of Ur and Larsa, ŠEŠ.AB and UD.AB respectively, may seem to confirm that èš in Nippur was actually in the form of a Ziqqurat. But the problem is, as Such-Gutiérrez noticed, the equation of É-kur and the temple of Enlil, é-dEn-líl, is earliest attested in texts as late as from the classical Sargonic Period.334 It is therefore no more than an educated guess the èš in the Pre-Sargonic sources was the “Vorläufer”, in the architectural sense, of the Ur III Ziqqurrat whose architectural relationship to the Ekur is known to us. In whichever architectural relationship to the later structure known as the Ekur, nevertheless, the èš in the sources would have referred to the same as the èš-EN.LÍL(É)ki referred to in the Barton Cylinder where the editors understood it as “the sacred area of Nippur”.335 It may be called simply èš in the context of offering and other economic activities but would be more fully named as èš-EN.LÍL(É)ki in ritual/mythic contexts. It referred to the major oikos of dEn-líl(É) and simultaneously to that of the city, comparable to the role of the èš of Sùd in Šuruppak.336
333 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 35, n. 56. 334 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 35, n. 56. 335 Alster and Westenholz 1994, p. 32, based on Yoshikawa 1985. 336 In Pre-Sargonic Lagaš, it seems èš-Gír-su was equivalent to é-dNin-gír-su, such as in Urn 27 where èš-Gír-su was attested in parallel to é-dNanše and more importantly èš-Gír-su and é-dNin-gír-su did not seem to appear in identical contexts. The referent of èš-Gír-su must be larger than a room, as indicated in Ukg 38, 80 gišmes 3-kam-ma ús-bi kiri6-é-šà-ga-ta Uru-inim-gi-na lugal-Lagaški-ke4 šà-èš-ka ì-du 10, “Uruinimgina king of Lagaš brought 80 Mes trees of the third



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
124
The city names of Nippur and Šuruppak were written EN.LÍL(É)ki and SÙD(SU.KUR.RU)ki respectively, which are both identical to their patrons’ names except for the determinative. This could not have been purely accidental. The format of the offering lists from Pre-Sargonic Nippur, and to do with Adab (ECTJ 086, OSP 1 016), is also reminiscent of the offering lists from Fāra discussed in the last chapter. The oikos of dEn-líl(É) that was called èš-EN.LÍLki would have been the institution associated with emedu dEn-líl, “house born slaves of Enlil” (OSP 1 109, ECTJ 119), géme dEn-líl-me, “female servants of Enlil” (OSP 1 023), and estates such as udu dEn-líl-kam, “sheep for Enlil” (OSP 1 109), aša5-dEn-líl (OSP 1 113). Such an èš in the Nippur texts may have been related to Ninurta in a way not unlike the èš of Šuruppak to Enlil in Šuruppak during the ED IIIa Period. Ninurta would be an important deity in Nippur already, but he did not seem to have a separate èš much earlier than the end of the Early Dynastic Period.337 The term èš in the Nippur personal name Ur-èš, furthermore, would have referred to the same institution. It is also very likely that the phrase èš-KID-lá in the personal name Ur-èš-KID-lá was actually the abbreviated writing of èš-EN.LÍL(É)ki, though the later may need to be understood as èš-Nibruki. If the oikos of dEn-líl and of EN.LÍLki was one and the same, it would be at least imaginable that èš-Nibruki was read as èš dEn-líl, then èš-/lil/-lá in certain contexts. If so, èš-KID-lá would have retained the earlier graphic realization of the element KID, instead of É, in the name of Enlil.
3.1.4 The KID of dNin-KID in Nippur
1. dNin-lilx(KID) -an-dùl, “Ninlil is protection”.338 a. OSP 1 085; c. AN-an-dùl (OSP 1 023), Lugal-an-dùl (OSP 1 028,
class from the Eša garden into the sacred area in the tenth year”. 337 Cf. Westenholz 1999, pp. 95–96, and Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 48. 338 an-dùl, ṣulūlu, is known to mean “roof, canopy, shelter, aegis, protection”.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD 125
ECTJ 010, TBIA 005, TBIA 115), dUtu-an-dùl (OSP 1 073), dEn-ki-an-dùl (TBIA 074, TBIA 151, Adab). 2. dNin-lilx(KID) -lá a. OSP 1 023. 3. dNin-/lil/(É/KID)-ama-mu, “Ninlil is my mother?”. b. dNin-É-ama-mu (OSP 1 023, OSP 1 024), dNin-KID-ama-mu (OSP 1 023); c. dEZINU-ama-mu (OSP 1 023), Nin-ama-mu (OSP 1 023, OSP 1 024), Usar-ama-mu (OSP 1 023).
If the èš as discussed above was identical to èš-EN.LÍLki in the Barton Cylinder, and was associated with Enlil via the city, it is again surprising that the attestations of Ninlil in the Nippurian corpus are only found in isolated personal names, as collected above following our listing rules. The reading of the divine name as nin-/lil/ in the corpus seems secure enough, especially in the personal name dNin-KID-lá where KID has to have a value /lil/.339 It is important to note, however, that in the -an-dùl names the elements in identical contexts with Ninlil included An, lugal, and dUtu, entirely compatible with the parallel name elements with Enlil in association with kingship. The parallel names of dNin-/lil/-ama-mu, on the other hand, have an entirely different set of theophoric elements. dNin-/lil/-ama-mu is in some cases written dNin-É-ama-mu and in some other cases dNin-KID-ama-mu.340 The writing dNin-É in dNin-/lil/-ama-mu was perhaps occasionally interchangeable with dNin-KID.341 The number of attestations may be too small to support any valid observations. But it may be not pure chance that in the -an-dùl names, dNin-KID shares the onomastic contexts with figures including An, lugal, or dUtu, deities who are securely known to have shared identical contexts with dEn-LÍL(É). I proposed in the last chapter that dNin-KID was either
339 Jacobsen 1989, p. 267, n. 1. 340 Westenholz 1975b, p. 109 and pp. 135–143, autographs of OSP 1 023–024. Note also dNin-líl (É)-[x] (ECTJ 034), and dNin-líl(É)-ama (ECTJ 034, ECTJ 044, ECTJ 191). 341 The names attested in OSP 1 023 and OSP 1 024 are often repetitive.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
126
the feminized AN.EN.KID by origin or at this time already the wife of Enlil in the local tradition of Nippur and perhaps elsewhere. The -an-dùl names composed with Ninlil agree that dNin-KID was either the “heir” of AN.EN.KID or was legitimate to partake in the protective power of Enlil.
3.2 Enlil in Pre-Sargonic Royal Inscriptions
The availability of the Pre-Sargonic corpus of votive and building inscriptions is biased as almost half of them are from the Lagaš region.342 Adab, Kiš, Nippur, Umma, Ur and Uruk in Babylonia, Ešnunna, Tell Agrab, Hafaja in the Diyala and noticeably Mari on the Euphrates contributed the rest.343 The corpus is too large and too small at the same time, since the inscriptions are in most of the cases short labels. And when they do contain narratives, the events recounted are difficult to put into modern historical terms. The dialects in which these inscriptions were written, and the styles in which they were composed, are all replete with regional variations.
3.2.1 dam-gàr in Nippur
The Ur-dEn-líl énsi-Nibruki in Urenl 2 must be identical to the homonymous person with the same title énsi-Nibruki in Urenl 1, for whose life A-ba-dEn-líl son of Lugalnibedu dam-gàr made a dedication to dNin-KID. An Ur-dEn-líl dam-gár-gal is known from AnNip 1, dedicated to dMÙŠ.ÍL (dInanna-eden?). In all likelihood, this latter person would be the same as Ur-dEn-líl énsi-Nibruki.344 The énsi was associated with a dam-gàr in
342 Cf. Bauer 1998, p. 432: “Von den 376 von H. Steible gesammelten Bau- und Weihinschriften stamen 183, also fast die Hälfte, aus Girsu (Telloh) und Lagaš (al-Hibā).” All my sigla of ED royal inscriptions follow the FAOS system. 343 The 41 Mari inscriptions were edited in Gelb and Kienast 1990 for the reason that they were written in the Akkadian language, though historically they must be considered with their roughly contemporary Sumerian counterparts. 344 Cf. Westenholz 1999, pp. 60–61, n. 245, on CBS 8417.

Urenl 1. And if he was the same dam-gàr-gal in AnNip 1, the suggestion is that, the énsi of Nippur and his office at this time seemed to have much to do with merchants or mercantile activities.345
3.2.2 The Office sanga-dEn-líl
Lugal-ḫur-sag (AnNip 25), Ì-dì-lum (AnNip 37), Zur-zur (AnNip 64), and perhaps Ì-lum-al-sù (AnNip 46) were known in Pre-Sargonic sources from Nippur as sanga-dEn-líl.346 It is noteworthy that at least one of the personal names bearing this title is to be read as Semitic rather than Sumerian but beyond this the functionality of the office in Nippur is unknown.347 Such-Gutiérrez compared the official title sanga and šabra/ ugula-é in Sargonic and Ur III sources to conclude that “sanga ist auf den Tempelbereich beschränkt, während dies auf šabra u. ugula-é nicht zutrifft” and that “sanga scheint vom König eingesetzt zu werden.”348 In Šuruppak in the ED IIIa Period, as discussed in the last chapter, sanga did not seem to be very “priestly”, thus better to be transcribed as umbisag in most of the cases, while sanga-GAR dEn-líl was one of the more “priestly” sanga-GARs. In Šuruppak the “priestly” sanga-GARs were active players in the administration of the city and were possibly associated with the temple institution é-géme. If this observation of the situation in ED IIIa Šuruppak is combined with the evidence from the lexical list ED Lú E, the situation of sanga dEn-líl in Pre-Sargonic Nippur may become clearer. The relative positions of sanga and sanga-GAR respectively in the list ED Lú E were probably
345 dam-gàr, Akkadian tamkāru, has to be associated with the title dam-gàr-gal, though the latter term is not known independently in lexical materials. 346 In addition, Su6-mú dam sanga-dEn-líl is known in AnNip 47. 347 AnNip 37 dì:lum:ì was read as ì-dì-lum, “idē’ilum”, “god knows”, in Steible and Behrens 1982b, p. 246, but note Goetze 1970, p. 45, read it as ì-ti-lum. 348 The “Tempelbereich” could neither be a purely “priestly” domain. determined following a hierarchical order, namely the earlier entries in the list would be titles of higher significance. The suggestion is that, Enlil seemed to have a more central role in ED IIIb Nippur than he had in ED IIIa Šuruppak, since a higher and less “priestly” office is now known here in his name. In the Sargonic Period, the office sanga-dEn-líl was associated with the central government of the palace and its holders were probably appointed directly by the king, as will be shown in the next chapter, while the question here is whether the Pre-Sargonic Nippurian sanga-dEn-líl was appointed by the local ruler, that of Nippur, or by some rulers of a larger region. This cannot be directly deduced from the contemporary sources. The situation, however, may be indirectly inferred through a look at the office sanga-Uru-sag-rig7 (ḪÚB.DU) known from contemporary Nippurian year names. The year name mu Íl sanga-Uru-sag-rig7 al-[dab5]-a (ECTJ 110), must be identical to mu sanga-Uru-sag-rig7 ì-dab5-ba-a (ECTJ 222). In ECTJ 110, it is recorded that 1 bar-dul5 1 túgaktum ki-lugal-ta LugaldEn-líl-le mu-mu4 iti-gurx-ku5 mu Íl sanga-Uru-sag-rig7 al-[dab5]-a, “one kusītum-garment, and one outer garment were given to Lugalenlile as clothing from the king. The month of Addaru, the year when Il was chosen as sanga of Urusagrig.” ECTJ 222 also mentioned aktumtúg when the same Lugalenlile was involved.349 OSP 1 099 recorded 2 ḫa-pù-daurudu ki Íl Bàd-Ur-dEn-líl-ta, the deliverer of which, Lugal-dEn-líl, was perhaps identical with the Lugal-dEn-líl-le above. The text OSP 1 099 was dated with the month name iti-šu-numun. ECTJ 211 recorded activities to do with A-zu-zu the lunga, “brewer”, and was dated with another year name, <mu X> énsi-Nibruki Uru-sag-rig7ki-da ì-da-tuš-a.350 Westenholz commented on the year name of ECTJ 211 that, “[i]t is somewhat difficult to see why the ensi should start besieging one of his own towns”, therefore “perhaps other meanings of GN-da tuš besides ‘to besiege GN’ should be considered.”351 The interpretation of the term tuš

The Sumerian word gidru in the title gidru-sum-ma-dEn-líl-lá must symbolically fall into the semantic field of á in á-sum-ma-dEn-líl, which must mean generally the ruling power. The use of gidru in the title only
356 For the “religious titles” of the Lagašite kings, see Selz 1995, pp. 360–361. 357 The ruler in AnLag 26, based on this observation, is likely to be Eannatum.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
132
of Entemena was probably the result of the expansion of Entemena’s power to Nippur where he might have had participated in some rite metaphorically receiving the gidru of Enlil (Ent 32), as the matter of concern denoted by the term gidru was likely the same as that mentioned in the Barton Cylinder, gidru-kù, which will be introduced later. Ningirsu could also grant ruling power to the Lagašite rulers but the ruling power designated as á which both Ningirsu and Enlil were capable of giving to the rulers has to be differentiated from the more specific gidru of Nippur. The title mu-pà-da-dEn-líl may be interpreted in the light of the “Determination of Royal Fate” ritual known from Ur III sources but probably practiced already in ED times.358 The attestation of mu-pà-dadNin-gír-su in Ean 22 of the same ruler suggests that rituals involving the giving of the throne name of the same ruler could take place more than once with different deities served as the name-giver. The juxtaposition of the title á-sum-ma-dNin-gír-su with mu-pà-da-dEn-líl suggests that the granting of the ruling power was in a different context than that of the throne name. The fact that mu-pà-da-dEn-líl and the epithet lugal-Lagaški were never attested in the same text renders it difficult to relate the giving of the throne name by Enlil as the pretext to the legitimacy of being called lugal since the latter ritual context would be different again from that of obtaining the throne name.
3.2.3.2 énsi-gal-dEn-líl and énsi-kal-ga-dEn-líl
Lugalzagesi had the most elaborate “religious titles” compared to most of the other Pre-Sargonic rulers and it is hard to imagine that such an elaborate list of titles was not quite moving to the ancients (Luzag 1):

“Lugalzagesi, King of Uruk, King of the Land, priest of An, ‘prophet’ of Nisaba, son of Bubu governor of Umma ‘prophet’ of Nisaba, looked on favourably by An king of all lands, the Chief Governor of Enlil, granted wisdom by Enki, chosen by Utu, High Vizier of Sin, General of Utu, provider for Inanna, son born of Nisaba and suckled on the milk of Ninḫursag, man of Mes-sangaunuga, brought up by Ningirim the lady of Uruk, and High Steward of the gods.”359
It is however not possible to decipher every title in the list. What is concerned here are only the titles énsi-gal-dEn-líl and énsi-kal-ga-dEn-líl. We know Ikūn-Šamaš and Isqī-Mari from Pre-Sargonic Mari also had the title énsi-gal-dEn-líl (MP 8, MP 17). The title énsi-gal-dEn-líl, furthermore, finds its closest parallel with the Lagašite royal title énsi-gal-dNin-gír-su which always came along with á-sum-ma-dEn-líl in the Lagašite texts. The combination of evidence here probably suggests that Enlil was in the title of Lugalzagesi considered as a patron deity not unlike that of Ningirsu to Lagaš. The titles énsi-gal-dEn-líl did not come first in the list of “religious titles”, in which išib-An-na seems to be the title Lugalzagesi cherished the most, perhaps due to the archaic link between An and Uruk. In addition, Luzag 2 has a set of titles different from that elaborated in Luzag 1:

The meaning of énsi-kal-ga-dEn-líl and whether it was simply a variant of énsi-gal-dEn-líl are unclear, especially if seen in the apparently different context than that of the latter.361
3.2.4 Titles of Enlil 3.2.4.1 dEn-líl lugal-kur-kur-ra
Enlil’s epithets kur-gal and nu-nam-nir known in the literary texts from the Early Dynastic IIIa Period, most noticeably in the Zà-mì Hymns, were however not attested in Pre-Sargonic royal inscriptions.362 In these royal inscriptions Enlil was known most famously as lugal-kur-kur(-ra), “king of the lands”.363 It is important to find here that the god An had exactly the same title (Table 3.2.1). The same title was used for the god An and for Enlil in the same Vase Inscription of Lugalzagesi, Luzag 1, Col. 1, line 14, An lugal-kur-kur-(a)k and Col. 1 lines 36-37, dEn-líl lugal-kur-kur-ra. An was phrased as a ki-ág-ni, “his beloved father”, of Enlil, in the same text (Col. 3, line 16).364 Lugal-ki-gin-né-du7-du7 from Uruk dedicated to An lugal-kur-kur as well as to dEn-líl lugal-kur-kur in Nippur. Lugal-TAR-si again probably from
360 The translation follows Cooper 1986, p. 95. 361 Note that, in addition to the translation “governor” and “executive” quoted here, Jacobsen 1991, pp. 113–114, saw énsi as “farmer” before the Ur III Period. 362 Note Lambert 1976, p. 230, took nu in nu-nam-nir as standing for nun, not a part of the title. Cf. also Krebernik 1984, p. 280, quoting kur-gal dEn-líl in IAS 392. 363 The meaning of kur-kur in Old Sumerian inscriptions is most likely mātātu, “the lands”, in general terms. The grammatical relationship between lugal and kur-kur is certainly genitive, unlike in the case of é-an-na kur-kur-ra mu-na-diri, “Eanna, surpassing all the lands”, quoted in Behrens and Steible 1983, p. 58. 364 Steible and Behrens 1982b, p. 316, line 14.

In the cone of Entemena, Ent 28-29, from Lagaš, Enlil is so named but the inscription was not itself dedicated to the deity. All of the rulers who had somehow addressed the god An as lugal-kur-kur, including Lugal-ki-gin-né-du7-du7, Lugal-TAR-si, and Lugal-zà-ge-si seem to have some connection with Uruk and/or Ur.365

The deity An is known as the “king of Uruk” in the literary text SF 036 from ED IIIa Fāra, paralleled in ARET 2 020-021 from Ebla.367 I noted earlier that in the personal names from Pre-Sargonic Ur there was no attestation of Enlil as the theophoric element, suggesting that Enlil was foreign and not originally the divine king in the city of Ur in the south. The combination of the evidence seems to suggest that, the kingship of Enlil as accepted in the south at the end of the Early Dynastic Period, especially at the time of Lugalzagesi, was perhaps achieved through an inclusion of the kingship of An originally signified by lugal-kur-kur in the southern tradition, into that of Enlil via a genealogical link Enlil being the beloved son of An. Lugalzagesi perpetuated, if not initiated, such an ideological innovation.
3.2.4.2 dEn-líl(É/KID)-é-ad-da
Entemena repeatedly reports that he had built the temple É-ad-da im-sag-gá (Ent 1, Ent 8–14, Ent 23). Among these reports, Ent 1 is the only one clearly addressing dEn-líl é-ad-da-ka-ra, “to Enlil of Eadda”, thus naming Enlil as the host of the temple. This designation of Enlil together with the name of his temple é-ad-da is clearly different from the title of Enlil, lugal-kur-kur, well known in the inscriptions of the southern rulers. The object of dedication from which Ent 1 is known was a diorite statue and the inscription was named in the text as En-me-te-na dEn-líl-le-ki-ág, “Entemena whom Enlil loves”. The statue was set up by the Lagašite ruler in É-ad-da but oddly enough it was found in the city of Ur. This perhaps can be explained by Entemena’s Grossmachtpolitik that enabled the ruler to install it in Ur, or the object could simply have been booty carried away later by Ur from around Lagaš.368 In addition to Ent 1, the first line mentioning the recipient of dedication of Ent 32, known from vase fragments from Nippur mentioning
lugal [erasedki gin] an ra kur kur, does not make sense. 367 Cf. Krebernik 1998, p. 325, n. 825. 368 See Selz 1992, pp. 200–203, for the Grossmachtpolitik of Entemena.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD 137
[gidru]-maḫ-nam-tar-ra dEn-líl-le EN.LÍLki-ta En-te-me-na-ra mu-na-sum, might be restored as [dEn-líl é-ad-da-ka]-ra, though not entirely certain. It is so restored because the sign before –ra cannot be taken as kur for the more expected dEn-líl lugal-kur-kur-ra in view of the traces, while the dative ending would be –la rather than -ra if the broken part consists only the name of dEn-líl. The restoration [dEn-líl é-ad-da-ka]-ra following the text of Ent 1 of the same ruler is thus the best choice left.369

There seemed to be a terminological difference between Lagaš and the southern cities, particularly Ur and Uruk, namely, dEn-líl-é-ad-da was the Lagašite designation of Enlil when the deity was the recipient of dedication, while dEn-líl lugal-kur-kur-ra was that for rulers from Ur and Uruk (Table 3.2.2).370 This difference of cultic terminology would have
369 Cooper 1986, p. 64, and Selz 1995, p. 127. 370 Note, however, dInanna as nin-kur-kur-ra in Lagašite inscriptions may refer to the dInanna-ib-gal of Lagaš (En I 35). Cf. Selz 1990, p. 120: “Surprisingly, the



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
138
reflected the differences between the configurations of the pantheon by different local traditions. Ent 1, Obv. Col. 3, lines 5–7, dEn-KID-la é-ad-da im-sag-gá mu-na-dù, “[Entemena] built the E’adda-Imsagga for Enlil”, in addition, posits a phonetic question. It is the earliest attestation of the sign KID securely to be raed as /lil/ in the name of Enlil. Significantly however, although dEn-KID-la here is surely to be read as dEn-/lil/-la, the dative ending was written –la instead of the expected –ra.371 We have also a less certain but possible attestation of the dative ending written as –la in Ean 62 from London!,372 in which É instead of KID was used to write the phoneme /lil/. In Sumerian as we know, if followed by the genitive /ak/, the name of Enlil is normally followed by –lá(LAL).373 It seems that, based on the use of –la instead of –ra for the dative ending to the divine name as in the above quoted inscriptions, the value of the final consonant /l/ in the /lil/ syllable of the name of Enlil is more likely that of -la and not that of –lá, at least to the Lagašites.
3.2.5 Divine Titles 3.2.5.1 dNin-gír-su ur-sag-dEn-líl-(lá)
In Pre-Sargonic Lagaš the most popular divine epithet was that of Ningirsu, who was called very frequently ur-sag-dEn-líl-(lá), “the hero of Enlil”, such as in Ean 1, Ean 60, Ean 69, and in more than twenty cases of later date, not counting the duplicates of identifiable inscriptions (for example, Ent 2, En II 1, and Ukg 4). Selz took the title ur-sag-dEn-líl-(lá) to
main temple complex in Lagaš was consecrated to Inanna, the chief goddess of the city of Uruk. Even her temple’s name, é-an-na, is borrowed from her temple in Uruk.” É-an-na is literally used in the name of É-an-na-túm. 371 The -na- infix in the verb needs to be picking up an earlier dative -ra. 372 Cooper 1986, p. 45: “found in the remains of a seventeenth-century London house.” Steible and Behrens 1982a, p. 173, imagined the origin of the inscription as, “wohl Girsu”. 373 Edzard 2003a did not speak of this problem but see Edzard 2003b, p. 46.

support his idea that Ningirsu was at the time of Entemena considered the son of Enlil in Lagaš. But this theory seems difficult to accept. The evidence based on which Selz made his suggestion included the epithet of Enlil ab-ba-dingir-dingir-ré-ne and the temple name É-ad-da.374 The epithet ab-ba-dingir-dingir-ré-ne, “ab-ba of all the gods”, was attested only in Ent 28-29, the cone of Entemena, in the context of the demarcation of the border between Lagaš and Umma, and the relationship between the ab-ba and the other gods as hinted by the meaning of the word ab-ba is by no means clear. In fact, even if the word ab-ba in ab-ba-dingir-dingir-ré-ne did mean “father” rather than “elder”, it is still difficult to say that Enlil was specifically the ab-ba of Ningirsu. The term ur-sag in the title of Ningirsu is qarrādu, “hero”, in lexical sources, but not “son”.375 Nor could the Sumerian word ad-da in the Lagašite temple name É-ad-da introduced above be interpreted as of the sonship of Ningirsu to Enlil. It is true that ad-da normally mean “father” and that the literal meaning of É-ad-da can be legitimately understood as “house of the father”, the fact that it is a proper name would discourage interpreting it as particularly identifying Enlil the host of the temple as the father of Ningirsu. Even if the literal meaning of the temple name did speak of Enlil being considered the ad-da of some important deity, there still lacks evidence to substantiate that he was considered as particularly father of Ningirsu, as this is not explicitly attested in the sources.376

3.2.5.2 dNin-KID šilamx(TÙR)-niga-na-ni dam-dEn-líl
Urz 1 is an inscription on a stone vessel fragment found in Nippur, and datable to late Early Dynastic times. The vessel was dedicated to both Enlil and Ninlil by Ur-zag-è lugal-kiški from Uruk, as it reads: dEn-líl lugal-kur-kur-ra dNin-KID nin-an-ki(KI.AN)-ra šilamx (TÙR)-niga-na-ni dam-dEn-líl-ra Ur-zag-è lugal-Kiški lugal ..., “For Enlil, king of all lands, and Ninlil, queen of heaven and earth, his well-fed cow, the wife of Enlil, Urzage king of Kish, king of Uruk ...”.377 The title of dNin-KID here, nin-an-ki, “queen of heaven and earth”, reminds us of Enlil’s title lugal-an-ki in Lagaš (Ean 1), and nun-an-ki in Uruk (Lukin 7). The phrase –an-ki in this inscription may even be related to the title of Nippur in the Zà-mì Hymns in which EN.LÍL(É)ki dur-an-ki is attested (IAS 266–271, IAS 257–258). The interpretation “heaven and earth” for the phrase -an-ki, however, is uncertain, since the writing Nin-KI.AN-ra could be understood as NIN of KI.AN, the latter of which is actually known in Luzag 1 to be a geographical name.378 The inscription Urz 1 is unique as far as the relationship between Ninlil and Enlil is concerned since it is the only attestation of Ninlil as the wife of Enlil in original Early Dynastic royal inscriptions. In the Ur III copy of the inscription of En-na-il lugal-[Kiš], as noted earlier, Ninlil was attested as the spouse of Enlil also in the context of Ur from the south (ECTJ 219). The relationship between dNin-KID and dEn-líl(É) was known in the ED IIIa Period in Abū Ṣalābīḫ northwest of Nippur (IAS 345). The evidence is scanty but if combined together, these traces would lead to the suggestion that the tradition in which dNin-KID was the spouse of dEn-líl(É) could not have been only known in the south as the Ur sources may be so mistaken. This tradition must have been already alive across
dingir-mu-ke4 u4 dNin-SIG7 gù e-na-dé-a ní mu-ni-tuku [a]-dingir-mu KUM.KU-e a [mu-na-ru], does mean “father”. 377 Cf. 3.2.6 Curse Formulae
The difficult and broken inscription Ean 63 known from fragments of clay vase dedicated to Ningirsu ended with a passage probably to be considered as a curse formula, albeit in the Lagašite tradition (Ean 63):379
Obv. 3,2dNin-gír-sú 3,3ušumgal ḫé 3,4dEn-líl-le 3,5absìn-na-na 3,6mun ḫa-bi-zi-zi 3,7dŠU.KALbroken... 3,10na-na-sum 3,15uru-TE:ME-na 3,16šu ḫé-na-zi
“Let Ningirsu be the dragon! Let Enlil in his furrows allow salt to surface! ... not given to him! Let hands be raised against him in his own city!”
The cone of Entemena somehow also ended with a curse involving Enlil, though even less loosely so taken (Ent 28):
Obv. 6,9lú Ummaki-a 6,10e ki-sur-ra 6,11dNin-gír-su-ka-ka 6,12e ki-sur-ra 6,13dNanše-ka 6,14á zi-šè 6,15a-šà GÁN tùm-dè 6,16am6-ta bal-e-da 6,17lú Ummaki ḫé 6,18lú kur-ra ḫé 6,19dEn-líl-le 6,20ḫé-ḫa-lam-me 6,21dNin-gír-su-ke4 6,22sa-šuš-gal-ni 6,23ù-ni-šu4 6,24šu maḫ gìri maḫ-ni 6,25an-ta ḫé-gá-gá 6,26nam-lú-ul8 uru-na 6,27šu ù-na-zi 6,28šà uru-na-ka 6,29ḫa-né-gaz-e
“If the leader of Umma transgresses the boundary-channel of Ningirsu and the boundary-channel of Nanše, to take away fields by force – no matter if he is leader of Umma or any other leader may Enlil destroy him! May Ningirsu, after casting his great battle net upon him, bring down upon him his giant hands and feet! May the people of his own city, after rising up against him, kill him there within his city!”380
379 Cf. Steible and Behrens 1982b, p. 81. 380 The translation follows Cooper 1986, p. 56.

There are too few cases to ground further observations and these Lagašite curses are not as typical curses as we will encounter in the next chapter. It might be inferred from the wordings quoted above, however, that the functionality of Enlil in the Lagašite tradition had been more closely associated with agricultural fertility at the time of Eannatum, while by the time of Entemena the military tone seems to have become stronger.
3.2.7 The Narrative Parts 3.2.7.1 sa-šuš-gal dEn-líl
