Table 3.3: Deities in the Oath of the Stele of the Vultures (Ean 1)
In the Stele of the Vultures, Ean 1, when Eannatum defeated the “man” of Umma and restored the Gu’edena to Ningirsu’s control, the ruler of Umma was forced to repeat an oath by the names of the gods Enlil, Ninḫursag, Enki, Su’en, Utu, and Ninki. The text describes how the oath was taken by the defeated ruler in the context of the border conflict between Umma and Lagaš, presumably in a way acceptable to both the winning Lagašites and the losing Ummaites. In so far as the Pre-Sargonic votive and building inscriptions are concerned, the description of oath taking is unique since the ritual setting was provided in considerable detail. The description was repetitive in the cases of Enlil, Ninḫursag, Enki, Su’en, and Utu, though with a differently phrased section for Ninki. The inscription is not well preserved and problems remain in the reading and



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD 143
understanding of the text but the description of the setting of the oath taking seems fairly clear.381 I omit dNin-ki from the table above knowing that it was the last deity invoked in the oath taking section.382 In each of the other cases to do with Enlil, Ninḫursag, Enki, Su’en, and Utu respectively, Eannatum gave the great battle net, sa-šuš-gal, of the correspondent deity to the lú of Umma, and made him swear. The lú of Umma swore by the life of the deity that he could only exploit the field of Ningirsu, a-šà dNin-gír-sú as GUR8, probably “interest-bearing loan”, without changing the course of the irrigation channel or transgressing the territory that belongs to Lagaš. The lú of Umma should not smash the monuments, presumably meaning boundary marks of fields. If Umma does this, the battle net of the deity will descend upon it from heaven, Ummaki-a an-ta ḫé-šuš. In each case after the oath was taken by the lú of Umma, Eannatum was described as being “very clever”, gal na-ga-mu-zu. In each of the cases, the text tells that Eannatum made up the eyes of doves, igi-ba šembi ba-ni-gar erin sag-ba ì-mi-du8, and released them to the place the deity in question presumably resided, probably with a message telling the deity what the lú of Umma swore. If the inim, “word”, of the ruler of Umma is not kept, may the sa-šuš-gal of the deity indeed descend upon Umma. It is clear that the acknowledgement of a deity as lugal-mu by the lugal-Lagaški was not confined to Enlil, since dEn-ki, dSu’en and dUtu, were also called lugal-mu by the same ruler. The Sumerian word lugal, besides “king”, has also the meaning “lord”, so that the acknowledgement of these deities as lugals does not automatically tell that each of them was perceived as a “king” as it is understood in English. But the Sumerian language allows for such nuances. In the title lugal-Lagaški here, for a ready instance, no qualification is needed for the Sumerians to specify that the lugal is “king” rather than “lord”. The acknowledgement of each
381 Cf. Cooper 1986, pp. 35–37, and elaborated in Cooper 1983b. See also Steible and Behrens 1982a, pp. 131–142, and Steible and Behrens 1982b, pp. 23–63, for philological details. 382 dNin-ki may be identical with dEreš-ki-gal as suggested in Selz 1995, p. 255.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
144
of the deities as the lugal-mu of the same ruler is significant here no matter how semantically the phrase means “my king” or “my lord”. The point to be made is that, all of these deities were at least at this occasion considered to be in the same category of deities by Eannatum. The term sa-šuš-gal understood as “the battle net” was associated as well with dNin-ḫur-sag, the “ama-mu” of the ruler, in addition to dEn-líl, dSu’en, and dUtu. In later Lagašite tradition the term was also attested with the local patron dNin-gír-su, as quoted above in the curse of Ent 28.383 The association of sa-šuš-gal with Ningirsu was probably a later development at the time of Entemena when Ningirsu the patron deity of Lagaš was somehow elevated to a higher rank. The Lagašite configuration of the pantheon seemed to have changed quite dramatically from before to after the time of Entemena.384 In Table 3.3 the broken lines of Ean 1, Obv. Col. 17, 3–4 concerning Enlil were restored as Nibruki-šè é-kur-ra following current convention and the restoration seems to suggest that the temple of Enlil was already known as é-kur to Eannatum. It needs to be stressed here, however, that this restoration of é-kur is highly problematic385. The restoration of the city name Nibruki is likely, since the geographic name EN.LÍL(É)ki is known elsewhere in inscriptions of the same ruler (Ean 62). But é-kur is never attested before this date. The term é-kur is known in Pre-Sargonic Nippur sources only from personal names and beyond this there is no contemporary evidence at all in support of restoring the term in the text of the Stele of the Vultures (Ean 1).386
383 Steible and Behrens 1982b, p. 327. 384 In the later Lagašite inscriptions Ninlil was the one to be paired with Enlil, in contrast with that in the Stele of the Vultures in which dNin-ḫur-sag seemed to take the place. Cf. Michalowski 1998, p. 240, n. 3, AO 4153 = Ukg 15, Col. 2, lines 2–5, an ki ur-ba sig4 an-gi4-gi4 u4-ba en-ki nun-ki nu-sig7 dEn-líl nu-ti dNin-líl nu-ti, also quoted in Horowitz 1998, p. 140. The conclusion is that, Nibruki may have been known in Lagaš as the residence city of Enlil but going one step further, it is unknown Enlil was already housed in a temple named é-kur at the time of Eannatum.
3.2.7.2 nam-lugal-kalam-ma
The term nam-lugal-kalam-ma, “kingship of the land”, is known for the first time in Luzag 1, the vase inscription of Lugalzagesi:
1,36u4 En-líl 1,37lugal-kur-kur-ra-ke4 1,38Lugal-zà-ge-si 1,39nam-lugal1,40kalam-ma 1,41e-na-sum-ma-a 1,42igi-kalam-ma-ke4 1,43si e-na-sá-a 1,44kur-kur gìri-na 1,45e-ni-sè-ga-a 1,46utu-è-ta 2,1utu-šú-šè 2,2gú e-nagar-ra-a 2,3u4-ba 2,4a-ab-ba-2,5sig-ta-ta 2,6Idigna-2,7Buranun-bi 2,8a-ab-ba 2,9igi-nim-ma-šè 2,10gìri-bi 2,11si e-na-sá 2,12utu-è-ta 2,13utu-šú-šè 2,14dEn-líl-le 2,15gaba-šu-gar 2,16nu-mu-ni-tuku
“When Enlil king of all lands had given the Kingship of the Land, made the Land obedient to him, thrown all lands at his feet, and subjected them to him from sunrise to sunset, he [Lugalzagesi] made his way from the Lower Sea, via the Tigris and Euphrates, to the Upper Sea, and Enlil allowed none to oppose him from sunrise to sunset.”387
The setting in which the Enlil gave nam-lugal-kalam-ma to king Lugalzagesi was the same as that of the ruler travelling from the Lower Sea to the Upper Sea, namely via the Tigris and Euphrates. The same text
NÌ.DU7-pa-è (OSP 1 121). Szarzynska 1992, p. 274, presumably located É-kur in the archaic corpus in Uruk. Such-Gutiérrez 2003, pp. 34-35, noted that in 2H-T25 of Enannatum I or Entemena the restoration of é-kur is also problematic. 387 The translation is after that of Postgate 1994a, p. 35. Note En-líl in line Col. 1, 36 was written without the determinative. This was the first clear attestation of Enlil granting kingship, according to Sallaberger 1997, p. 150. The term namlugal-kalam-ma cannot be a purely propagandist term claiming an unspecified “land” and kalam must be tangible for the ancients.

146
tells that Lugalzagesi had achieved his sovereignty through participating in some ceremony perhaps undertaken in the vicinity of Uruk (Luzag 1, Col. 2, lines 21–25): bára-bára-ki-en-gi énsi-kur-kur-ra ki-Unuki-ge menam-nun-šè mu-na-tar-e-ne, “The shrines of ki-en-gi, the Governors of all countries and the region of Uruk decreed the role of ruler for him.”388 The interpretation of the passage is not entirely certain, but insofar as this inscription is concerned, the term ki-en-gi here apparently refers to a region, a collectivity of cities that includes those mentioned in the inscription, Uruk, Ur, Larsa, Umma, Zabala, and KI.AN (Luzag 1). The matter of concern, me-nam-nun, “the role of ruler”, that was decreed to Lugalzagesi by the shrines of ki-en-gi and the governors of all countries would be a part that was integrated into the kingship of the Land, the kalam that Enlil “gave” to the ruler. En-šà-kúš-an-na of Uruk had also the titles lugal-kalam and en kien-gi (Enšak 1, Enšak 3). Ur-zag-è of Uruk had the title nir-gal-ḫuš-kien-gi (Urz 2). These royal titles containing ki-en-gi are probably all to be understood in the light of Luzag 1, namely that covering the region of the above cities but within the kalam, “the Land”. The nam-en and nam-lugal attested in Lukin 4, u4 dInanna-ke4 lugalki-gin-né-du7-du7-ra nam-en nam-lugal-la e-na-da-tab-ba-a, and in Lukin 1, u4 dEn-líl-le gù-zi é-na-dé-a nam-en nam-lugal-la e-na-da-tab-ba-a, are however to be understood as particularly those of Uruk and Ur rather than the nam-en and nam-lugal of the kalam, for which we now have a specific nam-lugal-kalam already. They were both to be integrated into the kingship of the Land by Lugalzagesi. The geographical domain under lugal-kalam in the inscriptions of Lugalzagesi would go far beyond that under the combination of the nam-en of Uruk and the nam-lugal of Ur.389
388 Postgate 1994a, p. 35. Cooper 1986, p. 94, took the passage as “the suzerains of Sumer and rulers of other lands conceded? sovereignty to him at Uruk.” 389 The phrase nam-lugal alone in the Pre-Sargonic Period seems only a general term without a concrete meaning and its specific meaning would be defined by the geographical name combined with it. See for instance, dNin-gír-su granted nam-lugal Lagaški to Uruinimgina, quoted in Jacobsen 1970, p. 372, n. 13.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD 147
Lugalzagesi offered Enlil food and drink offerings (nidba-gal-gal and a-du10) to dEn-líl lugal-ni Nibruki-a, “Enlil his king in Nippur”, as we know from the text of Luzag 1. The end of Luzag 1 explains the relationship of Enlil to the southern lugal-kur-kur-ra, the deity An, in a way in which the latter is termed the a ki-ág, “beloved father”, of the former. The relation of An, Enlil, and Lugalzagesi, it may be observed here, is much in parallel with the relationship of Enlil, dŠul-utul12, and Entemena as phrased in Ent 1, Col. 4, lines 2–10: En-te-me-na lú É-ad-da dù-a dingirra-ni dŠul-utul12 nam-ti En-te-me-na-ka-šè dEn-líl-la kìri šu ḫé-na-gál, “Entemena, who built the E’adda, may his personal god dŠul-utul12 forever pray to Enlil for the life of Entemena.”390 It cannot be purely accidental that Lugalzagesi perceived dEn- líl his lord in a way compatible to that of Entemena to the Lagašite deity dŠul-utul12 praying for the latter ruler.391
3.2.8 The Objects of Dedication
Westenholz observed of the royal inscriptions from the Pre-Sargonic Period that “none of the many votive inscriptions to Enlil was dedicated by the ensi of Nippur or by any private individuals, not even by Enlil’s own sanga, only by ‘Great Kings’ from ED III onwards”.392 Against his statement, the inscription of AnNip 61, inscribed on an alabaster stone vessel clearly dedicated to Enlil, tells that it was not by any lugal, “king”.393 It is doubtful if there were conceptual distinctions between the “private” and the “public” in early Mesopotamia. The presence of lugal in the dedicators’ titulary, in any case, is evidently not essential to qualify the dedicators in making their dedications to Enlil, since among them were certainly people called by the title énsi, like Entemena énsi-Lagaški. The focus of concern, in my view, must switch from the designations of the dedicators to the occasions on which dedications were made. The

phenomena when the scope in each case can differ. It is worth recalling that the énsi of Nibruki in the year name of ECTJ 211, who “sat in Uru-sag-rig7”, was probably by then also militarily active in a small region, despite Westenholz’ denial of the military intone of the year name.395 Seeing here that being militarily active in the region would already qualify his dedication to Enlil, this énsi of Nibruki becomes more likely the same énsi named as Ur-dEn-líl in Urenl 2 dedicated to Enlil.
3.3 The Barton Cylinder
The Barton Cylinder is one of the very few mythic-cultic texts from the city of Nippur dated to the Pre-Sargonic Period and is the only one among them that has been studied with reasonable thoroughness.396 In the definition of “The History of Deities” explained in the introduction, social relations may be deduced from around any “Index.” It needs to be made clear now that the social relations to be deduced from around mythic-cultic texts include not only those of the clay tablets or stone or brick artifacts on which the texts were inscribed but also those of the “immediate contexts” of Enlil within the mythic-cultic texts. In this sense, so to explain, we are effectively taking the “immediate contexts” of Enlil as also a kind of “artifacts”, or “indices” if using Gell’s terminology. This rationale is not always spoken out by textual scholars actually following it. But it is precisely for this reason that mythic-cultic texts become legitimately important for historical purposes, even if the entire compositions are not well understood, as is too often the case with early cuneiform literary texts.
3.3.1 The Ritual Context of the Text
The Barton Cylinder was found in a secondary archaeological con
395 Westenholz 1974, p. 155, and Westenholz 1975a, pp. 97–98. 396 The Pre-Sargonic literary fragments from Nippur published in Westenholz 1975b are much less preserved and have not been systematically studied.



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD
150
text in Nippur but is certainly earlier in dating than the classical Sargonic Period. The epigraphic features of the script used in the cylinder suggest that the composition came from the transitional time from the Early Dynastic to the Sargonic Period.397 The writing conventions suggest that the cylinder was probably inscribed by an Adab scribe.398 The text of the Barton Cylinder is difficult indeed and is still not well understood but it is relatively certain that the text narrated in mythological terms the activities of Enlil, Ninurta, and Nin-ḫur-sag, and that the cylinder was produced for cultic purposes. The editors of the cylinder based on the content of the text, in which Keš was mentioned twice and Nin-ḫur-sag was of central significance, considered that “it seems more likely that the cylinder was written in Keš, a place closely linked to Adab, and presumably partaking of the same handwriting tradition. Possibly the text was composed on the occasion of some religious event involving Nippur and Keš.”399 It is difficult to settle the matter but considering the fact that the site of Keš is yet not securely located and there is not sufficient epigraphic material to prove its handwriting tradition to be precisely identical to that of Adab, it appears better to stay with the epigraphic observation and to relate the occasion of the cultic activity in terms of Nippur and Adab instead of Nippur and Keš. In the offering list OSP 1 016 datable to the time of Sargon, which is to be discussed in the next chapter, nidba Adabki was delivered to the èš in Nippur.400 The 19th column of the Cylinder mentioned dEš-peš, who was
397 The cylinder, CBS 8383, was found on the southern outskirts of Mound I where the Onion Archive was excavated, according to Westenholz 1987, p. 87. 398 Cf. Alster and Westenholz 1994, p. 17, with reference to Biggs 1973, p. 45. The editors observed that signs like KA and BI in the cylinder “show forms without parallel in other contemporary texts from Nippur while they are regular only in texts from Adab. The immediate explanation would be that the text was written by a scribe from Adab.” 399 Alster and Westenholz 1994, p. 17. 400 The word nidba is written as KUR6.INANNA, rather than KUR6.dINANNA but



PART III: ENLIL IN THE PRE-SARGONIC PERIOD 151
certainly at home in Adab.401 The unusual attestation of the city name of Adab in the offering lists in the Nippur corpus must reflect some cultic activities involving both Adab and Nippur, which may be the background from which the Barton Cylinder came about as well. The material form of the cylinder, which was beautifully inscribed and made of solid clay, suggests that the cylinder belonged to a type of ritual in which the use of cylinders with inscribed texts has particular relevance.402 Although the mythology of the Barton Cylinder remains impenetrable, it is certain that the storyline involved dNin-ḫur-sag and Enlil. It is tempting, therefore, to imagine that the transference of the cultic centre of dNin-ḫur-sag from Keš to Adab had some connection with the Barton Cylinder, but this is hard to prove at the present.403 As early

3.3.2 The Sonship of dNin-urta 3.3.2.1 dNin-ḫur-sag nin-gal dEn-líl
Physically there is no visible indication on the cylinder where the text began but if the editors’ reconstruction of the sequence of the columns was correct, it starts with a stylized description of primordial condition: u4-rí-a u4-ri-šè na-nam ge6-ri-a ge6-ri-šè na-nam mu-rí-a mu-rí-šè na-nam u4 na-du7-du7 nin na-gír-gír èš-EN.LÍLki u4 na-du7-du7 nin na-gír-gír an-né ki-da inim an-dab6-e ki an-da inim an-dab6-e, “Those days were indeed faraway days. Those nights were indeed faraway nights. Those years were indeed faraway years. The storm roared, the lights flashed. On the sacred area of Nippur the storm roared, the lights flashed. Heaven talked with Earth. Earth talked with Heaven.”404 The primeval condition is the setting in which Enlil accomplished his duties, probably as some descendant of An and Ki.405 When the text resumes after a break, dNin-ḫur-sag joined the scene: igi-zi-gal-an-na ningal dEn-líl dNin-ḫur-sag igi-zi-gal-an-na nin-gal dEn-líl dNin-ḫur-sag-ra gèš mu-ni-du11 ne mu-ni-sub5 a maš imin šà mu-na?-ni?-ru, “When the ‘GrandGood-Lady-of-Heaven’, Enlil’s older sister, with Ninḫursag, with the ‘Grand-Good-Lady-of-Heaven’, Enlil’s older sister, with Ninḫursag, he had intercourse, he kissed her. The semen of seven twins he impregnated into her womb.” An unresolved issue here is whether it was actually Enlil that was the male partner engaging with Ninḫursag in some sexual activity, the “he” in the passage above.
404 Cf. Alster and Westenholz 1994, p. 26. The editors noted that the texts of TSS 079, IAS 280, and IAS 283, all from ED IIIa have similar opening: u4-rí-a u4-ri-šè. 405 Cf. Alster and Westenholz 1994, p. 37: “presumably one of the offspring of Heaven and Earth.” The parents of Enlil were probably variable in different traditions. Note IAS 114, Obv. Col., 9-11: UDGAL.NUN ama a dGAL.UNU UDNIN.KI, “Enlil, whose parents are Enki and Ninki”.

The part immediately following this passage is broken away and after the break the text resumed with ki muš-gír-da bal-bal an-da-za dI7-mah níg-TUR-zu a mu-da-túm x-ra dingir i7-da-ke4, “Earth had a conversation with the ‘scorpion’: ‘Supreme Divine River, your little “things” are carrying water’. For X, the god of the river?...” Michalowski understood the ki of ki muš-gír in the latter lines as the one who had intercourse with Ninḫursag, dEn-ki in his opinion. This possibility seems remote, however, since the latter lines most likely belong to a different plot.406 I follow the editors in taking the male part as Enlil and taking nin-gal as “sister” referring to Ninḫursag.407
3.3.2.2 dNin-urta dumu dNin-ḫur-sag
In connection with the birth giving activities of dNin-ḫur-sag and dEn-líl, certain events relating to the gudu4 priest and the removal of some objects caused Enlil to be bitter towards Nibru, Inanna towards Zabala, and Enki towards Abzu. The lack of food provisions in Nibru was reported to Enlil. Ninurta came out at this juncture and was dressed in a lion skin. After some preparatory activities involving guruš, Ninurta determined the fates of certain “children” and of winds. In unclear relation to the determination of fates, ki-su7 dEn-líl DU, “wasteland of Enlil”, occurs in a speech following gin-na dEn-ki DU ku-li-zu ḫè-am6.408 In a passage whose relation to the previous lines again remains unknown, dNin-ḫur-sag left, returned and re-established her seat in Keš, zag-è Kèš nam-mi-GUB, in a drama whose content is mostly lost. It is not clear how the composition ended but we read that dEš-peš bolted the KÁ.GAL and brought something from the èš. In broken context the text mentions AN.MUŠ.IR.ḪA.DIN.BALAG pre

sumably for dIrḫan.ÍD.AN.MUŠ.TIN.TIR.DÍB the name of Euphrates, pu-rattum (Antagal J II), or of Arahtum, a-ra-aḫ-tum, a branch of Euphrates (Erimḫuš VI).409 Ir-ḫa-an was the pronunciation indicator of ÍD that was evidently a-ra-aḫ-tum in the Zà-mì Hymns introduced in the last chapter, lines 160-162 of which in the section for dNin-girimx(A.ḪA.MUŠ.DU) read ir-ḫa-an MUŠ.DIN.DAR.BALAG.410 The broken condition of the Barton Cylinder and the difficulties reading the Zà-mì Hymns notwithstanding, the attestations of Ir-ḫa-an in both the Barton Cylinder and the Zà-mì Hymns would suggest that the geographical context in which the divine drama of the former was within the scope of that of the hymns that followed the same prologue speaking of Enlil. The storyline of the Barton Cylinder mentions Idigna kù Buranun kù-ge gidru-kù dEn-líl ḫur-sag! mu-DU, which would relate to the gidrukù that occurs in a section of speech apparently by dNin-ḫur-sag, Idigna kù Buranun kù-ge gidru-kù šeš-mu dEn-líl lú nam-DU an [x].411 These lines cannot be interpreted with certainty but the mentioning of gidru-kù dEn-líl, “the pure scepter of Enlil”, in the cylinder and in the context of the two rivers calls for special attention. In the speech that mentions Idigna kù Buranun kù-ge gidru-kù šeš-mu dEn-líl lú nam-DU an [x], in which the speaker must be dNin-ḫur-sag, Enlil was called šeš-mu, “my brother”, and dNin-urta was called dumu-mu, “my son.”412 The Barton Cylinder must be a kind of political myth used in political rituals but the contents differed considerably from Lugale especially on the detailed level. The ritual settings of the two texts would however be very similar to each other.413 The interesting observation from the Barton Cylinder is that the genealogical relationship that was significant to the political ritual was the sexual partnership between the šeš and the nin-gal, the same relationship the promiscuous dEn-líl(É) was involved in
409 Alster and Westenholz 1994, p. 36. 410 Biggs 1974, p. 55. 411 Alster and Westenholz 1994, p. 23, col. xiii, with copy on p. 45. 412 Alster and Westenholz 1994, pp. 24–25, col. xvii, and copy on p. 46. 413 Cf. Wilcke 1993, p. 60.

with various goddesses in Early Dynastic IIIa sources (IAS 345). It is difficult to go further, but if considering that Ninḫursag was the speaker of the speech in which Enlil was called šeš-mu, “my brother”, and Ninurta dumu-mu, “my son”, the sonship of Ninurta to Enlil seems to be established particularly through Ninḫursag, a curious but important fact not to be overlooked. I quoted earlier that in the Stele of the Vultures, sa-šuš-gal, “the battle net”, was associated with dNin-ḫur-sag, the ama-mu at home in Keš, as well as with dEn-líl, dSu’en, and dUtu the divine lugals (Ean 1). Also, the Bāseṭkī Statue from the Sargonic Period, though later in dating, named dINANNA in É-an-na-ki-im, dEn-líl in EN.LÍL.KI, dDa-gan in Tu-tu-li.KI, dNinḫur-sag in Kèš.KI, dEN.KI in Eridu.KI, dEN.ZU in ÚRI.KI dUTU in DINGIR.UD. KIB.NUN.KI, and dNergal in Gú-du8-a.KI, to acknowledge Narām-Sîn as the god of Akkade.414 If the attestation of Ninḫursag in the Barton Cylinder is politically significant as in the Stele of the Vultures in which she was apparently a peer of those divine lugals, and as in the Bāseṭkī Statue, which will be discussed in the next chapter, the establishment of Ninurta’s sonship to Enlil would be also a renewal of the cultic and political relationship of Nippur and Adab in the Pre-Sargonic context.
414 Frayne 1993, pp. 113–114.






Part IV: Enlil in the Time of Narām-Sîn and After
In his synthesis of the Sargonic Period, Westenholz began with a statement that “almost everything pertaining to the Sargonic period is a matter of controversy.”415 In the Sumerian King List, for instance, the sequence of Sargonic rulers, in which Rimuš Sargon’s son was the second king and the third king Maništušu was Rimuš’s older brother, was previously considered however reliable but in newly available sources the order of succession appears the reverse.416 The sources for the history of the Sargonic Period as we have them now, more practically,
“consist of some royal inscriptions; a handful of letters; maybe four thousand administrative documents, mostly from the South and dating toward the end of the period; a few pieces of
415 Westenholz 1999, p. 18. The geographical location of Akkade, the capital city of the Sargonic kingdom, is yet unknown. Weiss 1975 tried to identify Akkade with the site Išan Mizyad, but the proposal was later archaeologically disproved. McEwan 1982 proposed that Akkade may lie not far from Sippar, perhaps near the confluence of the Diyala and the Tigris. Wall-Romana 1990 suggested more specifically that Tell Muhammad in south Baghdad for the most likely candidate. Westenholz 1999, pp. 31–34, agreed with Wall-Romana that Akkade would be located on the Tigris, north of the Diyala but south of Aššur. 416 See Steinkeller 2003b, p. 272, for lines 16’–24’ of the Ur III manuscript of the SKL: Sar-ru-gin7-né [A]-ga-dèki-a mu 40 ì-na Ma-an-iš-ti-su mu 15 ì-na Rí-muuš-e, mu 8 ì-na, Na-ra-am-dSu-en-ke4 mu 54 iti 6 ì-na, “Sargon ruled 40 years in Akkade, Maništušu ruled 15 years, Rimuš ruled 8 years, Narām-Sîn ruled 54 years and 6 months.” Note, Westenholz 1999, p. 41, had followed Steinkeller 1989’s speculation that Maništūšu murdered Rīmuš his younger brother.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER
158
monumental art, mostly broken and nearly always found in secondary context; about two thousand seal cylinders or ancient impressions, some of them perhaps fakes; some private houses in Nippur and the Diyala, perhaps not representative; a town plan, possibly Sargonic; a few monumental buildings at Tell Brak and Eshnunna, of doubtful interpretation; and the incompletely excavated and even more incompletely published remains of the Sargonic version of Enlil’s temple in Nippur.”417
The lack of reliable knowledge concerning the general history of the Sargonic Period, similar in degree but different in aspects from that of earlier times, makes it impossible to address all of the questions related to Enlil. In this chapter the stress will be on the administrative aspect of the deity Enlil as under the Sargonic king Narām-Sîn and his son Šarkališarri, though equal attention will be paid to the official ideologies of the time period in general. Ideological change has to be seen in the context of administrative activities since it makes real sense only if it is expressed in the practical realization of power.418 Before getting into our textual sources, nevertheless, an excursion into the Old Akkadian cylinder seals seems necessary, as a certain group from among such seals
417 Westenholz 1999, p. 19, did not mention the few literary texts from the time period. MAD 1 172 is probably a legend of Narām-Sîn, as discussed in Westenholz 1997. MAD 4 008 is an Akkadian love incantation discussed in Westenholz and Westenholz 1977. MDP 14 090 is an Akkadian evil eye incantation discussed in Farber 1981. See also the Sumerian incantations dated to the time as presented in Cunningham 1997. Biggs 1989 had reported that literary texts were found in Umm al-Hafriyat, datable to the Old Akkadian Period. 418 Westenholz 1999, p. 117, believed that fundamental changes happened in the “religion” of the Sargonic Period, for instance, the images of the deities became much more vivid than before. But he was unwilling to specify how such changes were realized. Foster 1993a spoke of “the administration of the mind”, which had better refer to the administration of the “religious” institutions since the intended ideological control would have no effect if it was not institutionalized.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER 159
might be of particular interest to our matter of concern. The Old Akkadian cylinder seals are often beautifully inscribed with narrative scenes. And as such they had provoked much enthusiasm for iconographical studies in the early decades of the last century, to achieve a remarkable but mixed success.419 If seen from now, however, the methodology adapted by the early authors somehow handicapped if not discouraged further studies of their source materials. The repertoire of reference information which the earlier authors used in making iconographical identifications was often drawn from much later and mainly mythological texts, accumulated with little regard to the time and place where the sources came. On the other side, not just to be fair, despite methodological problems inherit in the early studies of the Old Akkadian seals, iconographical identification of “religious” motifs cannot be simply abandoned or subtly ignored when addressing the history of the Sargonic Period, especially the “religious” aspects of it.420 The Old Akkadiann cylinder seals are too precious as historical sources to be left aside, and visual representations are as essential as textual records for any “religious” life ancient and modern.421 For Old Akkadian cylinder seals, Rainer M. Boehmer is arguably the most representative scholar in the last century. In the main text of his major work, Boehmer presented his corpus in 29 chapters each entitled with an iconographic motif he considered to be securely identifiable in the seals: “Tierkampfszenen”, “Heilige Hochzeit”, and “Musikanten”, and so on. But as he was concerned more with dating the seals, he came to
419 Frankfort 1939a and van Buren 1945 were major earlier works representative of the time, though they were not exclusively concerned with the Old Akkadian evidence. More recently, Braun-Holzinger 1996 is perhaps one of such following a more traditional iconographical approach. 420 Buchanan, Hallo and Kasten 1981 had avoided iconographical interpretations altogether. Collon 1987 and Matthews 1997 paid little attention to iconographic identification. Wiggermann’s summary in Collon, Wiggermann et al. 2003 spoke only in very general terms of the “religious” aspect of glyptic iconography. 421 See Amiet 1980b for a call for continued studies.

divide all the seals known to him into six groups in chronological order: Mesannepada-Lugalanda-Stufe, Akkadisch Ia, Akkadisch Ib, Akkadisch Ic, Akkadisch II, and Akkadisch III, in his chronological terminology.422 Boehmer’s work has been criticized especially for falling into the trap of forcing historical periods to correspond to artistic styles and for being overly well-organized when grouping seals under specific motifs. If to establish the datings of particular seals or groups of seals with certain motifs, so to relate glyptic representation to particular historical events in any sense, by McMahon’ evaluation it would probably have to begin with a complete reworking of the entire corpus of Old Akkdian glyptic materials.423 I cannot engage further with Old Akkadian iconography for the lack of specialty. In our context, it only needs to be stressed that, the simple fact that up to now studies typically aiming at identifying deities with anthropomorphic images on Old Akkadian cylinder seals have failed for Enlil, is significant enough.424 The failure cannot be purely accidental if considering the evidenced significance of the deity to early Mesopotamia and the great effort made

to identify divine figures in visual representation. The reason that this divergence is made here, in fact, is because of the probable relevance of a group of seals on which a winged square is depicted on the back of an animal, namely the seals that had the so-called “geflügelten Tempel” represented on them.425 Bernbeck rencently correlated this “geflügelte Tempel” motif to the “Etana” motif, taking both as “Darstellung von Riten”.426 The “geflügelte Tempel” motif might be also associated with the boat motif representing cultic journeys (GAA 592).427 The winged square on the back of the ox-like animal on these seals, which Bernbeck proposed to consider as a kind of container, Selz saw as a Rohrkorb.428 Going a step further, Selz took the ox-like animal in the “geflügelte Tempel” scene as facing “in einigen Fällen eine thronende Gottheit”, when a bird is sometimes depicted within the Rohrkorb above it (GAA 591, GAA 594, GAA 597).429 The scene was thus interpreted as that of a ritual in which a caged bird was transported and finally released in a cultic “[p]rozession zu einem heiligen Ort bzw. zu einer Gottheit.”430

Selz then related the rite depicted in the scene to the “Himmelfahrt” of kings, which is “a logical corollary of royal deification”.431 The winged box motif, so to name the motif without further interpretation, will have to wait for future research. It is not certain whether the “Himmelfahrt” of kings was meant but it does seem that the motif represented ritual activities and the rite in all likelihood did involve the ruler. The person at the upper middle of the scene of GA 1351 = GAA 591 illustrated above, with a stick in his hand, would have represented the ruler, though the figure does not have to be identified with the ruler’s person in reality. am inclined to think that the winged box, whose image on GAA 591 must be in accordance with the contemporary tradition, depicted on the back of the ox might refer to the same object in reality that was realized earlier in writing as the KID sign, the LÍL part of the name of Enlil. In this sense, the winged box here might be the symbol of Enlil (Fig. 4.1). I admit that this is no more than an educated guess. But seeing the significance of Enlil for our understanding of early Mesopotamia, the point is worth mentioning at least to justify a search in this direction.
4.1 Enlil in Sargonic Administrative Texts
The administrative records from the Sargonic Period, although not as great in number as those available from the Ur III Period, are known from an extensive geographical area: about twenty sites across greater Mesopotamia including Sumer, Akkad, the Diyala region, and as far as the Jezirah, Nuzi and Susa.432 The archival contexts of the Sargonic texts are not always clear or traceable but such information, when available, will help greatly in reading the attestations of Enlil in live contexts.433 The Diyala and Nuzi texts excavated early in the last centuray and the more recently excavated texts from the Jezira are not treated below as personal names there bear no sign of Enlil.434 The texts from Susa are
432 Cf. Foster 1993b, pp. 175–177. Foster 1982a estimated 4,700 administrative documents known to have come from the Sargonic Period but see the more publications to be quoted below. See also the introduction to allegedly known but unavailable Sargonic texts, given in Pomponio, Stol et al. 2006. 433 Steinkeller 2003a in Brosius 2003 was concerned with Ur III administrative practice. The most complete survey of Sargonic archives remains Foster 1982a. 434 MAD 1 001–195 came from the northern building and the “private houses” in the northern part of Tell Asmar, the site of ancient Ešnunna, as according to Delougaz, Hill and Lloyd 1967, pp. 181–182 and pp. 196–197. The texts attributed to Ešnunna in Gelb 1955 cannot be actually from the site though they may be from the Diyala region. Khafaje, ancient Tutub, produced 66 tablets from around the time of Narām-Sîn, newly re-edited in Sommerfeld 1999, p. 39–44, indexed to

excluded because they were derived from an outpost in the Susiana plain with personnel from varied cities in Mesopotamia.435 KT 01–03 and KT 16–18 from Tell al-Wilayah, important as the site must be, are also not discussed here since Enlil was not attested therein in any form.436 The listing of personal names in this section follows earlier practices, though in some cases when listing does not seem necessary, names will be simply enumerated. If no other information is available, the siglum of the text in which the name is attested will be put in subcategory a, if the attestation is followed or preceded by titles or other information, the titles or the other information will be quoted together with the text in subcategory b, the attestations of parallel names will be in subcategory c, and finally, Akkadian names are typed in italic.
4.1.1 The Nippur Archives
The Nippur corpus from the time of Narām-Sîn and Šarkališarri, as available to us, consists of the “Akkadian” Archive (OSP 2 001–043, OSP 1 007, ECTJ 013, ECTJ 051, ECTJ 126, possibly ECTJ 205), the Enlilemaba Archive (OSP 2 044–065), the Onion Archive (OSP 2 079–181, PBS 03 093–094, likely OSP 2 182–187), a few Narām-Sîn Ration Lists (ECTJ 029, ECTJ 034, ECTJ 037, ECTJ 039, ECTJ 044, ECTJ 148, ECTJ 185, ECTJ 186, ECTJ 189, ECTJ 191, ECTJ 196), and a number of approximately contemporary administrative records or other texts that cannot be assigned to any of

4.1.1.2 The Enlilemaba Archive 4.1.1.2.1 Personal Names
The find spot of the Enlilemaba archive is unknown, though the texts seem to have been excavated by the 4th expedition at a site other than the Ekur courtyard. The dating of the Enlilemaba archive was a “reasonable guess”, according to which the earlier tablets belonging to the archive may be dated to the beginning of Narām-Sîn’s reign or slightly earlier, while the latest documents may date to the end of Narām-Sîn’s reign.444 Texts OSP 2 066–078, though not securely grouped into any particular groups, were remains of similar private archives from the same time. The individual persons involved in the Enlilemaba archive included mostly dam-gàr’s, “merchants”, such as dEn-líl-e-ma-ba, E-lú, Amar-dEzinu, artisans, and in the case of Šeš-ku-li, nu-bànda kù-dim, “head of the silversmiths’ guild”.445 The personal names known from the Enlilemaba archive and from the contemporary legal documents, 74 in total of such, include ten containing Enlil.
1. A-ba-dEn-líl, “Who is (like) Enlil?”446 b. dam-gàr (OSP 2 051-053, OSP 2 055, OSP 2 058-063), father of Ur-dDa-mu gu4-engar (OSP 2 063), dumu Du-du (OSP 2 074).
2. Á-dEn-líl-ta, “By the strength of Enlil”.447

The father of A-ba-dEn-líl in text OSP 2 074 was named Du-du while in another case the father of dEn-líl-lá was A-zu-zu.451 These “banana”named fathers had sons with names composed with Enlil.452 It may be inferred that using Enlil as a name element was not determined by ethnicity. The house born slave Pù-zur8-dEn-líl had a Semitic name. In the family lines of A-ba-dEn-líl and Gan-dEn-líl the children dEn-líl-le-ma-ba and dEn-líl-lá shared the same theophoric element with the names of their parents but this may be not representative.453 Ur-dDa-mu the brother of dEn-líl-le-ma-ba had a name nothing to do with Enlil. Enlil was attested in personal names of both sexes, as in the case of Gan-dEn-líl, though most of the name bearers were male.
4.1.1.2.2 (giš)dusu É-ad-da
The Enlilemaba archive has been interpreted as remnants of the records of a group of private money lenders and real estate owners who rarely did business with public institutions. In so interpreting the archive, Westenholz suggested that these money lender and real estate owners were probably all members of a “paternal house”, the real referent of the term É-ad-da.454 In the texts from the same archive, the term (giš)dusu, “basket”, is attached immediately before é-ad-da in OSP 2 052 and OSP 2
451 Westenholz 1987, p. 195, did not count A-zu-zu as a name in his name list but however so interpreted under the entry dEn-líl-lá. A-zu-zu engar is attested in Steinkeller and Postgate 1992, pp. 98-99, text MC 4 060 in Sumerian context with unknown provenance. 452 Foster 1982b, p. 299, counted 11 percent of “banana” names from Kiš and Mugdan, 12 percent from the Diyala and most impressively 16 percent from Gasur/Nuzi. In the south, Adab had 7 percent such names while otherwise the percentage is less than 5 percent. Foster 1982b, p. 303, noted that Du-du occurs with both Sumerian and Akkadian parentage. 453 Di Vito 1993, pp. 13–14, suggested that family inheritance is not a recognizable motivation in choosing theophoric name element for the children. 454 Westenholz 1987, p. 60.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER
170
062–063, while in OSP 2 054–055 the phrase was written (giš)dusu ad-da. Westenholz interpreted dusu É-ad-da as “the family basket”, from which individual members of the paternal house could borrow for commercial purposes and to repay with interests in annual instalments.455 Steinkeller pointed out that Westenholz’s theory considering dusu É-ad-da as a family fund is “almost certainly wrong”, since (giš)dusu, ilku, in all likelihood denoted the corvée-obligation, “more precisely, the money paid in lieu of the performance of that obligation” due to the state for the land held.456 The attestations of the term in the archive support Steinkeller’s interpretation to regard É-ad-da as the basic unit, namely the “paternal house”, on which the corvée was imposed, since (giš)dusu Éad-da was not even used consistently as a given term. It may be recalled that É-ad-da im-sag-gá known from Pre-Sargonic Lagaš in the inscriptions of Entemena was the name of the temple of Enlil built by the ruler when both the location of that temple, and the precise meaning of the name of the temple, by which the function of the temple might be inferred, cannot be determined. The usage of É-ad-da in the Enlilemaba archive would help to understand what the old Sumerian temple called É-ad-da was in the context of daily life.
4.1.1.3 The Onion Archive
The Nippur Onion Archive is so named because this group of texts recorded the cultivation, harvest, and distribution of onions, turnips, and coriander within and beyond the city. The archive was likely found in situ, which is to say in its original archaeological context. The records in the archive probably spanned the reign of Šarkališarri though “the earliest texts may date from very late in the reign of Naram-Sin”.457 A man with some position, Lugal-nì-BE-du10 the gal-sar, “headman of

The cultivators were apparently flexible in their services, as we see in the case of dEn-líl-an-zu lú-sar-ra, who served once as maškim in onion expenditures. In the case of dEn-líl-da lú-sar-ra, the person would be the same serving as muhaldim at one occasion.

two deities sharing common characteristics as the divine “ruler” (OSP 2 098, OSP 2 104, OSP 2 120). This is reminiscent of the divine rulership of An that was widely acknowledged in Nippurian personal names from the Pre-Sargonic Period..
4.1.1.3.2 zà-mu dEn-líl
The recipients of onions recorded in the Onion Archive were often high officials, suggesting that the gal-sar was part of a much larger bureaucracy “with links far beyond the city walls of Nippur, notably to the Akkadian province administration.”463 OSP 2 171 recorded 5 sum lídga sá-du11 zà-mu dEn-líl-kam, “1200 litres of onions, the regular offering for Enlil’s New Year Festival”. The festival zà-mu dEn-líl may have taken place either within or without Nippur, but at present no other information is known of this presumably important festival in the Sargonic administration of the Nippurian cults.464
4.1.2 The Isin Texts 4.1.2.1 Personal Names
In the texts from Isin dated to the time of Narām-Sîn, the personal names containing Enlil are much alike those from Nippur to the degree that they often find synonyms in those from the latter city, evidencing a link between these two cities during the Sargonic Period that would have been stronger than a simple geographical proximity.465

4.1.3.2 The nu-èš “Priests”
The Šarkališarri texts from Adab recorded activities of a number of individuals with their names written in forms bewilderingly alternating

between dEn-líl-lá, dEn-líl, and En-líl. But the concern here is however not how many individuals were actually recorded in the archive that could be referred to by the form (d)En-líl(-lá), though a closer reading of each of the attestations might help identifying the individuals behind.482 The question starts with the person dEn-líl-lá the dub-sar known in the corpus. The bearer or bearers of this name and title were in charge of the distribution of sá-du11 offerings, were closely associated with the é-maḫ temple of Nin-ḫur-sag (Ad 0646, Ad 0674, Ad 0923, Ad 0944, Ad 0965), and were known to have served as ugula (Ad 0637, Ad 0695) and maškim (Ad 0637).483 The interesting point is that, dEn-líl-lá was as well attested in the corpus as one of the persons with the title nu-èš (Ad 0658, Ad 0751, Ad 0793, Ad 0825, Ad 0828). The title nu-èš, besides dEn-líl-lá, was also attested with Un-íl (Ad 0714), Úr-ra-ni (Ad 0675), and Lugal-ni (Ad 0947). Un-íl is known also as dub-sar (Ad 0624, Ad 0816, Ad 1121). Such-Gutiérrez interpreted the nu-èš, known in Akkadian as nešakku, to be associated only with the dEn-líl circle in Nippur, where the divine name has not to be always attached to the title when it is mentioned in texts. Thus he saw the –èš in the title nu-èš as referring specifically to èš-EN.LÍLki, the èš at Nippur.484 In his perception, when the nu-èš priests of dEn-líl were mentioned outside the city of Nippur, the designation EN.LÍLki would necessarily follow. Therefore, as an example, Ur-dEn-líl was referred to as nu-èš-EN.LÍLki in BIN 08 061 from Isin.485 The quoted Adab attestations of the title nu-èš, if following the same line of thinking, would refer to the nu-èš priests of Enlil local to Adab since otherwise the title would be written as nu-èš-EN.LÍLki.486 The office of nu-èš is important because the questions of whether
482 dEn-lil-lá lú-ùri was juxtaposed with dEn-líl-lá AB×AŠ, “city elder” in Ad 0640, further complicating the situation. Note that, Yang 1989, p. 436, had taken lú-ùri and AB×AŠ as both independent personal names. 483 Yang 1989, pp. 243–244, and p. 248. 484 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 35, translated the title as “Mann: ‘Sanctuarium’”. 485 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 47, n. 133. 486 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 35, n. 59.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER
178
Enlil was the only deity with which the title was associated, and whether the title was written without mentioning the city name of Nippur only when it was attested within the city of Nippur, are both significant in elucidating the institutional aspects of the cult of Enlil in the Sargonic Period. From approximately contemporary sources, Íl nu-èš (4N-T268, 4N-T270, both sealings), Lú-kisal nu-èš (ECTJ 166), Šeš-bànda(TUR) nu-èš (ECTJ 159), are known in texts from Nippur, and Nin-ì-maḫ dam-Un-íl nu-èš is known in a letter with uncertain provenance (BIN 08 157). Nin-ì-maḫ dam-Un-íl is known from BIN 08 167 probably from Isin.487 I agree with Such-Gutiérrez that the title was likely associated with Enlil at this time, which is to say the nu-èš “priests” specifically belonged to the Enlil worship circle. But there is no need to ask whether nu-èš had to be written as nu-èš-EN.LÍLki when attested outside Nippur, since Enlil was by no means only worshiped in the city of Nippur. The geographical name following the personal names bearing the title nu-èš can better be explained as referring to the origin of the person in question, rather than referring to the origin of the title which was however not bound to the geographical location associated with the same deity. To illustrate the difference, it needs only to be pointed out that, the phrase Ur-dEn-líl nu-èš-EN.LÍLki can better be understood as “Ur-dEn-líl the nu-èš priest who as a person came from EN.LÍLki” rather than “UrdEn-líl the nu-èš that belongs to EN.LÍLki” (BIN 08 061). The attestation of the nu-èš title can only be expected in Adab if considering that the cult centre of dNin-ḫur-sag, the nin-gal of Enlil in the Barton Cylinder, was by then located in Adab. And in all likelihood the persons behind the names dEn-líl-lá nu-èš and Un-íl nu-èš known in Adab were identical with the dub-sars who had the same names.488 The consequence is that, the nu-èš “priests” of Enlil, as individuals were deeply involved in the Adab administration in which contexts they were referred to as dub-sars, another designation of duty or identity they legitimately had.489

dEn-líl-lá the dub-sar was in charge of deliveries of offerings, and Un-íl dub-sar for the allocation of grain for travellers.490 In a group of addir má-laḫ texts recording the “payment for boatmen”, shipped barley was separately computed at the rate of 1:39 (Ad 0658, Ad 0751, Ad 0793, Ad 0825, Ad 0828). dEn-líl-lá in this case took one fortieth share of the goods for the temple institution in whose ambit he was a nu-èš.491
4.1.3.3 ezen dBára-dEn-líl
Ur-é-maḫ received bread and beer in Ad 0721 for the event of ezen dBára-dEn-líl ù ezen dDumu-zi-da KÁ [x x x], “the festival of dBára-dEn-líl and the festival of dDumu-zi-da ...”. The signs after KÁ were broken but the traces can be reasonably restored as [dEN-x]. In Ad 0712, part of the grain was termed še ŠU.PEŠ5.RI a-[bul5]-la dEn-líl-[x], “barley of ŠU.PEŠ5.RI, the abulla of Enlil”. The ŠU.PEŠ5.RI was probably related to kangiškara, “a special kind of table, possibly an elaborate piece of furniture.”492 The title lúa-bul5-la, “guards of the ‘gate’”, is known in Ad 0680 in a context in which the expression en-né íl-la is also attested.493 The relationship of the cultic event involving še ŠU.PEŠ5.RI a-[bul5]-la dEn-líl-[x] of Ad 0712 and that of the ezen dBára-dEn-líl ù ezen dDumu-zida KÁ [x x x] in Ad 0721, or whether they were related at all, is unclear in the absence of direct evidence. But on the ground that a-[bul5]-la dEn-líl seems to be a significant part of both of them, they would have been at least related to each other if not identical.494

In Ad 1093, 1 máš É-maḫ udu zi-ga máš dBára-dEn-líl-gar-ra itu gáudu-ur4, we know one kid was issued from the É-maḫ for dBára-dEn-lílgar-ra in the month itu gá-udu-ur4.495 The location of the dBára-dEn-lílgar-(ra) was therefore not within the É-maḫ and the festival most likely took place out of Adab. In one of the texts dated with Šarkališarri’s year name, he was reported to have passed dEn-líl-le-gar-raki uru ki Lagaški (Ad 0651).496 The cone of Entemena from Pre-Sargonic Lagaš recorded the building of Bára-dEn-líl at nam-nun-da-ki-gar-ra, “place founded deep”: im-dub-ba-dNin-gír-su-ka Nam-nun-da-ki-gar-ra bára-dEn-líl-lá bára-dNinḫur-sag-ka bára-dNin-gír-su-ka bára-dUtu bí-dù, “[He] built bára-dEn-líl-lá, bára-dNin-ḫur-sag, bára-dNin-gír-su, and bára-dUtu at a place known as nam-nun-da-ki-gar-ra, [which was] the boundary of Ningirsu.”497 The questions are whether dBára-dEn-líl-gar-(ra) referred to a dais in the specific place dEn-líl-le-gar-raki uru ki Lagaški and whether the PreSargonic Bára-dEn-líl at the boundary of Lagaš may be identified with the dBára-dEn-líl in ezen dBára-dEn-líl ù ezen dDumu-zi-da and in dBára-dEn-lílgar-(ra) in Ad 1093. There is no easy answer, however, in spite of the seemingly linked terminologies in similarly ritual contexts. It is only possible to propose a reasonable guess that ezen dBára-dEn-líl ù ezen dDumu-zi-da probably took place in dEn-líl-le-gar-raki at the border of Lagaš and Umma. If this was the case, the name of the uru, dEn-líl-le-gar-raki in the Lagaš region, would have been derived from the fact that the dais of Enlil, Bára-dEn-líl, was built therein in times earlier (Ad 0651). The time when this place was “deified”, which we know through later god lists, would be somewhere after the Sargonic Period when ritual activities continued to

4.1.4 The Umma Texts
The Umma B archive could have resulted from a family enterprise managed by Ur-dŠára and Ama-é, with an associate named Ur-dEN.ZU who was connected with the state administration.499 Ama-é seemed “a business woman with a sphere of activity independent of her husband.”500 The Umma C archive consists of two or more subgroups of texts from the office of the énsi of Umma under the rule of king Šarkališarri.501 The Mesag archive came from an agricultural center in Sagub between Umma and Lagaš though the tablets could actually come from Umma.502 Mesag

Foster 1982c, p. 53, dated the Umma B to the time between Uruinimgina and Šarkališarri. the énsi of Umma attested in Umma C was in all likelihood the same person as Mesag the énsi who controlled his own estate in Sagub.503 It is significant to note that, the accounting practice of the Mesag archive is different from that of Umma C, the latter of which followed the Sumerian system whiles the former the Akkadian system. The Akkadian accounting system practiced in the personal estate of the Sargonic énsi may actually be introduced by the Sargonids who seemingly remained more faithful to their old ways of doing things when dealing with their own estates.504
4.1.4.1 Umma B and Umma C 4.1.4.1.1 Personal Names
The names containing Enlil from Umma B and C texts are few in percentage if compared to that of the contemporary texts from Nippur.505 The Ur-dEn-líl-lá who contributed 10.3.0 še gur-sag-gál DÙL received by Gala the šu-i would be one of the intermediaries in the hierarchy of Ama-é’s property in Umma B (MAD 4 022).506 BE-dEn-líl, possibly to be understood in Akkadian as Bēl-dEn-líl, was attested in Umma C (Serota

4.1.4.1.2 a-šà dEn-líl-lá
The field name a-šà dEn-líl-lá was very frequently attested in records of grain in Umma B (MC 4 013, USP 20, MAD 4 039, MAD 4 057, MAD 4 066-067, MAD 4 103, MAD 4 115, MAD 4 120, MAD 4 144, MAD 4 164). dEn-líl-lá in the field name is unlikely to be a personal name and presumably referred to the deity. It is unclear if the field names in Umma B designated with divine names such as dEn-líl-lá, dInanna, and Gibil were already in use before the establishment of the Ama-é estate but it is certain that these “divine” field names continued in use till later in the Ur III Period.510 The administration of the fields named after deities was not discernibly different from that of other fields mentioned in the archive. The field name a-šà dEn-líl is also known from Pre-Sargonic and Sargonic Girsu (STTI 180). The location of the fields named after divine deities would be quite certainly in between Umma and Lagaš though we have no evidence to identify the a-šà dEn-líl here with the aša5-dEn-líl known in Early Dynastic Lagašite texts, which must have been cultivated at the latest from the

4.1.4.2 The Mesag Archive 4.1.4.2.1 Personal Names
É-dEn-líl-e belonged to Bridges’ group II, “head of estate household” under Mesag. The frequency of the attestation of the name suggests he was an important person for the administration of the énsi’s personal property, and was resident in the Sagub headquarters (MC 4 033, MC 4 045, BIN 08 183, BIN 08 194, BIN 08 199, BIN 08 204, BIN 08 231, BIN 08 269). Ur-dEn-líl-lá, dEn-líl-lá, and dEn-líl-sipa were known in the archive but they seemed to be less closely associated with Mesag’s estate (MC 4 040, BIN 08 152, BIN 08 231). Bridges considered two Akkadians, I-rí-iš11 (LAM+KUR)-dEn-líl and Li-bur-KI-dEn-líl, to have only a transitory connection to the management of Mesag’s estate (BIN 08 123, BIN 08 128 and BIN 08 203, BIN 08 227).512

4.1.5 The Lagaš Texts
The majority of the Girsu texts from the Sargonic Period may have resulted from the administration of the é-gal when Lugal-ušum-gal was énsi of Lagaški.513 Among these texts there are some that may be taken as offering lists, based on which a glance at the cultic practice of the city in the Sargonic Period is possible. But Sargonic Girsu texts have not been subjected systematically studied, making it impossible to fully exploit the prosopographical information of the Girsu texts.514 This is unfortunate especially when Girsu was probably the palatial centre of a Sargonic province embracing much of Sumer.515
4.1.5.1 Personal Names
In a personnel list, dEn-líl-lá was the brother of Ur-da and Ur-sa6 (RTC 095).516 dEn-líl-lá dumu Lú-dUtu the guruš in ITT 1 1350 probably needs to be distinguished from the homonymous person in orchard and land documents (ITT 2 5725, STTI 143). dEn-líl-le held land (ITT 1 1404).517 dEn-líl-gal-zu, “Enlil is clever”, was attested in a personnel list as under a nu-bànda, and was probably identical with the homonymous báḫar,

Note, RCT 083 mentioned also Ur-é énsi-Lagaš. 514 Visicato 2000, pp. 124–129, gave a brief introduction to the Sargonic tablets from Girsu, updating Foster 1982a and Donbaz and Foster 1982. 2. ITT 1 1155
Obv. 1,1dNin-gír-su 1,2An 1,3dEn-líl 1,4 dNin-ḫur-sag 1,5kíg-SIG7 dingir-dingir-e-ne 1,650 [x] 1,71? [x]
“Ningirsu, An, Enlil, Ninḫursag, the afternoon meal, the great gods...”
ITT 1 1102 was a zi-ga expenditure text recording sheep and goats for a variety of deities. The listing of particular deities would depend on the specific context the expenditure was made, as can be evidenced by a



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER 187
comparison with ITT 1 1203, which records 1 udu dUtu 1 sila4 dEn-líl 1 sila4 dIškur, “1 sheep to Utu, 1 lamb to Enlil, 1 lamb to Iškur”, for another zi-ga expenditure.518 But the fact that Enlil is attested in the Girsu lists would already speak of his participation in the cultic administration of the city in the time of Narām-Sîn and Šarkališarri.519 Furthermore, dNin-gír-su, An, dEn-líl, and dNin-ḫur-sag were the first listed deities that presided at the kíg-SIG7, naptan līliāti, “afternoon meal”, in the text ITT 1 1155. An preceded Enlil in the list, showing that An held an important position and was probably regarded by then as the head of the pantheon in Lagaš. In Pre-Sargonic Lagaš in the inscriptions of Eannatum and Entemena, on the other hand, Enlil was regarded as the head of the official pantheon according to the sequence of the religious titles of the ruler discussed in the last chapter (Ean 1, Ean 2, Ent 32, Ent 35). If securely dated to the Sargonic Period, there are uncertainties concerning the dating of this text, ITT 1 1155 would give the earliest evidence of the cult of An in Lagaš, to be explained by political events to do with the immediate context of the rise of the Sargonids.520

4.2 Enlil in Sargonic Royal Inscriptions
The collections and editions of Sargonic royal inscriptions as currently available do not distinguish the original ones from those available only through Old Babylonian Sammeltafeln or Ur III or later copies. The criteria for dividing the corpus of Sargonic royal inscriptions in terms of chronology is no less problematic than the Pre-Sargonic inscriptions and the sign values of the Old Akkadian writing system are not read consistently by different editors.522 I assume the faithfulness of the Old Babylonian copists to their originals but will add a * sign before each of the RIME 2 siglum when the text is known only through later copies.523 The “Captions” and the “Colophons” that the Old Babylonian or later copiers of other times provided are considered only when they provide information for identifying the material forms of the inscriptions.524
521 Gelb 1970a considered IM 23302, IM 23304, IM 23305 (MAD 5 102–104), MAD 5 001-065 and MAD 5 114–116 as from Kiš. The reverse of MAD 5 036 preserves wa-si-bu, wāšibū?. Šu-dEn-líl is apparently a man of status in MAD 5 025: 60? LÁ 1 UDU [šu?]-dEn-líl [ŠU].DU8 [× UDU] [PN] MUHALDIM a-na MAŠKIM ŠU.DU8 1 UDU pù?-pù? LÚ [x] LUGAL MUḪALDIM ŠU.DU8. 522 Frayne 1993, p. 3. 523 Cf. Buccellati 1993, Westenholz 1998a for the loss of archaeological contexts. Franke 1995, pp. 86–87, considered Old Babylonian copies faithful. 524 I follow Frayne 1993 for captions and colophons but see Gelb and Kienast 1990 for the principles of extracting inscriptions from the Sammeltafeln.

4.2.1 Personal Names
The stamp inscription already mentioned above tells that Sargon the king named one of his sons after Enlil (E2.1.1.17). In other Sargonic royal inscriptions, we have an Igi-dEn-líl-šè dedicated a small cup for the life of Nam-maḫ-abzu énsi-EN.LÍLki to Nin-é-gal (E2.6.3.2001). dEn-líl-zi, mA-badEn-líl NU.BÀNDA Adabki, mdEn-líl-[x] GAL.SUKKAL AB×ÁŠ URU.KI of RÉC 349.KI were among those rebel leaders Narām-Sîn defeated (*E2.1.4.2). Na-bi-dEn-líl GÌR.NÍTA-ti-rí-ga-a-an was a Gutian general known from an Old Babylonian copy of an “inscription” of Utu-Ḫegal (*E2.13.6.4). The Utu-Ḫegal text was almost certainly a literary work but the personal name Na-bi-dEn-líl shows that at least in the perception of the composer of the text, the Gutians were equally free using Enlil as a name element.525
4.2.2 The Officialdom of Enlil 4.2.2.1 sanga-dEn-líl and sanga èš-dEn-líl
In the Enlilemaba archive, an énsi of Nippur was attested twice in broken contexts but this énsi is not known anywhere else, showing how little is actually available to us for the administrative structure of the city on the detailed level (OSP 2 049, OSP 2 060).526 A man named Lugal-nì-zu, on the other hand, is known to have held both the titles énsi-EN.LÍLki and sanga-dEn-líl in a number of brick stamps from Nippur (E2.6.2.1).527 The

question concerned here is not particularly the life career of Lugal-nì-zu but the office of sanga èš-dEn-líl attested in parallel to sanga èš-dNin-urta (CBS 8417).528 According to Westenholz, the temple of Enlil is separated from the administration of the city of Nippur now, under the offices of sangadEn-líl and énsi-EN.LÍLki respectively. The temple of Ninurta was presumably located in the West Mound of Nippur, not far from where the Onion Archive and the Barton Cylinder were found.529

The deity Ninurta is known from Pre-Sargonic Nippur personal names, toponyms and probably official titles.530 In OSP 2 074 found from the West Mound, roughly contemporary with the Enlilemaba archive, an oath was taken by the name of Ninurta and of the king, a practice to be expected with the patron deity of a locality.531 The deity Ninurta was marked in a Pre-Sargonic or Early Sargonic seal impression specifically as énsi-EN.LÍLki (MC 4 011).532 In the Barton Cylinder discussed in the last chapter, Ninurta was dumu dNin-ḫur-sag through which relationship his sonship to Enlil came to be established. Ninurta occupied the first place following èš-[EN.LÍLki] in the offering lists from Nippur dated to the time of Sargon (ECTJ 033, ECTJ 085, ECTJ 154). É-dNin-urta had been the recipient of an offering for the new moon festival, sag-ITI.SAR, made by Lugalzagesi (ECTJ 082). An Ur III text, CBS 13735, found from the West Mound, the same find spot as that of OSP 2 074, agrees with the latter in that the oath was taken by dNin-urta and the king.533 On the other hand, Ninurta was insignificant in the god-lists from the Fāra Period and was not mentioned at all in the Zà-mì Hymns from Abū Ṣalābīḫ.534
530 Ur-dNin-urta (OSP 1 029, OSP 1 122, OSP 1 020, ECTJ 011, ECTJ 080), Sipa-dNinurta (OSP 1 118), Uru-dNin-urta (ECTJ 021, ECTJ 022, ECTJ 024, ECTJ 122), aša5dNin-urta (ECTJ 070, ECTJ 134), kíndagal dNin-urta (OSP 1 083), nin dNin-urta (ECTJ 176). See Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 147, for the titles kíndagal dNin-urta and nin dNin-urta. 531 Westenholz 1987, p. 85. 532 Steinkeller and Postgate 1992, p. 38. 533 CBS 13735 comes from the West Mound, where Westenholz 1987, pp. 97–98, located the Ninurta temple. Note however, OSP 2 050 in the Enlilemaba archive invoked only the king when taking oath, mu lugal-šè lú lú nu-ba-gi4-gi4-da-a, which will further complicate the issue. 534 dNin-urta in the list SF 001 Col. 2, line 18 may refer to the deity dNám-urta attested in IAS 082, as according to Krebernik 1986, p. 169. Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 144, explained the absence of dNin-urta in the Zà-mì Hymns as due to the principle of “eine Stadt ein Gott” kept in the composition. This is to say, Ninurta



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER
192
The problem with the relationship between Ninurta and Nippur is that, the sources do not support a definitive statement that it was the patron deity of the city before the ED IIIb Period.535 In my view, Ninurta actually rose to power in the transition from the Early Dynastic to the Old Akkadian Period, however this may seem hardly meaningful a suggestion.536 The rise of Ninurta came to be confirmed by its sonship of Enlil dramatically established in the Barton Cylinder. The sonship to Enlil most likely also went hand in hand with its becoming the patron of Nippur, for which reason it could be called énsi-EN.LÍLki, a literal explanation of its patronage of the city. The invocation of Ninurta in the oath of OSP 2 074 can be safely dated after the attestation of Ninurta as énsi-EN.LÍLki. The institution èš-Nibruki in the Barton Cylinder must have been the same as èš-dEn-líl, oikos of Enlil, as no evidence shows Ninurta had a separate èš already in Pre-Sargonic times. The institution èš-dNin-urta that hosted the sanga èš-dNin-urta was probably enhanced or established anew to be the patron’s oikos after Ninurta became the patron deity, and the establishment coincided with the rise of the Sargonids.537 The title sanga-dEn-líl or sanga èš-dEn-líl held by the énsi of Nippur Lugalnizu would have had responsibilities beyond Nippur probably exactly because he held this higher office, while the office of sanga èš-dNin-urta was local to the city. Lugalnizu’s holding of both the énsi of Nippur and sanga-dEn-líl was exceptional though the two could be so combined if the king so wished. The understanding that sanga-dEn-líl was interregional in character and was probably immediately under the king’s control also suits well the

And as an ex voto of that for the life of the sanga, the dedication was made for the life of an unidentified person of concern, Ama-áb-zi, as well as for the life of the latter’s wife and children (E2.6.1.2001).540 The office of dub-sar-maḫ? é-dEn-líl must be directly under the administration of sanga-dEn-líl.

4.2.3 Royal Titles551 4.2.3.1 ÉNSI dEn-líl
ÉNSI dEn-líl is the Akkadian version of the Sumerian énsi-gal-dEn-líl according to the bilingual inscription of Sargon (*E2.1.1.1).552 The Akkadian version of the title appears elsewhere when the same “religious titles” of Sargon were enumerated (*E2.1.1.2, *E2.1.1.6). This KALAM, broadly speaking, lies in between “the Upper Sea and the Lower Sea” that Enlil gave to Sargon, a-ab-ba-IGI.NIM-ma-ta a-ab-ba-sig-sig-šè dEn-líl-le mu-na-sum/ti-am-tám a-lí-tám ù śa-pil-tám dEn-líl i-dì-nu-śum6, but does not include Ma-rí.KI, Elam.KI, and Kiš.KI (*E2.1.1.1, *E2.1.1.2). The difference is that the inscriptions of Sargon did not mention the two rivers, Idigna and Buranun, in the context of lugal-kalam-ma, in this case more likely due to the different routes to Idigna and Buranun from Akkade and Uruk respectively. Lugalzagesi probably took Enlil as his personal god since the order of An, dEn-líl and Lugalzagesi in his inscriptions shows the same pattern as that of Enlil, dŠul-utul12, and Entemena from Lagaš (Ent 1). 4.2.3.2 SAGI dEn-líl
In a Neo-Babylonian copy of a bilingual inscription Šarkališarri had the titles lugal kalag-ga/da!-núm [x] and sagi (SÌLA.ŠU.DU8) dEn-líl-lá/SAGI (SÌLA.ŠU.DU11) dEn-líl (*E2.1.5.4).557 In the same inscription Šarkališarri is recorded to have reached the sources of the Tigris and the Euphrates, geographically in Anatolia across the Khabur.558 The change of terminology from énsi-gal-dEn-líl/ÉNSI dEn-líl still in use in the inscriptions of Narām-Sîn to sagi dEn-líl-lá/SAGI (SÌLA.ŠU.DU11) for Šarkališarri is worth noting. The use of the term sagi in this title may suggest to us that the relationship between Šarkališarri and Enlil was by then likened to civil administration, although the probable fact that Šarkališarri ruled no more than the Akkad region and the Diyala would make this newly defined relationship between the king and the god duly qualified.559
4.2.4 Titles of Enlil
In the text of *E2.1.1.15 of Sargon Enlil was called ŚA.DÚ ì-li ra-bí-um, “the great mountain of the gods”.560 ŚA.DÚ in Sargonic Nuzi was attested with dUTU in a personal name dUTU-ŚA.DÚ (HSS 10 188). The word ŚA.DÚ would be derived from the Akkadian šadûm, “mountain”, therefore the

Note that after Lugalzagesi, the juxtaposition of Idigna and Buranun did not reappear in royal inscriptions untill as late as the time of Narām-Sîn (*E2.1.4.2, E2.1.4.28, MAD 1 231, MAD 1 236). 559 Cf. meaning of ŚA.DÚ ì-li ra-bí-um, “the great mountain of the gods”.561 It needs to be noted that although Enlil had shared identical contexts with dUtu in personal names since the Fāra Period, no personal name from texts earlier than the Sargonic Period is known precisely as *ŚA.DÚ-dEn-líl or *dEn-líl-ŚA.DÚ, nor in the Sargonic Period. ŚA.DÚ was likely to have been an import by the Sargonids from their homeland where deities could be described in terms of mountains.562 dEn-líl is known also as LUGAL ì-li in an Ur III copy of an inscription from a statue dedicated to Enlil by Šarkališarri (*E2.1.5.6). The title here would be compatible with ŚA.DÚ ì-li ra-bí-um, since both would have conveyed essentially the same meaning for the Sargonids. If considering lugal-kur-kur-ra and ab-ba-dingir-dingir-ré-ne, titles of Enlil in PreSargonic Sumerian inscriptions, the Akkadian ŚA.DÚ seemed to describe of both the Sumerian lugal, “king”, and ab-ba, “elder”.563 The Sumerian kur has the meaning ša-du-um, thus also suggesting a correlation of the Akkadian ŚA.DÚ ì-li ra-bí-um with dEn-líl(É) kur-gal in the Zà-mì Hymns.564 The somehow unexpected absence of kur-gal in PreSargonic Sumerian inscriptions, as noticed in the last chapter, makes the correlation far-reaching, since the Semitic ŚA.DÚ seems to recall more readily the earlier mythic tradition. The correlations of the Sargonic titles of Enlil with those known in Pre-Sargonic Sumerian would bring one to recall Piesl’s interprettion introduced in the introduction, that kur was the “Numinose Macht” of the Sumerian “Kollektiv” to be taken over by Enlil.565 In her scenario, “die Anthropomorphisierung” happened when Enlil took over the é-kur,
561 Steinkeller 1980 considers ŚA.DÚ-ì to mean “easterner”. But this cannot explain the term being used for the Amorites, as pointed out in Frayne 1992b, p. 627. See Hasselbach 2005, p. 284, for ŚA.DÚ-ì, /sadū’ē?/, as “mountaineers”. 562 Cf. di Vito 1993, pp. 249–250. 563 If so, we may even suppose that dEn-líl lugal-dingir-re-ne found in Gudea was a re-translation of the title into Sumerian.
