200
the “Machtzentrum der jeweiligen menschlichen Gemeinschaft”.566 Enlil together with a Semitic “*’ll-Theologie” associated with it reorganized the Sumerian system thereafter, though “eine Angleichung und zum Teil Identifizierung mit dem alten Numen kur(.gal)” could still be recognized in literary texts and in personal names from Fāra.567 If the above association of Enlil with the Akkadian word ŚA.DÚ could indeed retrospectively explain his title kur-gal, the order of things becomes the reverse. The title kur-gal is no longer the reminiscence of the remote Sumerian past but a part of the ideological load that was newly brought in. In my interpretation of the original reading of EN.KID, I considered that the writing of the city name pronounced somehow close to /nibru/ obtained the reading /ellil/ or /illil/ only later, when the “divinity” now known by us as ENLIL was associated with the city. I was inclined to put the time in which EN.KID obtained the reading /ellil/ or /illil/ and the “deification” of NIBRU to be ENLIL in between the Late Uruk to the Early Dynastic Period. I abstained in Part I, however, from introducing the ethnic affiliations of those partaking in this process since the “labelling” of NIBRU with ENLIL must be anyway in a multi-ethnic world. If allowed to go further starting from the observation made here that kur-gal the title of Enlil may actually be a part of the ideological load brought in at the beginning of the cult of Enlil in Nippur, it would be more likely that Enlil was originally from the Semitic milieu. The heavy consequence of this interpretation to our understanding of the “religious history” of early Mesopotamia, that at least partially a Semitic theology of Enlil was brought into the Sumerian tradition, may be seen through a new look at kur-gal in early Early Dynastic III sources. The ED Names of Professions List contains an official pap-kur-gal (IAS 061, IAS 073-074, IAS 080, MEE 3 043). And perhaps not accidentally the successor and son of king Ur-dNanše was named A-kur-gal (Akg 1, Akg 7). If the lexical texts from Abū Ṣalābīḫ and Ebla are not unexpected to have Semitic influence,
566 Cf. Piesl 1969, pp. 111–115. 567 Piesl 1969, p. 111.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER 201
the name of the Lagašite king, if so interpreted, would speak of the depth of the transformation. In relation to our retrospection here, I noted in Part III that é-kur is known in Pre-Sargonic Nippurian sources only from personal names and beyond this there is no contemporary evidence in support of restoring the term in the text of the Stele of the Vultures. I explained there that, although the city Nibruki may have been known in Lagaš as the residence city of Enlil but going one step further, it is unknown whether Enlil was housed in a temple named é-kur at the time of Eannatum. It is unfortunate that no architectural evidence of the Ekur is found in situ before the time of Narām-Sîn.568 And I am going to argue that, the name Ekur for the temple of Enlil in Nippur might even be a Sargonic creation. Here, seeing the evidence above as interpreted in this section, it may already be more confidently suggested that the temple of Enlil in Nippur was unlikely have been known as Ekur in the earlier days of the Early Dynastic Period. In spite of Piesl’s speculative reconstruction for the process of Anthropomorphisierung and so forth, the myth of Ekur being the name of the temple of Enlil in Nippur from time immemorial has to be abandoned to pave the way of a much later dating for it.
4.2.5 Curse Formulae 4.2.5.1 dEn-líl ù dUTU
In the Sargonic inscriptions dedicated to Enlil in times after Sargon, dUTU was often the deity following Enlil in the curses, a simple fact but not easily explained. The explanation for the appearance of dUTU in these curses, in my perception, may be inferred first from *E2.1.1.1 of Sargon in which dUTU was the single deity in the curse though the dedication was made to dEn-líl.569 dUTU in this curse was responsible for both suḫuš-a-ni
568 Cf. Gibson, Hansen et al. 2001 and McCown and Haines 1967, pp. 1–2. 569 Franke 1995, p. 226, n. 84, noted Šamaš is the only deity in the curse formula of *E2.1.1.1 dedicated to Enlil but continued to accept the principle that, the deity at the beginning of the curse formula is the one to whom the dedication



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER
202
ḫé-bù-re6/SUḪUŠ-śu li-sú-uḫ and numun-a-ni hé-ga-ri -ri-ge/ŠE.NUMUNśu li-il-qù-ut, “tear out his foundations and destroy his progeny”. In the curse of *E2.1.1.11, dINANNA is responsible for e [x] dumu-na-ni hé-ku5-e, “cut off his ... offspring” and Enlil responsible for numun-na-ni ḫé-til-le, “bring his progeny to an end”. Most helpfully, the verbs in the curse of *E2.1.1.8 in which dEn-líl, dUTU and dINANNA appear together were certainly dual.570 The use of the dual verbal forms in the curse of *E2.1.1.8 may be explained by assuming that each of the cursing capacities could be achieved by two of the three deities mentioned namely because dUTU and dEn-líl had both the cursing capacity concerning numun/ŠE.NUMUN, and because dUTU and dINANNA both the cursing capacity concerning SUḪUŠ, the use of the dual forms was actually necessary. This interpretation of the dual forms of the verbs in the curse of *E2.1.1.8 helps to account for dUTU as often being the deity following Enlil in Sargonic curses when the dedications were actually made to the latter alone, since dUTU at least shared with Enlil the responsibility for numun/ŠE.NUMUN. There seemed, however, even more common characteristics of dUTU and Enlil. The Nuzi name dUTU-ŚA.DÚ quoted above needs to be seen in the context of other ŚA.DÚ names from the north, in which it was often composed with the “lord” or the “elder” in a way not unlike the elements that was combined with Enlil in Sumerian personal names.571 If the use of the term ŚA.DÚ in the title ŚA.DÚ ì-li ra-bí-um was due to the influence by the tradition brought in to Sumer from the homeland of the Sargonids,
was made. 570 The curse of *E2.1.1.8 according to Frayne 1993: ša DUB śu4-a u-śa-sà-ku-ni dEnlíl dUTU ù dINANNA SUḪUŠ-śu li-sú-ḫa ù ŠE.NUMUN-śu li-il-qù-tá, “as for the one who removes this inscription, may the gods Enlil, Šamaš and Aštar tear out his foundations and destroy his progeny.” 571 Note: A-bu-ŚA.DÚ (MAD 1 232), ŚA.DÚ-ì-lum (MAD 5 038), Be-lí-ŚA.DÚ (BIN 08 122, HSS 10 041, HSS 10 205), ŚA.DÚ-AN (HSS 10 108, HSS 10 118, HSS 10 146, HSS 10 153-155, HSS 10 157, MAD 5 067).



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER 203
dUTU would seem to be a figure over there that was somehow compatible with Enlil’s role in the southern trandition before the Sargonids took over the south. It should not be misplaced here also to recall that in ED IIIa Fāra, Utu, though without the divine determinative, was one of the elements most compatible with Enlil in their onomastic contexts, and that dUtu was one of the divine lugals in the Stele of the Vultures (Ean 1). The fact that dUTU was the only deity in the curse of *E2.1.1.1 may therefore be further interpreted as one of the early versions of the “interpretation” of Enlil by the Sargonids, drawing on a tradition that was shared in their homeland for some time already. The interpretation concerning the role of dUTU in the homeland of the Sargonids can be only hypothetical as Sargonic curse formulae were stylized so soon that they can provide no more evidence for their earliest perception of Enlil as in relation to dUTU. This stylization, nevertheless, did not make the invocation of deities in curses entirely meaningless even in later times. This point may be indirectly supported by the curses of E2.1.5.1 and E2.1.5.2, both of which began with dEn-líl the recipient of dedication except that dINANNA was inserted into the curse of E2.1.5.1.572 The attestation of dINANNA in E2.1.5.1 may have cross-referenced to the title LUGAL A-kà-dè.KI ù bù-ú-la-ti dEn-líl which was absent in E2.1.5.2.
4.2.5.2 GIDRU a-na dEn-líl
In *E2.1.4.3 and *E2.1.4.5 of Narām-Sîn, both dedicated to Enlil and both on the conquest of Magan, the curses took prolonged forms hitherto unknown: dINANNA-an-nu-ni-tum AN dEn-líl ìl-a-ba4 dEN.ZU dUTU dNergal dU-um dNin-kar-ak DINGIR ra-bí-ù-tum in ŠU.NÍGIN-su4-nu ar-ra-tám la-mutám li-ru-úś GIDRU a-na dEn-líl śar-ru-tám a-na dINANNA a u-kí-il, “May the gods Aštar-Annunītum, Anum, Enlil, Ilaba, Sîn, Šamaš, Nergal, Um, Ninkarrak, the great gods in their totality curse him with a terrible curse,
572 Franke 1995, p. 226: “die Nennung von Ištar wäre eine Besonderheit”.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER
204
may he not hold the sceptre for Enlil, kingship for Aštar.”573 The DINGIR in DINGIR ra-bí-ù-tum will need to be read ilū to agree with the plural form of rabûm (*E2.1.4.3, *E2.1.4.5). The deities invoked here would have constitute the formal pantheon recognized in the time of Narām-Sîn.574 The lead position of dINANNA-Annunītum recalls the “religious” titles of Sargon in *E2.1.1.1, LUGAL A-kà-dè.KI MAŠKIM.GI4 dINANNA LUGAL KIŠ PA4.ŠEŠ AN LUGAL KALAM.MA.KI ÉNSI dEn-líl. The same rationale would have counted for the leading position of dINANNA-an-nu-ni-tum in the inscriptions of Narām-Sîn. The phrase GIDRU a-na dEn-líl śar-ru-tám a-na dINANNA in the curses shows that dINANNA could be associated with śar-ru-tám, but this is not to be considered evidence for the hypothetical “Aufstieg von Ištar” particularly at the expense of Enlil, since GIDRU, “the sceptre”, remained with Enlil. It was Enlil who granted the sceptre to Sargon and to Maništušu (*E2.1.1.15, *E2.1.3.1).575 In E2.1.4.24, from the stele found in Pir Hüseyn, dEN.KI was the one who gave no rival to Narām-Sîn in the four quarters, and in *E2.1.4.25 dINANNA did the same, but in both of these latter cases the granting of the sceptre was not used to describe the deities’ actions. The Barton Cylinder spoke of gidru-kù dEn-líl, “the pure sceptre of Enlil”, in the context of the two rivers, the Tigris and the Euphrates. The attestation of gidru-kù in the Barton Cylinder was probably the same
573 Frayne 1993, p. 102. 574 Roberts 1972, p. 145. Franke 1995, pp. 226–227, suggested that the lists of deities in these curses should be understood “im Laufe der Zeit zum Stilmittel”, and compared the list to that in the prologue of Codex Hammurapi. 575 Cf. Such-Gutiérrez 2003, pp. 38–39, also Roberts 1972, p. 145. Westenholz 1999, p. 39, saw no evidence “that the Akkadian kings ever tried to promote the cult of Inanna at the expense of other gods.” It needs to be noted that nam-lugal/ šarrūtum in Old Babylonian texts from the time of Išme-Dāgan was synonymous with nam-bára, while nam-dEn-líl/ellilūtu referred to “sovereignty” there. Note also that in the Bāseṭkī Statue, the Inanna attested there was that of Eanna, but not the dINANNA of Akkade, as one in the same rank with Enlil (E2.1.4.10).



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER 205
gidru known from the title of Entemena, gidru-sum-ma-dEn-líl-lá (Ent 28-29). The inscriptions of Entemena mentioned the granting of the gidru by Enlil in Nippur (Ent 32). The matter of concern in these Pre-Sargonic texts must be the same in the Sargonic inscriptions as described by the term GIŠ.GIDRU which is granted by Enlil.576 The “pure sceptre”, gidru in Sumerian and haṭṭu in Akkadian, may actually refer to some material object which symbolized power. The fact that no such object has been identified in archaeological findings may prefer the interpretation that it was a metaphor without a given material form but this can easily be explained by the high-value of the material of the “sceptre”, as usually the case with objects of cultic importance in Mesopotamia, presumably re-used later for other purposes.
4.2.6 dEn-líl in EN.LÍL.KI
In *E2.1.4.26, the god Dagan determined the verdict for Narām-Sîn and delivered into his hands Armānum and Ebla. The lords of the upper lands, EN.EN a-lí-a-tim, and the governors of Subartu, ÉNSI.ÉNSI ŠUBUR.KI, submitted to Narām-Sîn since they revered Enlil, ki-ma dEn-líl i-pá-la-ḫu (*E2.1.4.2). In the Bāseṭkī Statue, dINANNA in É-an-na-ki-im, dEn-líl in EN.LÍL.KI, dDa-gan in Tu-tu-li.KI, dNin-ḫur-sag in Kèš.KI, dEN.KI in Eridu.KI, dEN.ZU in ÚRI.KI dUTU in DINGIR.UD.KIB.NUN.KI, and dNergal in Gú-du8a.KI, were called upon to acknowledge Narām-Sîn as the god of the city of Akkade (E2.1.4.10).577 It can be observed that, the arrangement of the deities in the Bāseṭkī inscription is similar in principle to that in the description of the oath taking section of the Stele of the Vultures by the Umma ruler with the sa-šuš-gal of dEn-líl, dNin-ḫur-sag, dEn-ki, dSu’en, and dUtu, respectively (Ean 1). In the inscription of Narām-Sîn, the major difference is, dINANNA
576 Note that Braun-Holzinger 1996 considers the “Stab”, “staff” in English, represented in Old Babylonian glyptic iconography as the emblem of Enlil. 577 Frayne 1993, pp. 113–114.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER
206
in É-an-na-ki-im and dDa-gan in Tu-tu-li.KI are further included in the list. The mentioning of EN.LÍL.KI in the “offering list” in the Akkadian archive from Nippur, as mentioned earlier in this chapter, perhaps needs to be seen in the light of dEn-líl in EN.LÍL.KI here . Enlil was attested in the Nippur text as dEn-líl EN.LÍL.KI, “Enlil in/of Nippur” associated with “24 jars of wine, 30 jars of honey, 20 baskets of sweet almonds, 20 baskets of almonds” (OSP 2 001). In the light of the Bāseṭkī Statue, the mentioning of EN.LÍL.KI in OSP 2 001 would have particularly stressed the cultic background of the offering, namely the national-wide administration of cults in which Dagan in/of Tuttul was a counterpart of Enlil in/of Nippur, instead of that Enlil was local at Nippur. In addition, the attestation of dUTU in DINGIR.UD.KIB.NUN.KI, “dUTU in/of Sippar”, in the inscription E2.1.4.10, confirms our observation made above that dUTU was somehow comparable to the role of Enlil in the argonic perception probably as early as before they took “the land”.
4.2.7 The Objects of Dedication
In the following abstraction of information from the Sargonic royal inscriptions dedicated to Enlil, the material form on which each of the inscriptions is known to have been inscribed is provided after the siglum given by RIME 2. The royal titles attested in the text of the inscription are given under category a in their original sequence. In category b, on the other hand, it is to be found the geography concerned in the text, namely the localities mentioned in its narrative part. The names of the deities that appear in the curse formula of the same inscription are listed under c. If there is no information available, the correspondent category will be simply left unrepresented.

The statue with the inscription *E2.1.2.4 could actually be dedicated by Lú-dDa-mu instead of the king.595 This is however not a big issue since the dedication to Enlil is justified for any significant military leader. The texts of the inscriptions and the Old Babylonian captions and colophons attest to that, Sargonic dedications to Enlil were mostly made by kings, necessarily the most powerful figures in the military life of the time. The dedications were on average larger in size and handcraft complexity than Pre-Sargonic dedications. The material price of such costly dedications stemmed from the Sargonic luxurious style in appealing to the gods, not the piety of the Sargonic kings. It is illegitimate to judge the piety of the dedicator on the basis of the size or value of the dedication.
4.3 ki-en-gi ki-uri in the “Enḫeduanna” Temple Hymn
The daughter of Sargon En-ḫé-du7-an-na was installed by her father
593 *E2.1.5.4 is from a Neo-Babylonian copy. See Oelsner 1989, pp. 404–407. 594 Cf. Frayne 1993, p. 195 595 Such-Gutiérrez 2003, p. 33, n. 41.

in Ur as the entu-priestess of Nanna and was actually attested as MUNUS. NUNUZ.ZI.dNANNA dam-dNanna in the Disk of Enheduanna (E2.1.1.16).596 The Sumerian Temple Hymns were traditionally ascribed to Enheduanna and in fact have been considered representative of the official theology of the Sargonic Period based on this attribution.597 The attribution of the authorship of the Sumerian Temple Hymns to the Sargonic princess, in my perception, would have resulted from a different perception of authorship than that of the modern era when authorship is immediately associated with the concept of patent. Otherwise it will be difficult to explain that at least some of the hymns in the collection and some of the other literary works also ascribed to Enheduanna are indeed difficult to be from her time.598 This being said in general, the question of historicity remains with the hymn in praise of Ekur in particular (TH 002):599

temen ... settlement of Enlil ... your right and your left are ki-en-gi and ki-uri. House of Enlil, your interior is cool, your exterior determines destiny. Your door-jambs and architrave are a mountain summit, your projecting pilasters a dignified mountain. Your peak is ... peak of your princely platform. Your base serves heaven and earth. 34. Your prince, the great prince Enlil, the good lord, the lord of the limits of heaven, the lord who determines destiny. O shrine Nibru, the Great Mountain Enlil, has erected a house in your precinct, and taken his seat upon your dais. 13 lines: the house of Enlil in Nibru.”
In the “Enheduanna” temple hymn, the term ki-uri appears in juxtaposition with ki-en-gi in the Hymn to é-dEn-líl, zi-da-[zu] gáb!-bu-zu kien-gi ki-uri, “your right and your left are ki-en-gi and ki-uri”, which is to be understood as “your south and your north are ki-en-gi and ki-uri”. The Sumerian system of orientation supposes one to face the east, so that the right directs to the south and the left to the north.600 In relation to ki-en-gi and ki-uri, the Caption 2 of the inscription of *E2.1.4.5, of Narām-Sîn, which is “known from two partially preserved Old Babylonian tablet copies from Ur”, can be read as KI.TA GÌR.NITA KI.EN.GI KI.URI Lugal-uru-si, “Below, the general of ki-en-gi and ki-uri, Lugal-uru-si”.601 The caption of *E2.1.4.5, so far known to me, is the earliest historical attestation of the two terms strictly in juxtaposition to evidence that they were both geographical designations. It needs to be noted, however, that the border point was not specified as Nippur in the caption of *E2.1.4.5. I will discuss the term ki-en-gi in the concluding part, where I will argue that ki-en-gi originally referred to a smaller region than the later Sumer, at least so in the Fāra Period. The region ki-en-gi in Southern Babylonia was culturally important for the Sumerians, so that the entire Sumer was called by it in later times. The Fāra sources suggest also that
600 Wyatt 2001, p. 46. 601 Cf. Frayne 1993, p. 100, and Kienast and Sommerfeld 1994, p. 107.



PART IV: ENLIL IN THE TIME OF NARĀM-SÎN AND AFTER 215
there was some important link between Enlil and ki-en-gi from early on so that the elucidation of the ideological significance of ki-en-gi ki-uri forms an essential part of the history of Enlil. In this context, it needs to be noted that, ki-uri, the Sumerian word for Akkad, was not used in royal titulary earlier than the Ur III Period when lugal ki-en-gi ki-uri was indeed popular with the kings. For our purpose here, suffice it to say that, it cannot be uncritically taken that the “Enheduanna” temple hymn does reflect the historical situation in the Sargonic Period. The phrase ki-en-gi ki-uri was more likely used in the context of military administration in the Sargonic Period, as the title of Lugalurusi hints, while the ideological load as put into the term phrase by the Ur III kings was perhaps not fully developed yet.






Part V: The Metamorphosis of Enlil
I presented in the previous chapters the primary sources through which the social relations Enlil was involved in could be deduced. In this part I will summarize the findings of the previous chapters, but no less importantly I will attempt to relate Enlil to the historical developments of early Mesopotamia in which the history of the deity has to be located. The history of early Mesopotamia from the Late Uruk Period to the Sargonic Period, however, remains largely “proto-historic”. It is often difficult for current understandings of the general historical developments of Mesopotamia to directly shed light on more specific topics, and it actually makes better sense to understand early Mesopotamian history from the perspective of diachronic studies on smaller scale. In this way my study may as well contribute to the history of early Mesopotamia in general though I have to be aware that my concern is primarily with Enlil and Nippur. I continue the previous practice in this part to use the term “PreSargonic” as the equivalent of ED IIIb as defined after ED IIIa and before the Akkadian Dynasty under Narām-Sîn and Šarkališarri, the latter is defined the Sargonic Period. “Pre-Sargonic” is therefore a chronological term with a defined beginning and end, not a general term meaning all times before the Akkadian Dynasty as it is sometimes used by others. In this part, the previous chapters will be cross-referenced in brackets and by section numbers, for instance, §1.1.1 refers to section 1.1.1 in Part I. I will present relevant source materials in some cases if this is necessary, regardless of whether the materials had been already quoted in the main text. At the end of this chapter, I will reiterate in the most concise manner my outline of the history of Enlil in early Mesopotamia, which I



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
218
call the Metamorphosis of Enlil, to complete the thesis.
5.1 EN+KID = NIBRU in the Late Uruk Period
The Archaic City List fragment W21126, the Archaic City Seal, the few quoted administrative texts from Uruk III Jemdet Nasr, and the slightly later Uruk City Sealing W11456, are the only sources available for ENLIL/NIBRU before the Early Dynastic Period. In re-examining these sources, I arrived at the conclusion that the writing of NIBRU the city name in the Uruk III Period was EN.KID and that there lacks support for thinking that this was also the writing of ENLIL as a deity (§1.3).
5.1.1 The Writing and Reading of NIBRU
The entry ENa.KIDa for NIBRU in the Archaic City List was likely derived from the ENx+KIDx? part in ENx+KIDx?.NUNx? as represented on the Archaic City Seal, on which the ENx+KIDx? part of the sign combination must be a compound instead of two separate signs (§1.1.1). It is important to note that the name ENa.KID in the Uruk City Sealing W11456 followed the convention of cuneiform writing as represented by the Archaic City List rather than that of the Archaic City Seal, namely ENa.KID on W11456 stood for NIBRU without the NUNx? element (§1.1.2). This as a matter of fact suggests that soon after cuneiform writing was applied to the city name, the principle of glyptic representation switched from metonymic graphic depiction to that of writing, and in the process the originally inseparable compound ENx+KIDx was re-interpreted as two separate signs. The phonetic value of the combination ENx+KIDx?.NUNx? for NIBRU on the Archaic City Seal was presumably the same as that of ENa.KIDa in the Archaic City List, in the Uruk City Sealing, and likely also the same as in the administrative texts from Uruk III Jemdet Nasr. It is not certain whether the phonetic value in question was /nibru/, similar to the later city name, or /ellil/ similar to the later divine name, but the former is more likely, since the attestations of EN.KID are on all occasions interpretable as a toponym while hardly anywhere could it be taken as a



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 219
divine name. I hypothesize that ENa+KIDa or ENx+KIDx was primarily the name of the site Niffar and was close to /nibru/ in pronunciation, while later it obtained the reading /ellil/ or /illil/ when the same written form was taken to write the name of ENLIL as a deity. I abstain from introducing the problem of the ethnic affiliations of the humans partaking in this process since the “labelling” of NIBRU with ENLIL must be in a multiethnic world. The possibility exists that ENLIL was originally from the Semtic milieu (§4.2.4). The time when EN.KID obtained the new reading would be somewhere in the transition from the Late Uruk Period to the Early Dynastic Period in relative chronology (§1.3).
5.1.2 NIBRU and the “Inanna Amphictyony”
Matthews read on the Archaic City Seal Ur, Larsa, Nippur, Uruk, Keš, Zabala in sequence. The Archaic City List fragment W21126 preserved in sequence Ur, Nippur, Larsa, Uruk. The Uruk city sealing W11456 contained the names Nippur, Larsa, Uruk, Keš.602 It has been seriously challenged that the Archaic City Seal bears any relevance to its historical setting.603 The critical question is whether the semiotics of cultural objects such as the Archaic City Seal could represent historical reality.604 The arrangements of entries on the three major sources datable to the Uruk III Period were not identical to one another. If the sequence was seen as having any significance, the reason for the different arrangements of entries could only be that they were sensitive to the changing circumstances in the real world from which the sources came. I think the arrangement of the entries on the Archaic City Seal as well as in the City List and the Uruk sealing reflected historical realities. The attestations of the Archaic City Seal impression on a particular
602 See Matthews 1993, p. 39, Table 4, for a synopsis. 603 Michalowski 1993, p. 128, basically refrained from historical reconstruction based on the Archaic City Seal. He prefers to study the Seal semiologically. 604 Yoffee 1993a, p. 304, and Yoffee 1993b, p. 66.

type of tablets would indicate that the seal was used for the validation or authorization of the transactions. The attestation of the Archaic City List suggests that the grouping was perpetuated in the archaic scribal school, and W11456 shows again the administrative relevance of the grouping of city names that indicated “an intimacy of contact across the limits of southern Mesopotamia”.605 The tablets bearing the Archaic City Seal recorded small quantities of goods.606 Steinkeller further defined them as receipts of offerings.607 He understood the line NIa+RUa 3N57 MUŠ3a UNUGa on the city-sealed type of tablets from Jemdet Nasr as “the city NI.RU (= ancient Jemdet Nasr), for the triple Inanna of Uruk”, in which “the triple Inanna of Uruk” referred to the combination of MUŠ3a UDa, MUŠ3a SIGa, and MUŠ3a NUNa in archaic Uruk.608 ATU 1 656 probably from Tell Uqair bearing the same Archaic City Seal impression recorded goods similar to those of the Jemdet Nasr texts and contained a notation, UR2a.KU6a.RADa MAḪa×NAa ANa 3N57 PAPa UNUGa.609 Steinkeller took UR2a.KU6a.RADa as the name of Urum/Tell Uqair and interpreted the AN as DINGIR referring to Inanna “the” DINGIR of Uruk, so the line “the city of UR2a.KU6a.RADa (= Urum/Tell Uqair) ... for the triple Inanna of Uruk”.610

PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 221
As Steinkeller states, “[t]hese conclusions, if correct, would obviously be of great importance, for they would provide us with the first tangible evidence of a pan-Babylonian organizational scheme in the Uruk III period.”611 The pan-Babylonian organization, according to Steinkeller, had its focus on the Inanna of Uruk for whom ritual obligations on the part of the member cities were imposed. The collectors of these offerings validated the transactions in Jemdet Nasr and Tell Uqair with the Archaic City Seal so that we have the Archaic City Seal impressions on the tablets known above, which were the receipts of offerings. The organization might have had economic or political objectives but was fundamentally an amphictyonic scheme primarily concerned with cultic matters.612 Steinkeller was worried about the absence of Uruk on the seal, since the sign that Matthews read as UNUGx for Uruk was the Uruk IV form of URUa = ZATU597 but not UNUG for Uruk. It seems to me Matthews was right on this point, however, since URUa for UNUGx makes good sense if considering that the Archaic City Seal was cut in a way resembling a miniature relief better to be “read” in three dimensions.613 That the Archaic City Seal left no trace in the rather extensive findings from Uruk and the fact that the tablets bearing the seal came from Jemdet Nasr and Tell Uqair may suggest that the actual transactions using the seal happened in central Babylonia. The fact that fragments of the Archaic City List and certainly the Uruk City Sealing W11456 were found in Uruk suggests that the city of Uruk had taken an active part in the inter-city activities involving far away cities. Such inter-city activities, however, do not seem to be exclusively or even primarily concerned with cultic purposes. Archaeological surveys had discovered a group of towns along a canal to the south of Umma appeared in the Late Uruk Period. The surveyors considered that the formation of these new towns was perhaps some “federative activity”.614
611 Steinkeller 2002a, p. 256. 612 See Finer 1997, pp. 372–381, for a descrption of the Greek Amphictyony. 613 Cf. Steinkeller 2002a, p. 255, n. 29. 614 Adams and Nissen 1972, p. 12.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
222
It may be too hasty to relate the formation of this group of new towns, which is admittedly hypothetical for the surveyors as well, directly to the Archaic City Seal, but the like of such activities involving both NIBRU and Uruk, with more cities certainly also involved, would not be far from the scene.615 The immediate causes of collective activities such as building new towns would be likely military, especially if we take into consideration that these towns are located on the front-line to the Elamite highlands, but the ultimate drive must be economic interests in the broader sense, which were to be militarily protected and ritually consolidated.616 The member cities of the organization, Ur, Larsa, Nippur, Uruk, Keš, Zabala and more, had acted collectively in places of strategic importance, coordinated in a scheme not unlike Steinkeller’s Inanna Amphictyony. It needs to be noted here that Steinkeller in this original thesis did not consider the Uruk City Sealing which is certainly later than the Archaic City Seal. The absence of the city name of Ur on the Uruk City Sealing suggests that the pan-Babylonian organization was not a stable one but was in a process of continuous dividing and reorganizing, since it seems that later than the Archaic City Seal the city of Ur was excluded from the organization in the Uruk version of events.
615 Adams 1981, pp. 67–94, noted the population shift from the Adab-Nippur region to the Uruk region in the Uruk IV Period. See Postgate 1986, pp. 104–105, Fig. 9 and Fig. 10, for the changes in the occupation of sites within the triangle Uruk-Umma-Šuruppak, from the Uruk IV Period to the Early Dynastic I Period. 616 The Uruk Phenomenon would be primarily a commercial phenomenon, of which see especially Stein 1999, Postgate 2003, though the political side of the phenomenon remains too much in darkness. Westenholz 2002, p. 23, used the term “territorial state” for the Late Uruk Period but see Yoffee 2005 on problems in defining “state”. Rothman 2001, Postgate 2002 are recent collections of studies pertaining to the Uruk Phenomenon. van de Mieroop 2004, p. 48, understood the Archaic City Seal as evidence for the existence of a “trade network with a single supervisory body”. Note also, Lieberman 1992 had refused to understand Nippur as “the city of decisions” in terms of “religion”.

5.2 NIBRU/ENLIL in the Early Dynastic I Period
The beginning of the Early Dynastic I Period saw the building of new temple structures including most evidently the Inanna temple at Nippur. The Inanna temple at Nippur was built upon residential layers. This fact, as Zettler noticed and duly stressed, would leave much space for imagination concerning the development of “religious” thinking in early Mesopotamia.617 Enlil would have been established as the patron deity of Nippur alraedy in the Early Dynastic I Period (§5.1.1). But this general suggestion does not presume that the architecture of its “house” in Nippur was similar to that of Inanna or that of any deity anywhere. It is highly unlikely, at the least, that Enlil already had a temple named Ekur at this time (§4.2.4).618 For the city of Nippur or for any other cities, the “patron deity” needs not to be “the” major deity in the city though it would have an oikos there in the name of the city.619 The best criterion for the ancients to distinguish the “patron deity” from other deities, who might be worshipped in the same city, would be in contexts to do with the outside, in which the “patron” represents the city.620
617 Zettler 1992b, pp. 54–55, observed that the building of the Inanna Temple at the site of Niffar seemed to have been imposed on residential layers during the Early Dynastic I Period and asked about its implications for the changes in the religious landscape of early Mesopotamia. Note the term lugal was first attested in ED I Ur personal names, quoted in Jacobsen 1970, p. 141, and Krebernik 2002, pp. 61-65, Table 4. Early Dynastic I Period seems a time of ideological innovation. 618 Heinrich 1984 presented known “temples” from early Mesopotamia, for which see also discussions from a cultural history perspective in Crawford 2004. The Ur III “shrine” of Enlil discussed in Zettler 1984 will show how varied the architectural form and cultic function of a temple could be. 619 See Renger 1994 for the concept of oikos in third millenium Mesopotamia, and Postgate 1994a, pp. 135–136, for the temple as a wealthy neighbour. 620 The concept of patron deity was formulated by Paffrath 1913 See the discussions in Lambert 1975, p. 192, quoted in Selz 1990, p. 116, and Selz 1992, p. 193.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
224
5.2.1 The Writing of NIBRU/ENLIL
The Ur SIS sealings are the only sources known that can put NIBRU/ENLIL in historical terms from the end of the Jamdet Nasr Period to the beginning of the Early Dynastic III Period. The name of NIBRU was UD.EN.KID in U001, while the ANx?.KIDs in U016 and U002 could have the same referent. If the ANx?.KIDs in U016 and U002 were indeed the name of NIBRU the consequence would be that the difficult forms of ANx? for the Ur seal cutters could alternate with the UD sign or the sign combination UD.EN (§1.2). It is difficult to imagine how the indivisible EN+KID compound could further be reduced to a single KID. But it can be certain that the re-interpretation of the name by the ancients must be a part of the development of cuneiform writing, and simultaneously also of the ways of thinking. The plausible fact that the forms of ANx? in the Ur SIS sources could alternate with the UD part in the full name may be interpreted as evidence for the “deification” of the geographical name as well (§1.3).
5.2.2 NIBRU and the “Inanna Amphictyony”
In a communication supplementary to his proposal of the Inanna Amphictyony theory for the Uruk III Period, Steinkeller built on the observation that one-fourth of the Ur SIS city sealings are countersigned with stamp seals, which always depicted a rosette the symbol of Inanna, to reconsider the administrative contexts of the Ur SIS seals.621 The Uruk
621 Steinkeller 2002b acknowledged the observation of the attestation of the rosette in the SIS sealings to Cohen, now published in Cohen 2005, pp. 132–133 and p. 136. Note Steinkeller 1998: “The connection between Inana/Ishtar and the rosette/star is clear throughout ancient Mesopotamian history: from the Uruk art, in which rosette are paired with the volute-like symbols of Inanna, down to the Neo-Babylonian period, which knows the ‘stars (kakkabtu) of the Lady-ofUruk’.” Matthews 1993 interpreted the stamp seals producing the rosette as the end of the cylinder seals which produced the city sealing. He suggested that the rosette “may have designated a general office or level of responsibility, while the

III Jemdet Nasr tablet MSVO 1 221 bears a stamp-seal impression also depicting a rosette but the cylinder rolling on MSVO 1 221 was not from the Archaic City Seal or any recognizable kind of city seal, nor does the text of the tablet relate to Inanna’s cult. Steinkeller was certain that either of these points would clinch the matter of early Babylonian unity conclusively with the central thesis being a pan-Babylonian Inanna Amphictyony from the Uruk III Period into the ED I Period.622 Matthews noted that there is no consistency in the order in which entities were named in the Ur SIS city sealings, and that no geographical pattern can be identified in the attestation of city names in the Ur SIS sealings. The attestation of individual cities on each sealing was not consistent, as in different seal impressions the cities involved were often not the same cities as in other impressions. The arrangements of city names were also not fixed in the Ur SIS seal impressions. In some cases, the entries were organized within one register while in other cases city names may be ordered in two or more registers. The style of the seal impressions varied to a considerable degree, as some of them had their symbols arranged in boxes while the majority did not.623 I agree with Steinkeller that there would be still Amphictyony-like organizations in the Early Dynastic I Period, but the observations above suggest that the organizations were in the process of continuous reconfiguration, rather than consistent and stable. McMahon had noticed that the seals that produced the Ur SIS seal impressions may be made of perishable or recyclable materials.624 The fact that not a single examplar of these seals is known to us, considering the number of seal impressions available to us, would suggest that, the organizational scheme behind them may be also “recyclable” at the time of their production. The Ur SIS sealing U001, on which UD.EN.KID more securely stood for NIBRU, was not countersigned with the rosette, even though the diffi
accompanying cylinder design more specifically designated particular officials.” 622 Steinkeller 2002b, n. 5. 623 Cf. Matthews 1993, pp. 39–41. 624 McMahon 1993, pp. 12–13, n. 7.

226
cult U002 was. It may be that the seals of U001 and U002 were produced in different organizational contexts in which the city of Ur found itself differently situated. The Ur sources are perhaps also not representative of all the activities happening across southern Mesopotamia.625 The city of Ur was attested in all the Ur SIS sealings in which Nippur might have been attested (MSVO 2 U001, MSVO 2 U002, MSVO 2 U016). The other cities that had been attested together with Nippur in the Ur SIS sources include Larsa (U001, U002 , U016), Keš (U001, U002 ), and Adab (U001), but never Uruk (§1.2). It is possible to suggest, therefore, that in the Early Dynastic I Period, Nippur, Larsa, Keš, and Adab were once in closer relationship with one another for some “federative activities” the city of Ur also took part in, although how long this relationship lasted as a political scheme is unknown.626 Furthermore, it is important to note at this point that the exclusion of Ur from the Uruk City Sealing W11456, as pointed out above, finds the other side of the same coin at Ur, in that Uruk was somehow excluded in the Ur version of events particularly in relation to Nippur. The exclusion of Ur in the Uruk source and the exclusion of Uruk in the Ur source, both in relation to Nippur, would suggest that, after the Late Uruk Period and for a certain time, Ur and Uruk were likely competing with each other on the inter-city scale while both maitained a close link to the city of Nippur. The nature of the competition between Ur and Uruk is unknown, but their link to Nippur is probably in continuation to their traditional links to the city as evidenced by the Archaic City Seal (§5.1.2).

See the maps in Pollock 1999, pp. 56–62, reproduced after Adams 1981, for settlement sizes from Uruk IV to the Sargonic Period. 626 Cf. 5.3 ENLIL in the Early Dynastic IIIa Period 5.3.1 The Writings of dEn-líl(É) and dNin-KID
The “standard” orthographical change of the name from Pabilgagi’s AN.EN.KID to that of AN.EN.É in the “god lists” and the administrative texts from Fāra and Abū Ṣalābīḫ is difficult to be taken as simply a switch of scribal coventions though no certain interpretation is possible. I ventured that this orthographical change happened either because an AN.EN.É from somewhere out of Nippur femenized AN.EN.KID to be AN.NIN.KID or because Ninlil was a goddess originally unrelated to Enlil but was married to AN.EN.KID at this time when the latter’s name was changed either due to the switch of scribal covention or the taking over of AN.EN.KID by AN.EN.É. In either case, the orthographic change of the name of Enlil must coincide with another major breakthrough in the history of the deity now characteristically a promiscuous male (§2.3.1). The name of Enlil in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography may however be related to the ancient ENx+KIDx.NUNx? known on the Archaic City Seal, since UD.GAL.NUN seems a joining together of the Late Uruk ENx+KIDx. NUNx? and the ED I Ur SIS forms of ANx?.KID and UD.EN.KID, the divine name as it was written in the place of its home city (§2.3.2). The city name of Nippur on the Archaic City Seal may be the precursor of both the “standard” and the UD.GAL.NUN forms of the divine name. If taking the UD.GAL.NUN system of writing as a kind of cryptography that is meant to deliver hidden message, the divine name of Enlil being so written was probably also an ideological innovation on purpose. The evolvement of the UD.GAL.NUN orthography could not have been without the context of the common use of the “standard” Sumerian orthography in which context it was meaningful that for some reason GAL took over the position of EN.KID when the reading of the name most likely remained /ellil/ or /illil/ (§2.3.2). The UD.GAL.NUN orthographical change as such, though the texts written so are relatively late, must have taken place quite much earlier than ED IIIa.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
228
5.3.2 dEn-líl(É) and the “Politics of Praise”
The Keš Temple Hymn shows that nam-lugal, perhaps already to be interpreted as “kingship” in the Fāra Period, was closely connected to Enlil the “princely lord” (§2.3.3.1). Enlil was characteristically associated with the concept of kingship and this was cosmologically justified by his being the one who separated Heaven from Earth as attested in contemporary sources such as the Zà-mì Hymns. Nippur is the bond of Heaven and Earth for the same reason (§2.3.3.2). The human lugal at this time would be characteristically the arbitrator who had a role similar to that played by Me-salim in the border conflict between Umma and Lagaš.627 The human lugal has to have Enlil on his side to have such authority, through participating in certain rites which were however unlikely to be designed for lugal-Kiški alone. It is possible that, when the OB version of the Keš Temple Hymn were written, the term lugal-Kiški had long ceased to have its special Early Dynastic significance, but the suppression of the reference to lugal-Kiški in the OB version of the Keš Temple Hymn calls for an interpretation if considering the apparently ancient tradition of it going back into the Early Dynastic Period. In my opinion, the suppression of lugal-Kiški in the OB version of the Keš Temple Hymn may be retrospectively explained that the rite recorded in the hymn was designed for lugal in general but not lugal Kiški in particular (§2.3.3.1). The Zà-mì Hymns were arranged by and large in a topographic order but with a prologue. The hymns suggest that in the divine sphere Enlil won recognition technically through being praised by the other deities each of them representing a different cultic centre (§2.3.3.2). Considering that in the Keš Temple Hymn, the human lugal may be recognized as the “king” through his participation in the rite in which Enlil spoke in praise to Keš, the most characteristic action in the ritual procedures of that time would be that of “praise”, the current interpretation of the Sumerian word zà-mì. If these hymns in their original sitz im leben did reproduce the most
627 Cf. Postgate 1994a, pp. 137–141, on the Palace A of Kiš.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 229
characteristic action in the political rituals of the time, it seems the citystates, now we may call them as such, had put much weight on the act of praising in their manner of mutual recognition at that time. The act of praising, in this scenario, was the key gesture of the relevant parties in sharing the ideology of the “kingship” associated with Enlil.628 It would be helpful to draw on its key gesture to lable this type of political ideology as the “Politics of Praise”.629 In the ED IIIa Period and in the culture of the “Politics of Praise”, the human lugal as recognized through rituals involving Enlil would be expected to provide protections and arbitrations for those parties who recognized him. The individual arbitrator most likely accomplished his duty at his own expense, as in the case of Me-salim recorded in the cone of Entemena, who could not have come alone to the border of Umma and Lagaš, the text did not give a hint of who were paying for the arbitrator’s trip. The cities in the “Politics of Praise” system of mutual recognition were able to formulate inter-city organizations on the premise of acknowledging the same arbitrator, but the identity and the origin of the human lugal so recognized must be open to change. In any case, the kind of Amphictyonic organizations likely attested earlier was no longer the norm at least in central and northern Babylonia by the beginning of ED III, when and where the deity Enlil seemed to
628 Note here especially that Bauer 1998, p. 314, took mu-uru-uruki, the “Atlante Geografico”, also as “Preislied auf GN [Göttername]”, despite the viewpoints of Pettinato 1978, Frayne 1992a, Englund 1995 (IAS 091, IAS 106). 629 The use of the phrase of “the Politics of Praise” is inspired by the use of “the Politics of Recognition” in Taylor 1994, though I am aware that my use of the phrase is on a less abstract level. In terms of comparative evidence, I suspect that the scenario, not the details of the “Politics of Praise” in early Mesopotamia was not unlike that of Pre-Modern Bastar in central India as Gell 1999 explains. The point will of course need future explication. The sphere Enlil belonged to in this “Politics of Praise” would be also the “peer polity interaction sphere” in Postgate 1994b, p. 1. See further Postgate 1994a, pp. 262–263, for the rationale in the recognition of political power, “quite simply, success is self-justifying.”



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
230
dramatically sanction the human lugal. Of course, it needs to be reminded that, Babylonian cities did not necessarily have only one lugal at a time as the Sumerian King List would like to suppose, since even if all the Babylonian cities shared the same political culture, they did not have to be organized into a single political community with a single lugal.630 The action of praise may be a common political gesture of the age but it could also be associated with other deities and other designations of rulership, at least in theory. There is no definitive conclusion for the origin of the particular “Politics of Praise” ideology centred upon Enlil and lugal. The Sumerian King List recorded Kiš as the first city to hold nam-lugal after it descended from heaven, but the city of Kiš does not seem to be the home of the political ideology that was particularly associated with Enlil, since apparently, if it were Kiš, one would have to explain why Enlil of Nippur and not Zababa of Kiš was the deity sanctioning kingship. In relation to the central role of Enlil in the “Politics of Praise” associated with it, the switch of models seemed to have not only discontinuities but also continuities from the Late Uruk Period. I noted above that Ur and Uruk were competing with each other after the Late Uruk Period but both of them kept a close link to Nippur. The fact that Ur and Uruk kept their links to Nippur would suggest that their alliance with Nippur was important on the interregional level also from the traditional point of view. Enlil in the new paradigm attested in central Babylonian sources would be therefore perhaps a re-definition of the traditional significance of the city though not a complete innovation. It was perhaps for the traditional significance Nippur held in Babylonia that nam-lugal could be associated in the divine sphere with Enlil, who was by then the patron of the city. The orthographical changes of the name of Enlil illustrated above shows that the historical circumstances in which the re-definition was accomplished must be very complex but unfortunately it is not possible at the present to describe them in more details.
630 See Jacobsen 1939 for the Sumerian King List’s principle of compilation.

5.3.3 ki-en-gi and the “Hexapolis”
