“2360 iku of unkenki land from Adab and Umma, 3110 iku of unkenki land from Lagaš, unkenki (has)? 3980 iku of land. In total 3980 iku of land of Lagaš.”
The term written as EN.GI.KI in the texts of WF 092 and WF 094 must be a geographical designation but the exact meaning is disputed. Current opinions on the interpretation of EN.GI.KI are divided between taking the term as referring to the whole of Sumer and taking it as some unknown but smaller place in southern Babylonia. The sequence of the signs used for the writing of the term in these texts was EN.GI.KI instead of KI.EN.GI, suggesting that perhaps a quite different location Engiki than ki-en-gi was meant.633 Visicato had actually identified the EN.GI.KI in the Fāra sources with a small centre between A.Aki and ÉREN.Aki (TSS 758).634 In the text IAS 247 written in the UD.GAL.NUN orthography but somehow readable, however, we find the PA.NUN of Kiš, the PA.NUN of Adab, the GAL of Aratta, written LAM.KUR.RU, and the GAL of ki-en-gi were attested apparently in juxtaposition. It may be inferred from IAS 247 that, in partial agreement with Visicato, ki-en-gi originally referred to a more specific location or “region” within but smaller than Sumer as we know of in later times, since here Adab seemed to be considered not a part of but a counterpart of ki-en-gi (IAS 247). Jacobsen had early on understood the designation EN.GI.KI in WF 092 and WF 094 as referring to the Sumer we know.635 The problem of his interpretation is that, even if EN.GI.KI as a toponym was the designation
633 WF 142 and WF 758 wrote the term with the same form EN.GI.KI according to Edzard, Farber et al. 1977, p. 86. The attestations of ki-en-gi collected in Ibid., pp. 86–88, are not complete. Note énsi-GAR (PA.GAR.TE.SI) EN.GI.KI in TSS 627, also with the form EN.GI.KI. Wilcke 1974 opted to regard EN.GI in the ki-en-gi instead of the whole written form as the part corresponding to the Akkadian Šumerim. 634 Pomponio, Visicato and Alberti 1994, p. 11 and n. 11. 635 Cf. Jacobsen 1970, p. 148, with both WF 092 and WF 094 quoted in p. 384, n. 70. Steible and Yildiz 1993, p. 25, n. 31, quoted OB sources in support.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 233
of Sumer, the question whether the geographical scope of the region known as Sumer corresponded to that of the region EN.GI.KI referred to in the ED IIIa sources is still unanswered. The border of ki-en-gi as against ki-uri was not clearly defined at least before the Sargonic Period (§4.3). The Akkadian form of ki-en-gi, read Šu-me-rí-im, is known the earliest in Rimuš’ inscriptions (*E2.1.2.4). The two terms of ki-en-gi and ki-uri were juxtaposed in Caption 2 of *E2.1.4.5 of Narām-Sîn, where the border point was not specified and the context was that of military administration rather than geographical survey (§4.3). In the Enḫeduanna temple hymn ki-uri was in juxtaposition with ki-en-gi and the border between ki-uri and ki-en-gi was given as Nippur, but the hymn probably dates to as late as the Ur III Period (§4.3). In my opinion, reading EN.GI.KI in WF 092 and WF 094 as ki-en-gi posits no linguistic question but in all likelihood, historically EN.GI.KI at this time still referred to some region smaller than the later Sumer. The Sumerian form of the name ki-en-gi was perhaps derived from *ki.eme. gi(r), “the region [where] the Sumerian language [is spoken]”, which is not a concrete geographical definition.636 The region ki-en-gi may have been from the earliest time culturally and politically very significant for all the Sumerian cities and this was perhaps the reason why Sumer could be referred to as ki-en-gi when Adabki became a part. The inclusion of Adabki in ki-en-gi would have been a consequence to the historical situation witnessed by the texts WF 092 and WF 094 above, though there lacks evidence to determine when exactly this inclusion took place. It is worth mentioning here, that in Luzag 1 in which ki-en-gi certainly referred to a region encompassing at least Uruk, Ur, Larsa, Umma, Zabala, and KI.AN, the city of Adab was still absent. Instead of Jacobsen’s “Kengir League” theory also drawing heavily on the Fāra sources, Visicato recently hypothesized a Hexapolis based on the same corpus.637 In Visicato’s theory, the Hexapolis was the specific
636 Postgate 1994b, p. 4. 637 Pomponio, Visicato et al. 1994, pp. 11–19. Note Jacobsen 1970, p. 148, already considered the Fāra corpus as some kind of “palace archive”.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
234
inter-city organization incorporating the cities in WF 092 and WF 094, namely Uruk, Adab, Nippur, Lagaš, Šuruppak, and Umma but centred in Šuruppak. In addition to the activities recorded in texts such as WF 092 and WF 094 in relation to ki-en-gi, the member cities of the Hexapolis were also attested acting collectively in connection to Kiški and UNKENki. UNKENki in the Fāra corpus may refer to a district composed of territories of Adab, Umma and Lagaš but was put under the control of Lagaš as an installation of defence against Elam (TSS 242).638 The sources do not tell if UNKENki was so named because the district was “the place of assembly” where the cities of the Hexapolis met for common interests or because it was jointly owned by Adab, Umma and Lagaš. The activities the member cities collectively undertook in relation to ki-en-gi, however, show some differences from that in the contexts of Kiški and UNKENki. In the contexts of ki-en-gi, the above texts recorded soldiers, guruš, being regularly stationed there, while in relation to Kiški and UNKENki the texts mostly record goods or lands but rarely people.639 The differences suggest that ki-en-gi may have a more special position as regarding the Hexapolis if compared to Kiški and also UNKENki. It needs to be recalled that the high rank of Enlil expressed in the offering lists from Fāra was at a time when the Hexapolis seemed to undertake regular military activities at ki-en-gi (§2.1.2). Combining all the evidence, I hypothesize that Enlil in some way was already associated with the region named ki-en-gi much before the latter came to refer to the whole of Sumer as we know, and that the central significance of the original region ki-en-gi to the Sumerians may at least partially explain the role of Enlil in Sumerian political ideologies. Visicato saw the énsi-GAR-gal of Šuruppak as perhaps established by an earlier lugal-Kiški, which Šuruppak kept as the title of its highest authority in remembrance of the latter connection, therefore the Fāra archive recorded only an énsi-GAR-gal but no lugal. Since lugal-Kiški had been the overlord of Šuruppak, thus explains Visicato, the Hexapolis had
638 Pomponio, Visicato et al. 1994, pp. 12–13. 639 Pomponio, Visicato et al. 1994, p. 16.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 235
good reason to act collectively in relation to the city of Kiški.640 Visicato’s interpretation of énsi-GAR-gal has somehow to be taken as hypothetical in the absence of immediate evidence, but if the highest authority of Šuruppak was truly an installation by an earlier lugal-Kiški, the absence of lugal in the Šuruppak archives would provide evidence that the previous lugal-Kiški, however it was actually from the city of Kiški, had left real impacts in the cities once under his jurisdiction.641 In this case, the Hexapolis would therefore be a transitional form between the kind of political communities formulated in the “Politics of Praise” ideology as we name it above, and real territorial states the kings of which would regularly establish local rulers for cities under their reigns and hold responsibilities for administrations on the local level. That the Hexapolis did not have its own lugal, therefore, would become both the evidence of its continuity with the past and of the possibility for the future when a new concept of nam-lugal suitable for a real territorial state was going to be defined. The distribution of the personal and geographical names containing Enlil shows that the deity was deeply rooted in the name-giving practice of the region of the Hexapolis (§2.1, §2.2). The region named ki-en-gi that was closely connected with Enlil must also be in the same area that was under the control of the Hexapolis. In fact, the ideology combining lugal with Enlil in the way of praise was perhaps primarily formulated in the same region, although it seems the system of the “Politics of Praise” was taken advantage of by cities like Kiš at a very early time (§3.1.1.1). If powers competing with the Hexapolis were to become real territorial states, they would not only have to re-define lugal but also have to re-define Enlil, only if the Hexapolis region, in which Enlil was a key political figure, continues to be the major field of political struggle.
640 Cf. Pomponio, Visicato et al. 1994, pp. 15–19. 641 Cf. Westenholz 2002, p. 38: “It is not explained why the title lugal kiši was abandoned by the southern rulers in favour of lugal kalama in the century before Sargon of Akkade.”



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
236
5.4 dEn-líl(É) in the Pre-Sargonic Period
The collective activities involving the cities of Uruk, Adab, Nippur, Lagaš, Šuruppak, and Umma, known from the archives of Šuruppak, must have helped perpetuate the close association of nam-lugal with dEn-líl(É) in the subsequent Pre-Sargonic Period. The difficulty historians have in elucidating the history of southern Mesopotamia during the transition from the Early Dynastic Period to the Sargonic Period must be due to the complexity of the process of the reshuffling of regional powers. The regional power that began the process after the Hexapolis was likely Ur from the south, excluded from the Hexapolis when the Royal Cemetery evidenced the growth of royal power in the city.642 The disbanding of the Hexapolis may be the achievement of Meskalamdu or Mesanepada in coalition with Mari.643
5.4.1 dEn-líl(É) and nam-lugal-kalam-ma
The nam-lugal closely associated with Enlil in the Fāra Period came to be redefined as nam-lugal-kalam-ma, “kingship of the Land”, first by Lugalzagesi, but almost at once adopted by the rising Sargonids. In the Fāra personal names An was not evidently a parallel element to Enlil and lugal, while in the Pre-Sargonic Period, the deity An began to feature in personal names also as a close parallel to both Enlil and lugal (§3.1.1.1). In royal inscriptions of the southern rulers both An and Enlil were known as lugal-kur-kur-(ra) in dedicatory inscriptions though in Lagaš the dedicatory designation of Enlil was dEn-líl é-ad-da. It seems that this Lagašite dedicatory designation of Enlil resulted from a localization of
642 Steible and Yildiz 1993, pp. 18–19, p. 26, noted that Ur, Eridu, and Larsa are absent in the Fāra texts relating inter-city activities. Note Enlil was not attested as a name element in the Ur texts in Burrows 1935 dated from the Early Dynastic I to the Sargonic Period. See also Cohen 2005 on the tombs of Ur and Marchesi 2004 on the textual findings from the Royal Cemetery. 643 Cf. Pomponio, Visicato et al. 1994, p. 20.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 237
Enlil in Lagaš which was once a participant city in the Hexapolis, and that the southern dedicatory terminology was perhaps borrowed from that of An, the ancient “king of Uruk”. In the inscriptions of Lugalzagesi, who was perhaps the first lugalkalam-ma, the kingship of Enlil seemed to have integrated the kingship of An via a genealogical link by which Enlil was the beloved son of An (§3.2.4.1). The concept of nam-lugal-kalam-ma, “kingship of the Land”, furthermore, seems integrating not only the kingship of An as in the south and the kingship of Enlil as in the Hexapolis tradition, since Lugalzagesi adopted also the title of Ikūn-Šamaš and Isqī-Mari of Mari, namely énsi-gal-dEn-líl. The Mari kings would have also contributed to the concept of kingship according to which Enlil became the patron of kalam in a way not unlike that of Ningirsu to Lagaš (§3.2.3). The geographical coverage of kalam, “the land”, in the inscriptions of Lugalzagesi and Sargon is unclear but it is certain that it went far beyond ki-en-gi, which seemed to have become a political concept then including also the deeper south. The widening of the kingship of Enlil to be that of the kalam may also have re-defined sanga-dEn-líl in Nippur, which by the way, was perhaps newly established in the process, since now interregional rulers entitled to nominate the holders of the office would be the same kings of the Land (§3.2.2). The rise of Ninurta to the patron of Nippur perhaps also happened in this time, when Enlil became effectively the patron of the much broader kalam, thus leaving an empty place to be filled in Nippur. It is too difficult to argue that Ninurta was the patron deity of the city before the ED IIIb Period in the absence of solid evidence. In the Barton Cylinder, Ninurta obtained his sonship to Enlil through his sonship to Ninḫursag but in the Fāra Period god lists Ninurta was evidently insignificant. The institution èš-Nibruki in the Barton Cylinder must be the same as èš-dEn-líl, the oikos of Enlil in Nippur, as no evidence shows Ninurta had a separate èš already in Pre-Sargonic times. The èš-dNin-urta as seen in the Sargonic officical title sanga èš-dNin-urta was probably enhanced or established anew after Ninurta became the patron at the rise of the Sargonids.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
238
The evidence inevitably leads to the conclusion that Ninurta being the patron of Nippur was relatively recent though the deity may have been known much earlier (§4.2.2.1). If the attestation of Ninḫursag in the Barton Cylinder rather than any other goddesses is also politically significant, as with the attestations of Ninḫursag in the Stele of the Vultures and in the Sargonic Bāseṭkī Statue, the rise of Ninurta to be a politically important deity and thereafter the patron of Nippur, would coincide with the renewal of the ancient link between Nippur and Adab (§3.3.2). The mythic plot in the Barton Cylinder apparently switched its focus from the pattern of sexual partnership, which was the traditional link between the god of Nippur and the goddess of Adab, to that of sonship between Ninurta and Enlil, which however finds its counterpart in the genealogical link between Enlil and An with real political implications in the contemporary world. The development of political ideology in the Pre-Sargonic Period in the complex process of political reshuffling must be also a systematic shift rather than only one of details. More importantly, the concept of nam-lugal-kalam-ma, “kingship of the Land”, has now to be seen as in a different paradigm from that of nam-lugal associated with Enlil in the Fāra Period. The political merge of regional powers before the formation of the Akkadian kingdom resulted in the merge of different ideologies, such as that of Ur from the deeper south and Mari upstream on the Euphrates, when the traditional idea of divine sanction perhaps once shared by all of these cities changed fundamentally. The idea of divine sanction was perhaps still kept in the memories of the Pre-Sargonic rulers, typically Lugalzagesi, but this time when these originally local rulers were struggling for supra-regional control, divine sanctions became more and more parts of their weaponry rather than the origin of their legitimacy. It is significant to note in this context that both the rise of Ninurta relative to Enlil, and the obtaining of the title lugal-kur-kur-(ra) by Enlil from An, were made through their sonship to Enlil and to An respectively, an easy but human way for these rulers to explain things to their conquered subjects.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 239
5.5 dEn-líl(É) in the Sargonic Period
Sargon somehow has to be considered as an Early Dynastic ruler who took away from Lugalzagesi not only his political control over the land but also the nam-lugal-kalam-ma political ideology Lugalzagesi formulated in a remarkably creative way. The reason Lugalzagesi was led to the Gate of Enlil in Nippur does not seem to be that Sargon did not buy the defeated ruler’s ideological innovation, but was more likely the opposite. Sargon could be using Lugalzagesi’s own weapons against him, taking advantage of the fact that ideological innovations are bound to be attacked by the same ideas the innovations were based on. The biggest problem concerning Sargon at the end of the Early Dynastic Period is that the early career of Sargon remains too much unclear. Enlil’s title, ŚA.DÚ ì-li ra-bí-um, suggests that the Sargonids did bring in something of their own and dUTU as the only deity in the curse of *E2.1.1.1 may be interpreted as an early version of their “interpretation” of Enlil, drawing on traditions that were shared in their homeland (§4.2.4-5). The offering lists from Nippur suggest that Sargon may have played a role in renewing the cultic link between Nippur and Adab, which would at least do favour to the rise of the Sargonids (§3.1.2). The facts that Sargon was offering in Nippur and that he took over the ideological construction by Lugalzagesi, however, suggest that the king had also to make his own power acceptable to other cities. It is only possible to infer from scattered sources that king Sargon was also caught in a dilemma not unlike that of Lugalzagesi, for the same reason that the real politics of the time needed a systematically reconfigured political ideology based on available traditions but different from any of them. The systematically reconfigured political ideology that suits the new political practice of the time, that of the territorial state, is only achieved by Narām-Sîn, the first Mesopotamian ruler that was “deified” in his life time. The Bāseṭkī Statue claimed that the “deification” of Narām-Sîn was acknowledged by the traditional ruler gods, though in human terms the deification may be the result of the military might of the king. The deification of Narām-Sîn seemed on the surface not having



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
240
immediate consequences for the ideology concerning Enlil, since instead of driving him away Narām-Sîn called him “father”. But the difference is that, this LUGAL ki-ib-ra-tim ar-ba-im, “king of the four quarters”, is now by himself the “ruler god”, which is necessary when the powers of the other “ruler gods” were to be replaced (§4.2.3.1). The (re)-organization of the pantheon to include regions beyond the Land is in fact only possible with Narām-Sîn’s deification (§4.2.6). The “Aufstieg von Ištar” and the retention of the GIDRU, “sceptre”, by Enlil, have both to be seen in the context that the ideological changes resulted in the formation of a new system in which deities had to find their own positions, acceptable in the old way but essentially all different. The change is a change of the system rather than the characteristics of particular deities (§4.2.5.2).
5.5.1 The Sargonic Administration of dEn-líl(É)
It is unlikely that the administration of the temple of Enlil in Nippur was already separated from the city administration before the rise of the Sargonids, when the office of sanga-dEn-líl was established by some unknown ruler not long before (§3.2.2, §3.2.3.1). In the time of Narām-Sîn and Šarkališarri, the office of sanga-dEn-líl continued to exist beside the énsi of the city, but as in the case of Lugalnizu, the offices sanga-dEn-líl and énsi-EN.LÍLki can be combined in one person. The two offices were probably not within one administrative subsystem. The office of sangadEn-líl was interregional and was directly under the king in the time of Narām-Sîn and Šarkališarri, not unlike the office of NIN.DINGIR dEn-líl, which was very likely an innovation by Narām-Sîn creatively continuing an earlier local tradition (§4.2.2). The office sanga èš-dNin-urta was established perhaps right at the rise of the Sargonids and was put in the charge of the énsi from the beginning (§4.2.2.1.1). In Adab the double identities of the nu-èš people show that the “priestly” circle of Enlil was also heavily involved in civil administration at the time (§4.1.3.2). The attestations of the terms zà-mu dEn-líl from Nippur and ezen dBára-dEn-líl from Adab, both unattested in Ur III cultic calendars known in sources found from the sites, suggest that the Sargonic administration



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 241
had its own means in appealing to Enlil (§4.1.1.3.2, §4.1.3.3). However, the possible connection between ezen dBára-dEn-líl and the Pre-Sargonic Bára-dEn-líl suggests that the Sargonic cultic administration did not completely break away from the earlier local traditions of the Sumerian cities (§4.1.3.3). The offering lists from Sargonic Girsu suggest also that the Sargonic administration did not alter very much the local cults established perhaps by Pre-Sargonic rulers (§4.1.5.2). In sum, the Sargonic kings seemed to be concerned more with the highest level in the ideological re-formulation of their power domain while otherwise they left most of the local practices on the detailed level as they traditionally were. If more attention was paid to Nippur where our Enlil was housed, and to the other most important Sumerian cultic centers such as Ur, it was for the same reason that the Sargonic kings were concerned more with the highest level of the divine world which they had to domesticate first.
5.5.2 The É-kur Project and dEn-líl(É)
The architectural change made by Narām-Sîn of the temple of Enlil has been interpreted as his “attack” on the Ekur described in the cursing of Agade (OB version, ECTSL No. 2.1.5):644
129itima é ud nu-zu-ba ùg-e igi i-ni-in-bar 130dub-šen kug dingir-re-e-ne-ke4 Uriki igi i-ni-in-bar
“The people could see the bedchamber, its room which knows no daylight. The Akkadians could look into the holy treasure chest of the gods.”645
644 Liverani 1993, pp. 56–59, suggested that Narām-Sîn “modified the traditional aspect of the Ekur as a dark and inaccessible shrine, providing the cella with a wider accessibility”, thus violating the Sumerian tradition of temple building, in which the cool inside is not to be seen. 645 See Black, Cunningham et al. online.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
242
It may be suggested, however, that according to the inscriptions of Narām-Sîn, when the rebellious rulers, Iphur-Kiši of Kiš, Amar-girid of Uruk and another anonymous king of unknown origin, were brought before Enlil in Nippur, the Ekur project had already begun.646 In the inscriptions of Sargon, Lugalzagesi was brought to the Gate of Enlil , ká-dEn-líl-lá e-de6/ana KÁ dEn-líl u-ru-úś (*E2.1.1.1, *E2.1.1.2), while the wording for Narām-Sîn bringing the defeated rebellion rulers also in neck stocks changed to be maḫ-rí-íś dEn-líl a-bí-śu, “before Enlil his father” (*E2.1.4.8-9, *E2.1.4.13, *E2.1.4.28). The phrase maḫ-rí-íś dEn-líl is attested in curse formulae of Sargon, maḫ-rí-íś dEn-líl e DU, to be interpreted as “may he not walk before Enlil” (*E2.1.1.2, *E2.1.1.6, *E2.1.1.7). The Akkadian term maḫriš in the inscriptions of Narām-Sîn had a nuance of “in the presence of” while ana KÁ in the inscriptions of Sargon must put the stress on the KÁ, bābum, probably some brick structure (§2.3.4).647 The contemporary sources suggest that the cursing of Agade may have had some twisted historical basis, since Narām-Sîn did seem to have made important changes to the design of the earlier “sacred area of Nippur” which originally had brick-built gateways. The fact that the clear equation of É-kur and the temple of Enlil, é-dEn-líl, is attested the earliest in an inscription of Šarkališarri, nevertheless, leaves much space for speculation on the significance of the Ekur project (E2.1.5.1).648 In the last chapter, I have suggested that based on textual sources the temple of Ekur seems to be a much later structure to appear in the city of Nippur, at least substantially later than Eannatum (§4.2.4). It may be ventured here that the temple name É-kur was even actually coined for the new building by the Sargonic kings, having not much to do with the earlier “sacred area of Nippur” at all. It might be an ancient name of
646 Frayne 1991, p. 385, considered Lugal-nì-zu ensí of Nippur as the third ruler. But Westenholz 1999, pp. 53–54, proposed Lugal-an-né of Ur instead. 647 Narām-Sîn defeated Ipḫur-Kiš at KÁ dNin-kár (*E2.1.4.6). 648 Kienast and Sommerfeld 1994, p. 113, restored temen-É-kur before é-dEn-lílka for OSP 2 040, i.e., E2.1.4.14 in Frayne 1993, p. 118. Note, Westenholz 1987, p. 55, n. 60, regarded the broken part difficult to accommodate the name of É-kur.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 243
significance as we know from the ED IIIa sources, but Narām-Sîn and/or Šarkališarri took it for a new and somehow different use for the name of the newly built temple of Enlil.649 The more important question here is of course the relationship of the (re)building of the temple of Enlil to the fall of Akkad. The building project under Narām-Sîn had been considered as the cause of the Great Rebellion, in Westenholz’ chronology right before the fall of Akkad, so as to substantiate the historicity of the cursing of Agade.650 The fact that Narām-Sîn could have altered the architectural design of the residence of Enlil in his building project to be completed by his son may provide some historical background for the description of the “attack” by the cursing of Akkade, but to my knowledge at least, there is not at all evidence that the rebel rulers took the building of Ekur as their reason to rebel.
5.6 The History of Enlil and the Metamorphosis of Enlil
The history of a human being may take the form of a biography from birth to death and the history of an object such as a statue may be concerned with the making, the function and the recycling of the object. But no such forms of history are possible for Enlil. Enlil is not a human being and not an object since he did not even have a statue known to us from early Mesopotamia, though statues of deities did exist there. In fact, Enlil does not even have an “identity” that can be materialized, except that a name refers to it in imagination. And unfortunately the name of Enlil also changed its written forms through time. The most apparent advantage of “The History of Deities” approach to Enlil is that, it allows the Prototype to have no “identity”. I defined in the Introduction that “The History of Deities” is the diachronic study of the relations between the Prototype and the Artists
649 Steinkeller 1993b, p. 142: “Westenholz is probably right that during the reign of Naram-Sin only the substructures and possibly some of the pavements of the Ekur were executed; the actual temple was completed by Šar-kali-šarri.” 650 Westenholz 1987, pp. 24–27.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
244
and between the Prototype and the Recipients, but only those knowable from Indices, the available primary sources. The history of a given deity pays attention to but is not primarily concerned with the theological views the Artists and Recipients could have had of any Prototype. It is primarily concerned with the relations between the humans and the Prototype, and between the Artists and Recipients (§0.3.3). “The History of Deities” is a bottom-up approach and the result of such a study will be primarily a collection of the relations the Indices could have evidenced. The fabrication of these relations into a narrative by the student, warned of the weighty harms of “frameworks” but with limited holistic insights into and insufficient knowledge of the world in which the Prototype is furthermore unknown even as being one and the same, will inevitably be very difficult. Such a fabrication as the end product of “The History of Deities” approach when applied to Enlil may better be taken as a Metamorphosis of Enlil rather than a History of Enlil, so as to acknowledge first of all the risky nature of the attempt delineating the changes of Enlil in the course of time.651 In order that the Metamorphosis of Enlil becomes the History of Enlil, the history of the world of Enlil has to be sufficiently clear on the most detailed level and the identity of the deity called Enlil has to be granted as one and the same. The former is impossible at the present and the latter is most likely untrue, because the writings from different times that are read all as the name of Enlil were likely Indices to quite different Prototypes. The use of the word “Metamorphosis” not only admits that the account below is not really a history confidently written but also tells that Enlil had to be Indexed to different Prototypes. In the Uruk III Period NIBRU the city was written as EN.KID when the divinity of ENLIL is not clearly evidenced. In this sense the concept of Enlil as a divine agent perhaps did not even exist in the Late Uruk Period. ENa+KIDa or ENx+KIDx was most likely the name of the site Niffar and was close to /nibru/ in pronunciation. Only later it obtained the reading /ellil/ or /illil/ when the form was taken to write a divine name.
651 The use of the word is inspired by Ovid’s “Metamorphosis”. See Melville 1986.



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL 245
The city we may refer to as NIBRU seemed to be imposed on by a divinitiy from the outside soon after the end of the Late Uruk Period and for this reason EN.KID obtained the new reading. The divinity was written as AN.EN.KID but read /ellil/ or /illil/ serving as the patron of Nippur. AN.EN.KID of Nippur was replaced by an AN.EN.É perhaps again from outside of Nippur before the Fāra Period. I abstain from stating the ethnic origin of the name Enlil or the theologies of Enlil first of all because early Mesopotamia was always multi-ethnic and there must have been Semitic elements in Enlil (§4.2.4). The name dEn-líl(É), more precisely speaking, is that which indexed to the Prototype that was considered as the most important divine figure in the “Politics of Praise” in the Fāra Period as practised by Me-salim. In the “Politics of Praise” developed in central Babylonia, dEn-líl(É) was associated with the concept of nam-lugal, “kingship”, that in the real world meant the human lugals’ authority in providing arbitration in inter-city affairs. In the subsequent Pre-Sargonic Period, the collapse of the Hexapolis perhaps began the process of the political reshuffling of regional powers across Babylonia, some of which brought in their only interpretation of dEn-líl(É) if the deity was known already in their tradition. The fusing of regional cultural traditions concerning Enlil became realized in the deity being the patron of nam-lugal-kalam-ma, “kingship of the Land”, at the rise of the Sargonids. The idea of Enlil as the patron deity of nam-lugal-kalam-ma was perhaps the innovation of Lugalzagesi who was defeated by Sargon. It seems the Sargonids had also their own interpretation of Enlil the patron of nam-lugal-kalam-ma. But when the Sargonids succeeded in their military conquests even beyond the Land, kalam, so as to create a large territorial state, the Babylonian nam-lugal-kalam-ma became insufficient and finally king Narām-Sîn was deified alive to accommodate the new situation. The deified king as the highest authority in his kingdom domesticcated Enlil by his cultic administration. The characteristics of Enlil as the one who grants ruling power remained in theory and he was still taken



PART V: THE METAMORPHOSIS OF ENLIL
246
care of in a luxurious style but importantly the ideological system is no longer evolving around him and the focus of royal power is now the deified human king. The “misdeed” of Narām-Sîn described in the cursing of Agade being the “attack” of the king on the temple of Enlil perhaps had twisted historical background but is hardly interpretable as the cause of the fall of Akkad. The reason behind the Ekur project in which Narām-Sîn changed the traditional architectural design of the “sacred area” of Enlil in Nippur may also be understood in the light of the king’s systematic management of cults beginning from the highest administrative level.
